قوله : ( لكل شيء حقيقة هو بها هو ( الظاهر أن المراد بالشيء ما هو أعلم من الموجود ولو مجازاً، إذ الماهية تعم الموجود والمعدوم، وهي المرادة بالحقيقة ها هنا، ويمكن أن يراد به معناه الحقيقي ، أعني الموجود بناء على ما اشتهر من أن الحقيقة تختص بالموجود، ثم قوله : هو بها أن في موقع التعريف للحقيقة، والظاهر على ما في شرح المقاصد أن التفسير المذكور مبني على الماهية ليست مجعولة بجعل الجاعل كما هو رأي جمهور الفلاسفة والمعتزلة، فلا يصدق التعريف على العلة الفاعلية وقد يمنع البناء على ما ذكر ؛ لأن القائلين بأن الماهية مجعولة يفسرونها بهذا التفسير أيضاً. ويدفع الاعتراض بالعلة الفاعلية بأن الشيءعبارة عن الأمر الخارجي، والباء في بها متعلقة بالاتحاد المستفاد من هو هو، فإن هو هو كانه الاتحاد، علم في ولذا لم يقل : ما به الشيء هو مع أنه أخصر، وتلخيصه : أن الماهية عبارة عن الصور العقلية، وهي من حيث ذاتها نفس الأمر الخارجي، فإنه لو اقترنت الصورة العقلية بالوجود الخارجي وما يتبعه كان الحاصل عين الأمر الخارجي، وإذا جرد الموجود الخارجي عن العوارض كان الباقي فيه تلك الصور العقلية، فمعنى التعريف ما به يتحد الأمر الخارجي في الوجود ولا يخفى عليك ما فيه من التعسف.
His saying: 'In distinguishing quiddity from what is other than it' i.e., an explanation that that which is true of quiddity is something separate from every concept that is true of it as being other than it, but there is no consideration in this judgment under the aspect that it is other than it, so that the judgment would be vain, but rather by its essence, and he only expressed it by 'other than it' due to the multiplicity of those concepts. So the intent is, for example, that the quiddity of man is other than 'laughing', 'writing', 'speaking', and other things. There is no doubt that this judgment needs explanation. And the gist of the explanation is that considering that which is true of quiddity insofar as it is that by which the thing is what it is makes the mentioned judgment self-evident, and hence otherness follows from interpreting reality as 'by which it is what it is' due to its unity.
His saying: 'for every thing' i.e., that which can be known and informed about.
His saying: 'The second observatory on quiddity, and mā'iyya is its synonym, though the aspect of naming differs. So māhīyya is attributed to 'mā huwa', and it is applied to reality in consideration of its suitability to answer the question 'what is it?', just as reality is applied in consideration that the thing's realization is by it. And mā'iyya is attributed to 'mā' and is applied to its suitability to answer the question 'what is it?'.
An explanation of the concept of the reality of a thing. The particular reality is called identity (huwiyya) and identity is sometimes used to mean external existence. The universal reality is called quiddity (māhīyya). Then reality in itself is either compared to things disparate from it, then there is no confusion, because disparate things are negated in the sense that they are not the quiddity itself nor included in it nor accidental to it. Or it is compared to ... [corruption]
His saying: 'Reality is apparently quiddity, but he put the word reality in its place to indicate their unity, therefore the commentator did not discuss their unity.'
His saying: 'By which it is what it is' requires considering a distinction between subject and predicate for predication to be valid. So the first refers to the essence of the thing, and the second refers to what is necessary for it, namely its being actualized in itself such that by it [the thing is what it is], and the causality derived from the bā' suffices for conceptual distinction. The objection from the efficient cause is not applicable, for the efficient cause actualizes the existence of the thing, not the thing itself. This is the meaning of their saying: the efficient cause makes the thing existent, not that thing. And this interpretation includes both universal and particular, unlike that by which one answers the question 'what is it' according to the terminology of logic, for it is specific to the universal. And between the two meanings there is generality and particularity in some respects. His saying: 'interpretation etc.' means that the description is revealing, not restrictive.
His saying: 'Then reality in itself' i.e., without observing anything with it, even this aspect, as if it were said: that which is true of reality without observing anything with it.
His saying: 'disparate etc.' i.e., separate, indicated by his saying 'nor accidental'. His saying: 'that' i.e., the thing compared has no confusion with any of those things, because of its distinctness from them in all respects, so the author did not discuss its explanation.
His saying: 'Every thing has a reality by which it is what it is' — apparently what is meant by 'thing' is more general than existent, even metaphorically, since quiddity includes existent and non-existent, and that is what is meant by 'reality' here. And it is possible that what is meant is its literal meaning, namely existent, based on the well-known view that reality is specific to existent. Then his saying: 'by which it is' that it is in the position of definition of reality. And apparently according to Sharh al-Maqāsid, the mentioned interpretation is based on the view that quiddity is not made by the making of a maker, as is the opinion of the majority of philosophers and the Mu'tazila, so the definition does not apply to the efficient cause. But it may be objected that the construction is based on what was mentioned, because those who say quiddity is made also interpret it with this interpretation. And the objection regarding the efficient cause is repelled by [the fact] that 'thing' means the external affair, and the bā' in 'bihā' is related to the unity derived from 'huwa huwa'. So if 'huwa huwa' were like unity, it would be known, and therefore he did not say 'what by which the thing is' even though it is shorter. Summary: quiddity consists of mental forms, and in their essence they are the same as the external affair. For if the mental form were combined with external existence and its concomitants, the result would be the very external affair. And if the external existent is stripped of accidents, what remains in it are those mental forms. So the meaning of the definition is 'that by which the external affair is united in existence'. And it is not hidden from you what is in it of artificiality.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is the first objective of the second observatory: distinguishing quiddity from other concepts. It begins by explaining the lemma 'every thing has a reality by which it is what it is'. The author clarifies that quiddity is distinct from all attributes and concepts that are not identical to it, such as 'laughing' or 'writing' for man. The key point is that when we consider quiddity as that by which a thing is what it is, the distinction becomes self-evident. The passage then defines 'thing' as anything knowable. It distinguishes between 'māhīyya' and 'mā'iyya' and explains the terminology. It then discusses reality (ḥaqīqa) and its types: particular reality called identity (huwiyya) and universal reality called quiddity (māhīyya). It notes that reality in itself can be compared to disparate things (no confusion) or to accidents (which require further analysis). The commentary then examines the expression 'by which it is what it is', explaining that it requires a conceptual distinction between subject and predicate, and addresses the objection from the efficient cause: the efficient cause makes the thing existent, not the thing itself. It notes that this definition applies to both universal and particular, unlike the logical definition. The passage concludes with a discussion of whether quiddity is made (maj'ūla) and a summary of the definition according to the view that quiddity is identical to mental forms which are the external affair when stripped of accidents.

Key Terms
الماهية (al-māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that quiddity includes its attributes as part of its essence.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that quiddity is other than attributes like laughing and writing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes the judgment that quiddity is distinct from its attributes self-evident?
A) Considering quiddity as that by which the thing is what it is.
B) Considering quiddity as a collection of attributes.
C) Considering quiddity as an external object.
D) Considering quiddity as a mental concept.

Key Takeaway
Quiddity is essentially distinct from its attributes, and this distinction becomes self-evident when we view quiddity as that by which a thing is what it is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often define yourself by your attributes—your job, your skills, your relationships. But what is your quiddity? The thing that makes you you regardless of all those attributes? Have you ever considered that? How would your life change if you focused on your essence rather than your accidents?
?
Sanusi says the distinction becomes self-evident when we consider quiddity as that by which a thing is what it is. When you strip away everything other than your essence, what remains? Are you willing to let go of attributes that define you to find your true self?
?
If you truly believe that your quiddity is distinct from your attributes, how should that affect your attachment to your status, your achievements, or your flaws?

Reality: Particular and Universal
Sanusi defines the terms māhīyya, mā'iyya, and ḥaqīqa, and distinguishes particular reality (huwiyya) from universal reality (māhīyya).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the terminology of reality (ḥaqīqa), including particular reality (identity) and universal reality (quiddity), and the distinction between them.

Core Idea
Particular reality is identity; universal reality is quiddity.

Sanusi now delves into the technical terminology of quiddity and its synonyms. He explains that the word 'māhīyya' is derived from 'mā huwa' (what is), while 'mā'iyya' is similar. More importantly, he distinguishes between particular reality, called huwiyya (identity), and universal reality, called māhīyya (quiddity). This lesson clarifies these foundational terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on terminology
Read the Arabic and translation that explain the derivation of māhīyya and mā'iyya.
2
Distinguish between particular and universal
Note that particular reality is called identity (huwiyya) and universal reality is called quiddity (māhīyya).
3
Understand the comparison with disparate things
The passage states that when reality is compared to disparate things, there is no confusion because they are completely separate.
This sets up the next lesson on comparison with accidents.

قوله : ( فى تميز الماهية عما عداها ) أي بيان أن ما يصدق عليه الماهية أمر وراء كل مفهوم يصدق عليه أنه ما عداها، لكن لا ملاحظة في هذا الحكم بعنوان أنه ما عداها، حتى يكون الحكم لغواً بل ذاته، وإنما عبر عنه بما عداها لكثرة تلك المفهومات فالمقصود مثلاً أن ماهية الإنسان غير الضاحك والكاتب والناطق وغير ذلك، ولا شك أن هذا الحكم محتاج إلى البيان الإنسان فيما صدقت عليه وحاصل البيان : أن ملاحظة ما صدق عليه الماهية من حيث إنه ما به الشيء هو هو يجعل الحكم المذكور بديهياً، ولذا ترتب المغايرة على تفسير الحقيقة بما هو هو . لاتحادها
قوله : ( لكل شيء ) أي ما يصح أن يعلم ويخبر عنه .
قوله : (المرصد الثاني في (الماهية ويرادفها المائية، وإن اختلف وجه التسمية، فالماهية منسوبة إلى ما هو، ويطلق على الحقيقة باعتبار صلوحها للجواب عن السؤال بما هو كما يطلق عليها الحقيقة باعتبار أن تحقق الشيء بها. والمائية منسوبة إلى ما ويطلق عليها صلوحها للجواب عن السؤال بما .
تفسير لمفهوم حقيقة الشيء، والحقيقة الجزئية تسمى هوية وقد تستعمل الهوية بمعنى الوجود الخارجي، والحقيقة الكلية تسمى ماهية ، ثم الحقيقة من حيث هي : إما أن تقاس إلى أمور مباينة إياها فذلك لا التباس فيه ؛ لأن الأمور المباينة لها مسلوبة بمعنى أنه ليست نفس الماهية ولا داخلة فيها ولا عارضة لها. وإما أن تقاس إلى عن،
قوله : (حقيقة الظاهر ماهية إلا أنه أقام لفظ الحقيقة مقامها تنبيهاً على اتحادهما، ولذا لم يتعرض الشارح لبيان اتحادهما .
قوله : (هو بها هو لا بد من اعتبار التغاير بين الموضوع والمحمول ليصح الحمل، فالمراد الأول ذات الشيء، وبالثاني ما يلزمه وهو كونه متحصلا في نفسه بحيث بهو، والسببية المستفادة من الباء يكفيه التغاير الاعتباري، ولا يتجه النقض بالفاعل إذ الفاعل يتحصل به وجود الشيء لا الشيء نفسه. وهذا معنى ما قالوا: إن الفاعل يجعل الشيء موجوداً لا ذلك الشيء، وهذا التفسير شامل للكلي والجزئي، بخلاف ما به يجاب عن الشيء بما هو على ما هو مصطلح المنطقي، فإنه مختص بالكل، وبين المعنيين عموم وخصوص من وجه . قوله : ( تفسير إلخ ) يعني أن الصفة كاشفة لا مقيدة.
قوله : (ثم الحقيقة من حيث هي ) أي من غير أن يلاحظ معه شيء حتى هذه الحيثية، فكأنه قيل : ما صدق عليه الحقيقة من غير ملاحظة أمر معه .
قوله : ( مباينة إلخ ) أي مفارقة يدل عليه قول : ولا عارضة . قوله : (فذلك) أي المقاس لا التباس فيه بشيء من تلك الأمور ؛ لامتيازه عنها من جميع الوجوه، فلذا لم يتعرض المصنف لبيانه .
قوله : ( لكل شيء حقيقة هو بها هو ( الظاهر أن المراد بالشيء ما هو أعلم من الموجود ولو مجازاً، إذ الماهية تعم الموجود والمعدوم، وهي المرادة بالحقيقة ها هنا، ويمكن أن يراد به معناه الحقيقي ، أعني الموجود بناء على ما اشتهر من أن الحقيقة تختص بالموجود، ثم قوله : هو بها أن في موقع التعريف للحقيقة، والظاهر على ما في شرح المقاصد أن التفسير المذكور مبني على الماهية ليست مجعولة بجعل الجاعل كما هو رأي جمهور الفلاسفة والمعتزلة، فلا يصدق التعريف على العلة الفاعلية وقد يمنع البناء على ما ذكر ؛ لأن القائلين بأن الماهية مجعولة يفسرونها بهذا التفسير أيضاً. ويدفع الاعتراض بالعلة الفاعلية بأن الشيءعبارة عن الأمر الخارجي، والباء في بها متعلقة بالاتحاد المستفاد من هو هو، فإن هو هو كانه الاتحاد، علم في ولذا لم يقل : ما به الشيء هو مع أنه أخصر، وتلخيصه : أن الماهية عبارة عن الصور العقلية، وهي من حيث ذاتها نفس الأمر الخارجي، فإنه لو اقترنت الصورة العقلية بالوجود الخارجي وما يتبعه كان الحاصل عين الأمر الخارجي، وإذا جرد الموجود الخارجي عن العوارض كان الباقي فيه تلك الصور العقلية، فمعنى التعريف ما به يتحد الأمر الخارجي في الوجود ولا يخفى عليك ما فيه من التعسف.
His saying: 'In distinguishing quiddity from what is other than it' i.e., an explanation that that which is true of quiddity is something separate from every concept that is true of it as being other than it, but there is no consideration in this judgment under the aspect that it is other than it, so that the judgment would be vain, but rather by its essence, and he only expressed it by 'other than it' due to the multiplicity of those concepts. So the intent is, for example, that the quiddity of man is other than 'laughing', 'writing', 'speaking', and other things. There is no doubt that this judgment needs explanation. And the gist of the explanation is that considering that which is true of quiddity insofar as it is that by which the thing is what it is makes the mentioned judgment self-evident, and hence otherness follows from interpreting reality as 'by which it is what it is' due to its unity.
His saying: 'for every thing' i.e., that which can be known and informed about.
His saying: 'The second observatory on quiddity, and mā'iyya is its synonym, though the aspect of naming differs. So māhīyya is attributed to 'mā huwa', and it is applied to reality in consideration of its suitability to answer the question 'what is it?', just as reality is applied in consideration that the thing's realization is by it. And mā'iyya is attributed to 'mā' and is applied to its suitability to answer the question 'what is it?'.
An explanation of the concept of the reality of a thing. The particular reality is called identity (huwiyya) and identity is sometimes used to mean external existence. The universal reality is called quiddity (māhīyya). Then reality in itself is either compared to things disparate from it, then there is no confusion, because disparate things are negated in the sense that they are not the quiddity itself nor included in it nor accidental to it. Or it is compared to ... [corruption]
His saying: 'Reality is apparently quiddity, but he put the word reality in its place to indicate their unity, therefore the commentator did not discuss their unity.'
His saying: 'By which it is what it is' requires considering a distinction between subject and predicate for predication to be valid. So the first refers to the essence of the thing, and the second refers to what is necessary for it, namely its being actualized in itself such that by it [the thing is what it is], and the causality derived from the bā' suffices for conceptual distinction. The objection from the efficient cause is not applicable, for the efficient cause actualizes the existence of the thing, not the thing itself. This is the meaning of their saying: the efficient cause makes the thing existent, not that thing. And this interpretation includes both universal and particular, unlike that by which one answers the question 'what is it' according to the terminology of logic, for it is specific to the universal. And between the two meanings there is generality and particularity in some respects. His saying: 'interpretation etc.' means that the description is revealing, not restrictive.
His saying: 'Then reality in itself' i.e., without observing anything with it, even this aspect, as if it were said: that which is true of reality without observing anything with it.
His saying: 'disparate etc.' i.e., separate, indicated by his saying 'nor accidental'. His saying: 'that' i.e., the thing compared has no confusion with any of those things, because of its distinctness from them in all respects, so the author did not discuss its explanation.
His saying: 'Every thing has a reality by which it is what it is' — apparently what is meant by 'thing' is more general than existent, even metaphorically, since quiddity includes existent and non-existent, and that is what is meant by 'reality' here. And it is possible that what is meant is its literal meaning, namely existent, based on the well-known view that reality is specific to existent. Then his saying: 'by which it is' that it is in the position of definition of reality. And apparently according to Sharh al-Maqāsid, the mentioned interpretation is based on the view that quiddity is not made by the making of a maker, as is the opinion of the majority of philosophers and the Mu'tazila, so the definition does not apply to the efficient cause. But it may be objected that the construction is based on what was mentioned, because those who say quiddity is made also interpret it with this interpretation. And the objection regarding the efficient cause is repelled by [the fact] that 'thing' means the external affair, and the bā' in 'bihā' is related to the unity derived from 'huwa huwa'. So if 'huwa huwa' were like unity, it would be known, and therefore he did not say 'what by which the thing is' even though it is shorter. Summary: quiddity consists of mental forms, and in their essence they are the same as the external affair. For if the mental form were combined with external existence and its concomitants, the result would be the very external affair. And if the external existent is stripped of accidents, what remains in it are those mental forms. So the meaning of the definition is 'that by which the external affair is united in existence'. And it is not hidden from you what is in it of artificiality.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is the first objective of the second observatory: distinguishing quiddity from other concepts. It begins by explaining the lemma 'every thing has a reality by which it is what it is'. The author clarifies that quiddity is distinct from all attributes and concepts that are not identical to it, such as 'laughing' or 'writing' for man. The key point is that when we consider quiddity as that by which a thing is what it is, the distinction becomes self-evident. The passage then defines 'thing' as anything knowable. It distinguishes between 'māhīyya' and 'mā'iyya' and explains the terminology. It then discusses reality (ḥaqīqa) and its types: particular reality called identity (huwiyya) and universal reality called quiddity (māhīyya). It notes that reality in itself can be compared to disparate things (no confusion) or to accidents (which require further analysis). The commentary then examines the expression 'by which it is what it is', explaining that it requires a conceptual distinction between subject and predicate, and addresses the objection from the efficient cause: the efficient cause makes the thing existent, not the thing itself. It notes that this definition applies to both universal and particular, unlike the logical definition. The passage concludes with a discussion of whether quiddity is made (maj'ūla) and a summary of the definition according to the view that quiddity is identical to mental forms which are the external affair when stripped of accidents.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqa)
الماهية (al-māhiyya)
الهوية (al-huwiyya)
المائية (al-mā'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'reality' (ḥaqīqa) only applies to external existents.
Correction: The passage shows that reality is applied to both particular and universal, and includes quiddity which covers both existent and non-existent.

Checkpoint
What is the term for particular reality according to this passage?
A) Māhīyya
B) Mā'iyya
C) Huwiyya
D) Ḥaqīqa

Key Takeaway
Particular reality is identity (huwiyya), while universal reality is quiddity (māhīyya).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you reduce yourself to your universal attributes (human, believer) or do you recognize your unique identity (huwiyya) before Allah? How can this distinction deepen your personal accountability?
?
In your daily prayers, do you contemplate the reality (ḥaqīqa) of what you recite, or do you let the words become mere habits? This lesson teaches that reality is what allows a thing to be realized. How can you bring more reality into your worship starting today?
?
The lesson explains that disparate things are negated: they are not the quiddity nor part of it. Are there beliefs, habits, or relationships in your life that are 'disparate' to your faith—things you claim are not your true identity but still tolerate? What would it look like to negate them completely?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
10 min
Interpreting 'By Which It Is What It Is'
Sanusi interprets the definition 'by which it is what it is', addresses the efficient cause objection, and presents the mental forms theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of reality as 'that by which a thing is what it is', the conceptual distinction required, the objection from the efficient cause, and the view that quiddity is identical to mental forms.

Core Idea
The definition 'by which it is what it is' requires a conceptual distinction and includes both universal and particular.

The lemma 'every thing has a reality by which it is what it is' is central to Islamic philosophy. In this passage, Sanusi unpacks its meaning, addressing potential objections and presenting the view that quiddity consists of mental forms identical to external reality when stripped of accidents. This lesson explores these profound ideas.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the lemma
Read the Arabic and translation that discuss the meaning of 'thing' and the definition of reality.
2
Understand the conceptual distinction
Note the requirement of a distinction between subject and predicate for predication.
3
Consider the efficient cause objection
The passage addresses an objection about the efficient cause making the thing existent, not the thing itself. Understand how it is repelled.
4
Explore the mental forms interpretation
The passage concludes with the view that quiddity is the mental form that is identical to the external affair when stripped of accidents.
This is a nuanced metaphysical position.

Commentary
This passage is the first objective of the second observatory: distinguishing quiddity from other concepts. It begins by explaining the lemma 'every thing has a reality by which it is what it is'. The author clarifies that quiddity is distinct from all attributes and concepts that are not identical to it, such as 'laughing' or 'writing' for man. The key point is that when we consider quiddity as that by which a thing is what it is, the distinction becomes self-evident. The passage then defines 'thing' as anything knowable. It distinguishes between 'māhīyya' and 'mā'iyya' and explains the terminology. It then discusses reality (ḥaqīqa) and its types: particular reality called identity (huwiyya) and universal reality called quiddity (māhīyya). It notes that reality in itself can be compared to disparate things (no confusion) or to accidents (which require further analysis). The commentary then examines the expression 'by which it is what it is', explaining that it requires a conceptual distinction between subject and predicate, and addresses the objection from the efficient cause: the efficient cause makes the thing existent, not the thing itself. It notes that this definition applies to both universal and particular, unlike the logical definition. The passage concludes with a discussion of whether quiddity is made (maj'ūla) and a summary of the definition according to the view that quiddity is identical to mental forms which are the external affair when stripped of accidents.

Key Terms
المجعول (al-maj'ūl)
الصورة العقلية (al-ṣūra al-'aqliyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the definition 'by which it is what it is' applies only to external existents and excludes non-existent quiddities.
Correction: The passage states that 'thing' can be broader, including non-existent, so the definition applies to quiddity in general.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the objection from the efficient cause repelled?
A) By saying the efficient cause makes the thing existent, not the thing itself.
B) By interpreting 'thing' as the external affair and the 'bā' as denoting unity.
C) By arguing that quiddity is not made by a maker.
D) By claiming the definition is only for mental forms.

Key Takeaway
The definition 'by which it is what it is' is interpreted to mean that quiddity is the mental form that is identical to the external affair when stripped of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very essence (quiddity) is merely a mental form that becomes identical to your external existence only when combined with it, what does that say about the depth of your dependence on Allah for every moment of your existence? Sit in silence for one minute, then say: 'O Allah, I am nothing without You. Let my existence be a testament to Your sustaining power.'
?
The author says quiddity is what remains when all accidents are stripped away. Strip away your health, wealth, status, appearance, and even your thoughts—what is left? Is that core something you have earned or something given? How should this reshape your pride and your prayer? Commit to making one sincere dua today acknowledging your absolute need for Allah.
?
The passage addresses the efficient cause objection to show that the definition of quiddity is not about what brings something into existence, but what it is in itself. Reflect: Do you often define yourself by your origins, your past, or what others have made you? Or do you seek to know your true reality before Allah? Write down one identity label you cling to that might be accidental, and ask Allah to show you your true self in His sight.

Haqiqa: Definition, Types, and First Branch (Mubayana)
Defines haqiqa, its two types, and the first branch of mahiya's relation to mubayana matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of haqiqa (reality), its two types (huwiya and mahiya), and the first branch of its relation to matters that are separate (mubayana) from it.

Core Idea
Haqiqa branches into partial identity (huwiya) and universal quiddity (mahiya); the first logical relation is separation (mubayana).

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces the foundational concept of haqiqa (reality) in kalam. It distinguishes between the partial reality of an individual thing (huwiya) and the universal reality shared by a species (mahiya). It then explains that when the mahiya is considered in relation to matters that are completely separate from it, there is no confusion because those matters are clearly distinct—they are neither the mahiya itself, nor part of it, nor accidental to it. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping how later discussions treat the attributes of God and created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation, noting how haqiqa is defined and divided.
Pay attention to the technical terms: haqiqa, huwiya, mahiya, mubayana.
2
Identify the two types of haqiqa
Distinguish between huwiya (partial reality, sometimes meaning external existence) and mahiya (universal reality, quiddity). Understand why this distinction matters.
Huwiya is specific to an individual; mahiya is the universal essence.
3
Understand the first branch (mubayana)
Focus on what it means for matters to be mubayana (separate) from mahiya. Note the three negations: they are not the mahiya itself, not internal to it, and not accidental to it.
This is the simplest relation—complete distinctness.
4
Reflect on why there is no confusion
The commentary explains that because these matters are completely distinct, there is no ambiguity. This is the foundation for more complex relations in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفسير لمفهوم حقيقة الشيء، والحقيقة الجزئية تسمى هوية وقد تستعمل الهوية بمعنى الوجود الخارجي، والحقيقة الكلية تسمى ماهية ، ثم الحقيقة من حيث هي : إما أن تقاس إلى أمور مباينة إياها فذلك لا التباس فيه ؛ لأن الأمور المباينة لها مسلوبة بمعنى أنه ليست نفس الماهية ولا داخلة فيها ولا عارضة لها. وإما أن تقاس إلى قوله : (حقيقة الظاهر ماهية إلا أنه أقام لفظ الحقيقة مقامها تنبيهاً على اتحادهما، ولذا لم يتعرض الشارح لبيان اتحادهما . قوله : (تفسير إلخ ) يعني أن الصفة كاشفة لا مقيدة. قوله : (ثم الحقيقة من حيث هي ) أي من غير أن يلاحظ معه شيء حتى هذه الحيثية، فكأنه قيل : ما صدق عليه الحقيقة من غير ملاحظة أمر معه . قوله : ( مباينة إلخ ) أي مفارقة يدل عليه قول : ولا عارضة . قوله : (فذلك) أي المقاس لا التباس فيه بشيء من تلك الأمور ؛ لامتيازه عنها من جميع الوجوه، فلذا لم يتعرض المصنف لبيانه .
Explanation of the concept of the reality (haqiqa) of a thing. The partial reality (al-haqiqa al-juz'iyya) is called identity (huwiya), and identity may be used in the sense of external existence. The universal reality (al-haqiqa al-kulliyya) is called quiddity (mahiya). Then reality in itself: either it is measured against matters that are separate (mubayina) from it, and that has no confusion because matters separate from it are negated, in the sense that they are not the quiddity itself, nor internal to it, nor accidental to it. Or it is measured against… His saying: (The reality of the apparent is quiddity except that he placed the word reality in its position to indicate their unity, so the commentator did not discuss their unity.) His saying: (Interpretation etc.) means that the attribute is disclosing, not restrictive. His saying: (Then reality in itself) i.e., without observing anything with it, even this respect, so it is as if it is said: what the reality is true of without observing an accompanying matter. His saying: (Separate etc.) i.e., distinct, as indicated by his statement: and not accidental. His saying: (Thus that) i.e., the measured thing, there is no confusion in it with any of those matters because of its distinctness from them in all respects, so the author did not need to explain it.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of haqiqa (reality) and then classifies its relation to other things. First, it explains that haqiqa has two types: partial reality, called huwiya (identity), which is sometimes used to mean external existence, and universal reality, called mahiya (quiddity). Then it states that reality in itself can be considered in relation to matters that are mubayana (separate) from it. For such matters, there is no confusion because they are clearly distinct from the mahiya—they are not the mahiya itself, nor part of it, nor accidental to it. The commentary then elucidates several points: the phrase about 'the reality of the apparent' emphasizes the unity of haqiqa and mahiya; the attribute in the definition is kashifa (disclosing) not muqayyida (restrictive); the phrase 'then reality in itself' means without considering any accompanying factor; 'mubayana' means distinct (mufariqa); and the reason there is no confusion is the complete distinctness of the measured thing from those matters, so the author did not need to elaborate further.

هوية
ماهية
مباينة
عارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that haqiqa only refers to the universal essence (mahiya) and does not include individual identity.
Correction: The passage clearly states that haqiqa has two types: partial (huwiya) and universal (mahiya). Huwiya is the reality of an individual thing, sometimes synonymous with external existence.

Key Takeaway
The first step in understanding the reality of things is to distinguish between partial identity (huwiya) and universal quiddity (mahiya), and to recognize that matters completely separate from the mahiya are unambiguously distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can knowing this help you clear confusion about your purpose? What one step will you take today to align your actions with your true reality?

The Definition of Mahiya: Scope and Interpretations
First part: definition, scope of 'thing', and objection-response on mahiya being made.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of mahiya as 'that by which a thing is what it is' is interpreted differently based on the scope of 'thing' and whether mahiya is considered made or not.

Core Idea
The definition of mahiya is not merely descriptive; it involves a deep relation between mental form and external reality.

In this passage, al-Sharif al-Jurjani begins by unpacking the famous definition of mahiya. He clarifies what 'thing' means in this context—whether it includes non-existents or only existents—and then addresses a theological dispute about whether mahiya is created by a maker. This sets the stage for understanding the definition's precise meaning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'thing'
Read how the author explains that 'thing' here is more general than 'existent', including non-existent mahiyas, though it can also mean only the existent.
Note the flexibility of terminology in kalam.
2
Examine the definition's basis
See the interpretation from Sharh al-Maqasid: the definition assumes mahiya is not made by a maker. Consider the opposing view that mahiya is made.
This is a key point of difference between philosophers and mutakallimun.
3
Follow the objection and response
The author presents an objection: even those who say mahiya is made accept this definition. Then he gives a response involving the unity of mental form and external reality.
The response may seem artificial; the author notes this.
4
Grasp the summary
The definition boils down to 'that by which external reality is united in existence'. Reflect on what that means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لكل شيء حقيقة هو بها هو ( الظاهر أن المراد بالشيء ما هو أعلم من الموجود ولو مجازاً، إذ الماهية تعم الموجود والمعدوم، وهي المرادة بالحقيقة ها هنا، ويمكن أن يراد به معناه الحقيقي ، أعني الموجود بناء على ما اشتهر من أن الحقيقة تختص بالموجود، ثم قوله : هو بها أن في موقع التعريف للحقيقة، والظاهر على ما في شرح المقاصد أن التفسير المذكور مبني على الماهية ليست مجعولة بجعل الجاعل كما هو رأي جمهور الفلاسفة والمعتزلة، فلا يصدق التعريف على العلة الفاعلية وقد يمنع البناء على ما ذكر ؛ لأن القائلين بأن الماهية مجعولة يفسرونها بهذا التفسير أيضاً. ويدفع الاعتراض بالعلة الفاعلية بأن الشيءعبارة عن الأمر الخارجي، والباء في بها متعلقة بالاتحاد المستفاد من هو هو، فإن هو هو كانه الاتحاد، علم في ولذا لم يقل : ما به الشيء هو مع أنه أخصر، وتلخيصه : أن الماهية عبارة عن الصور العقلية، وهي من حيث ذاتها نفس الأمر الخارجي، فإنه لو اقترنت الصورة العقلية بالوجود الخارجي وما يتبعه كان الحاصل عين الأمر الخارجي، وإذا جرد الموجود الخارجي عن العوارض كان الباقي فيه تلك الصور العقلية، فمعنى التعريف ما به يتحد الأمر الخارجي في الوجود ولا يخفى عليك ما فيه من التعسف. قوله : (هو بها هو لا بد من اعتبار التغاير بين الموضوع والمحمول ليصح الحمل، فالمراد الأول ذات الشيء، وبالثاني ما يلزمه وهو كونه متحصلا في نفسه بحيث بهو، والسبيية المستفادة من الباء يكفيه التغاير الاعتباري، ولا يتجه النقض بالفاعل إذ الفاعل يتحصل به وجود الشيء لا الشيء نفسه. وهذا معنى ما قالوا: إن الفاعل يجعل الشيء موجوداً لا ذلك الشيء، وهذا التفسير شامل للكلي والجزئي، بخلاف ما به يجاب عن الشيء بما هو على ما هو مصطلح المنطقي، فإنه مختص بالكل، وبين المعنيين عموم وخصوص من وجه .
His saying: (For everything there is a reality by which it is what it is.) Apparently, what is meant by 'thing' (al-shay') is what is more general than the existent, even if metaphorically, for quiddity (mahiya) includes the existent and the non-existent, and it is what is meant by 'reality' here. It is also possible that what is meant is its real meaning, i.e., the existent, based on what is well-known that reality is specific to the existent. Then his saying: 'by which it is' (huwa bihi huwa) is in the position of defining reality. And apparently, according to what is in Sharh al-Maqasid, the mentioned interpretation is based on the quiddity not being made (maj'ula) by the making of a maker, as is the view of the majority of philosophers and the Mu'tazila; so the definition does not apply to the efficient cause. But it is possible to object to this foundation because those who say that quiddity is made also interpret it with this interpretation. And the objection regarding the efficient cause is repelled by saying that 'thing' (al-shay') refers to the external reality (al-amr al-khariji), and the 'ba' in 'bihi' is attached to the unity (al-ittihad) derived from 'huwa huwa', for 'huwa huwa' is like the unity. That is why he did not say 'ma bihi al-shay' huwa' even though it is more concise. The summary: quiddity is the mental forms (al-suwar al-'aqliyya), and they are in themselves the same as the external reality. For if the mental form is conjoined with external existence and what follows it, the result is the very external reality. And if the external existent is stripped of accidents, what remains in it are those mental forms. So the meaning of the definition is 'that by which the external reality is united in existence'. And it is not hidden from you what artificiality there is in it. His saying: (By which it is what it is (huwa bihi huwa) must consider the difference (al-taghayur) between subject and predicate for the predication to be valid. So the first refers to the essence of the thing, and the second to what accompanies it, i.e., its being realized in itself such that it is what it is. The causality (al-sababiyya) derived from the 'ba' suffices for the conceptual difference (al-taghayur al-i'tibari). And the objection regarding the agent is not valid, for the agent realizes the existence of the thing, not the thing itself. This is the meaning of what they said: the agent makes the thing existent, not that thing. This interpretation includes both the universal and the particular, unlike 'that by which one answers what the thing is' as per the logicians' terminology, which is specific to the universal. There is generality and particularity from one perspective between the two meanings.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage analyzes the definition of mahiya (quiddity) as 'that by which a thing is what it is' (ma bihi al-shay' huwa huwa). The commentary begins by clarifying the term 'thing': it is more general than 'existent', including both existent and non-existent, but it may also specifically refer to existent based on the view that reality (haqiqa) is specific to existents. The interpretation in Sharh al-Maqasid is that this definition presupposes that mahiya is not made (maj'ula) by a maker, a view of most philosophers and Mu'tazila. However, an objection is raised that even those who hold mahiya as made still accept this definition. The response is that 'thing' here means external reality, and the 'ba' in 'bihi' indicates the unity derived from 'huwa huwa' (identity). The definition is summarized as 'that by which the external reality is united in existence', though the commentator notes some artificiality. The second part of the commentary explains that the phrase 'huwa bihi huwa' requires a conceptual difference (taghayur i'tibari) between subject and predicate for the predication to be valid: the first 'huwa' refers to the essence, the second to what accompanies it (its being realized). The 'ba' implies causality, and this suffices for the conceptual difference. The definition applies to both universal and particular mahiyas, unlike the logical definition which is specific to universals. The two meanings have a general-particular relationship from one perspective.

Key Terms
شيء
ماهية
حقيقة
هو بهو
علة فاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the definition 'ma bihi al-shay' huwa huwa' means merely that the thing has a reality, without further implication.
Correction: The definition involves a specific relation: the 'ba' indicates unity, and the definition is about the union of mental form with external existence, not just a property.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the interpretation that the definition of mahiya does not apply to the efficient cause?
A) The efficient cause is not a 'thing'
B) The definition assumes mahiya is not made by a maker
C) The efficient cause has no reality
D) The definition only applies to mental forms

Key Takeaway
The definition of mahiya as 'ma bihi al-shay' huwa huwa' is interpreted in light of whether mahiya is considered created or not, with a response that redefines 'thing' as external reality and emphasizes the unity of mental and external aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the reality of your own actions—are they merely mental forms (intentions and thoughts) or do they have an external reality that truly defines you? This passage suggests that the reality of a thing is its essence when stripped of accidents. Strip away the excuses and justifications: what remains as the true reality of your deeds? Are you willing to face that stripped reality today?
?
The passage shows that a definition can be reinterpreted to fit different theological stances. In your own life, have you ever reshaped your understanding of a truth to avoid discomfort or accountability? Confront that tendency now: ask yourself if your current beliefs are molded more by convenience than by a sincere pursuit of what is real, and commit to seeking the objective truth even if it costs you.
?
The commentator notes 'artificiality' in the response defending the definition. When you defend your own positions—whether in theology or daily life—do you sometimes resort to artificial arguments? This lesson challenges you to examine your defenses: are they grounded in reality, or are they cleverly constructed to avoid admitting error? Pause and ask God to show you the truth, even if it humbles you.

The Logic of 'Huwa Bihi Huwa': Conceptual Difference and Scope
Second part: necessity of conceptual difference, role of 'ba', comparison with logical definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical and logical requirements of the phrase 'huwa bihi huwa' in defining mahiya, including the need for a conceptual difference and its validity for universals and particulars.

Core Idea
The repetition in 'huwa bihi huwa' is not redundant; it signals a unity with a conceptual difference that allows predication.

The second part of the commentary dives into the precise wording of the definition: 'huwa bihi huwa'. Why repeat 'huwa'? Because a simple 'ma bihi al-shay' huwa' would be insufficient. This section explains that the phrase requires a distinction between the subject and the predicate to make a valid predication. It also compares this definition with the logicians' definition of 'that by which one answers what the thing is'.

Guided Path
1
Notice the grammar of 'huwa bihi huwa'
See that the author explains the need for conceptual difference between the first and second 'huwa'. The first refers to the essence, the second to its realized state.
This avoids identity without distinction.
2
Understand the role of the 'ba'
The 'ba' indicates causality (sababiyya), which provides enough of a conceptual difference without implying actual separation.
Conceptual difference (taghayur i'tibari) is sufficient.
3
Apply to the agent objection
The definition is not invalidated by the efficient cause because the agent brings about existence, not the thing itself.
This preserves the definition's scope.
4
Contrast with the logical definition
The logical definition (answer to 'what is it?') applies only to universals, while this definition applies to both universals and particulars.
A key difference: the two definitions overlap in some cases but not all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لكل شيء حقيقة هو بها هو ( الظاهر أن المراد بالشيء ما هو أعلم من الموجود ولو مجازاً، إذ الماهية تعم الموجود والمعدوم، وهي المرادة بالحقيقة ها هنا، ويمكن أن يراد به معناه الحقيقي ، أعني الموجود بناء على ما اشتهر من أن الحقيقة تختص بالموجود، ثم قوله : هو بها أن في موقع التعريف للحقيقة، والظاهر على ما في شرح المقاصد أن التفسير المذكور مبني على الماهية ليست مجعولة بجعل الجاعل كما هو رأي جمهور الفلاسفة والمعتزلة، فلا يصدق التعريف على العلة الفاعلية وقد يمنع البناء على ما ذكر ؛ لأن القائلين بأن الماهية مجعولة يفسرونها بهذا التفسير أيضاً. ويدفع الاعتراض بالعلة الفاعلية بأن الشيءعبارة عن الأمر الخارجي، والباء في بها متعلقة بالاتحاد المستفاد من هو هو، فإن هو هو كانه الاتحاد، علم في ولذا لم يقل : ما به الشيء هو مع أنه أخصر، وتلخيصه : أن الماهية عبارة عن الصور العقلية، وهي من حيث ذاتها نفس الأمر الخارجي، فإنه لو اقترنت الصورة العقلية بالوجود الخارجي وما يتبعه كان الحاصل عين الأمر الخارجي، وإذا جرد الموجود الخارجي عن العوارض كان الباقي فيه تلك الصور العقلية، فمعنى التعريف ما به يتحد الأمر الخارجي في الوجود ولا يخفى عليك ما فيه من التعسف. قوله : (هو بها هو لا بد من اعتبار التغاير بين الموضوع والمحمول ليصح الحمل، فالمراد الأول ذات الشيء، وبالثاني ما يلزمه وهو كونه متحصلا في نفسه بحيث بهو، والسبيية المستفادة من الباء يكفيه التغاير الاعتباري، ولا يتجه النقض بالفاعل إذ الفاعل يتحصل به وجود الشيء لا الشيء نفسه. وهذا معنى ما قالوا: إن الفاعل يجعل الشيء موجوداً لا ذلك الشيء، وهذا التفسير شامل للكلي والجزئي، بخلاف ما به يجاب عن الشيء بما هو على ما هو مصطلح المنطقي، فإنه مختص بالكل، وبين المعنيين عموم وخصوص من وجه .
His saying: (For everything there is a reality by which it is what it is.) Apparently, what is meant by 'thing' (al-shay') is what is more general than the existent, even if metaphorically, for quiddity (mahiya) includes the existent and the non-existent, and it is what is meant by 'reality' here. It is also possible that what is meant is its real meaning, i.e., the existent, based on what is well-known that reality is specific to the existent. Then his saying: 'by which it is' (huwa bihi huwa) is in the position of defining reality. And apparently, according to what is in Sharh al-Maqasid, the mentioned interpretation is based on the quiddity not being made (maj'ula) by the making of a maker, as is the view of the majority of philosophers and the Mu'tazila; so the definition does not apply to the efficient cause. But it is possible to object to this foundation because those who say that quiddity is made also interpret it with this interpretation. And the objection regarding the efficient cause is repelled by saying that 'thing' (al-shay') refers to the external reality (al-amr al-khariji), and the 'ba' in 'bihi' is attached to the unity (al-ittihad) derived from 'huwa huwa', for 'huwa huwa' is like the unity. That is why he did not say 'ma bihi al-shay' huwa' even though it is more concise. The summary: quiddity is the mental forms (al-suwar al-'aqliyya), and they are in themselves the same as the external reality. For if the mental form is conjoined with external existence and what follows it, the result is the very external reality. And if the external existent is stripped of accidents, what remains in it are those mental forms. So the meaning of the definition is 'that by which the external reality is united in existence'. And it is not hidden from you what artificiality there is in it. His saying: (By which it is what it is (huwa bihi huwa) must consider the difference (al-taghayur) between subject and predicate for the predication to be valid. So the first refers to the essence of the thing, and the second to what accompanies it, i.e., its being realized in itself such that it is what it is. The causality (al-sababiyya) derived from the 'ba' suffices for the conceptual difference (al-taghayur al-i'tibari). And the objection regarding the agent is not valid, for the agent realizes the existence of the thing, not the thing itself. This is the meaning of what they said: the agent makes the thing existent, not that thing. This interpretation includes both the universal and the particular, unlike 'that by which one answers what the thing is' as per the logicians' terminology, which is specific to the universal. There is generality and particularity from one perspective between the two meanings.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage analyzes the definition of mahiya (quiddity) as 'that by which a thing is what it is' (ma bihi al-shay' huwa huwa). The commentary begins by clarifying the term 'thing': it is more general than 'existent', including both existent and non-existent, but it may also specifically refer to existent based on the view that reality (haqiqa) is specific to existents. The interpretation in Sharh al-Maqasid is that this definition presupposes that mahiya is not made (maj'ula) by a maker, a view of most philosophers and Mu'tazila. However, an objection is raised that even those who hold mahiya as made still accept this definition. The response is that 'thing' here means external reality, and the 'ba' in 'bihi' indicates the unity derived from 'huwa huwa' (identity). The definition is summarized as 'that by which the external reality is united in existence', though the commentator notes some artificiality. The second part of the commentary explains that the phrase 'huwa bihi huwa' requires a conceptual difference (taghayur i'tibari) between subject and predicate for the predication to be valid: the first 'huwa' refers to the essence, the second to what accompanies it (its being realized). The 'ba' implies causality, and this suffices for the conceptual difference. The definition applies to both universal and particular mahiyas, unlike the logical definition which is specific to universals. The two meanings have a general-particular relationship from one perspective.

Key Terms
تغاير اعتباري
هو هو
هو بهو
تعريف
كلية
جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the efficient cause not a counterexample to the definition of mahiya according to the passage?
A) The efficient cause has no essence
B) The efficient cause brings about existence, not the thing itself
C) The efficient cause is not a 'thing'
D) The definition only applies to mental forms

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'huwa bihi huwa' implies a conceptual difference between essence and its realization, with the 'ba' signifying causality, and the definition applies to both particulars and universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define yourself by your actions or achievements, are you confusing your essence with your existence? How can the distinction in this lesson help you see yourself more clearly?
?
The agent brings about existence, not essence. Think of someone you hold a grudge against. Do you judge their essence based on their actions? How would separating their essence from their existence change how you treat them?

The Three Comparisons: Essence and Separate, Accidental, and Internal Matters
Al-Jurjani enumerates the three comparisons and states the correct negation for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of things that are other than the essence (separate matters, accidental matters, internal matters) and the correct negation for each based on rank and description.

Core Idea
Negation of essence from others depends on rank and description; only separate matters allow both.

Al-Jurjani now lays out the three kinds of things that are not the essence itself: those completely separate, those that inhere as accidents, and those that are internal constituents. For each, he specifies which aspects of negation are valid and which are not. This classification is foundational for understanding how to speak correctly about what the essence is and is not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the three categories.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three types of 'other'
Find the three terms: مباينة (separate), عارضة (accidental), داخلة (internal). Notice how each is introduced with 'وإذا قيست إلى'.
These are the three comparisons.
3
Note the valid and invalid negations
For each type, the author states what negation is sound (صح) and what is not (لا يصح). Pay attention to the two aspects: rank (مرتبة) and description (اتصاف).
Only separate matters allow negation in both aspects.
4
Understand the objection
The last part addresses an objection about internal matters: why not also say 'nor an accident'? The author explains why that is not said.
Reflect on why saying 'not an accident' for internal matters would be incorrect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيست الماهية إلى الأمور الداخلة فيها صح السلب بمعنى أنها ليست نفسها ; لأن الداخل في الماهية ليس عينها من حيث هو داخل فيها . وأما الأجزاء المحمولة فهي، وإن كانت بحسب الخارج عين الماهية، لكن باعتبار آخر. فإذا سألنا بطرفي النقيض وقيل : الإنسانية ) من حيث هي إنسانية (ا)، وليست (ا) كان الجواب الصحيح أنها ليست من حيث هي (ا) ؛ لا أنها من حيث هي ليست (ا)، فإن تقديم حرف السلب على الحيثية كما في العبارة الأولى معناه المتبادر أنها إذا أخذت بهذه الحيثية لا هي إلى الأمور المباينة أي المنفكة عنها صح نفيها عنها باعتبار المرتبة والاتصاف معاً، فيقال : إنها ليست نفسها ولا داخلة فيها، لعدم كونها في مرتبتها ولا عارضة لها؛ لعدم اتصافها بها، وإذا قيست إلى الأمور العارضة صح نفيها عنها باعتبار المرتبة بالوجهين فيقال : ليست نفسها ولا داخلة فيها؛ لعدم كونها في مرتبتها، ولا يصح نفيها باعتبار الاتصاف بأحد النقيضين، وإذا قيست إلى الأمور الداخلة صح نفيها عنها باعتبار المرتبة، بمعنى أنها ليست نفسها فقط؛ لأن في مرتبة الماهية شيئان نفسها، ومقوماتها ونفي المقومية ليس بصحيح، فبقي نفي العينية. فاندفع ما قيل : إنه ينبغي أن يقول : ولا عارضة لها أيضاً ، فتدبر فإنه قد زل فيه الأقدام . قوله : ( فإذا سئلنا إلخ) تفريع على قوله : فالإنسانية من حيث هي إنسانية ليست إلا الإنسانية قوله : ( بطرفي (النقيض أي بالمفردين اللذين كل واحد منهما نقيض الآخر، بأن يؤخذ أحدهما سلباً للآخر لا عدولاً ويردد بينهما . قوله : ( كان الجواب الصحيح أي الجواب الذي لا شبهة في صحته بناء على المعنى المتبادر . قوله : ( فإن تقديم الخ ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره يدل على أن مدار الفرق تقديم السلب على الربط وتأخيره فإنه على الأول تكون القضية سالبة فيفيد نفي الاقتضاء، وهو صحيح. وعلى الثاني موجبة فيفيد اقتضاء الاتصاف بالسلب وهو باطل. وعبارة المتن يدل على أن مدار الفرق تقديم حرف السلب على الحيثية وتأخيره عنها ، وهو الظاهر؛ لأنه إذا أخرت كان معناه نفي كون الحيثية منشأ للاتصاف وإذا قدمت كان معناه أن الحيثية منشأ لسلب الاتصاف، وإن كانت القضية في الحالتين سالبة . قوله : ( المتبادر) قيد بذلك ؛ لأنه يمكن إرادة الاتصاف بالسلب بأن يعتبر السلب مؤخراً في المعنى، لكنه خلاف المتبادر ، وكذا الحال في صورة التقديم. وبالجملة : ماهية الأربعة إذ لم تكن مقتضية للزوجية بأي اعتبار أخذ كان عدم كونها قابلة للفردية بذلك الاعتبار بطريق الأولى فتأمل . قوله : (بمعنى أنها ليست نفسها إن قلت: لم لم يتعرض لصحة السلب بمعنى أنها ليست عارضة لها ؟ قلت لأن السلب بهذا المعنى لو صح لصح سلب الشيء عن نفسه، ولم يقل به أحد . قوله: (لكن باعتبار (آخر هو أن جعل الجزء الذهني جعل الكل، لا أن الطبيعة الجنسية مثلاً من حيث إنها جزء الطبيعة النوعية عينها.
And when the essence is compared to the internal matters, negation is sound in the sense that it is not identical to them, because the internal in the essence is not identical to it in terms of being internal in it. As for the predicative parts, even though they are externally identical to the essence, but from another consideration. So if we are asked with the two extremes of the contradiction and it is said: 'Humanity, as it is humanity, is (a), and is not (a)', the correct answer is that it is not, as it is, (a); not that it is, as it is, not (a). For advancing the particle of negation over the consideration, as in the first expression, its evident meaning is that if it is taken with this consideration, it is not [that] it is [identical] to the separate matters, i.e., those dissociated from it, negation of it from them is sound in terms of both rank and description, so it is said: It is not identical to them nor internal in them, because it is not in their rank nor an accident to them due to lack of description by them. And when it is compared to the accidental matters, negation of it from them is sound in terms of rank in both ways, so it is said: It is not identical to them nor internal in them, because it is not in their rank, but negation in terms of description by one of the two opposites is not sound. And when it is compared to the internal matters, negation of it from them is sound in terms of rank, meaning that it is not identical to them only; because in the rank of the essence there are two things: itself and its constituents, and negation of constituency is not sound, so only the negation of identity remains. So what was said is repelled: that he should have also said 'and it is not an accident to them', so reflect, for feet have slipped in it. His saying: '(fa-idha su'ilna etc.)' is a branching from his saying: 'Humanity as it is humanity is nothing but humanity.' His saying: '(bi-tarafay al-naqid)' means the two individuals each of which is the contradictory of the other, by taking one as the negation of the other without alternation, and the two are alternated. His saying: '(kana al-jawab al-sahih)' means the answer that has no doubt in its correctness based on the evident meaning. His saying: '(fa-inna taqdim al-khabar etc.)' indicates that the basis of the distinction is the advancing of the negation over the connection and its delay; for in the first, the proposition becomes negative and it gives the negation of necessitation, which is correct. And in the second, it is affirmative and gives the necessitation of description with the negation, which is false. And the expression of the text indicates that the basis of the distinction is the advancing of the particle of negation over the consideration and its delay from it, and it is apparent; because when it is delayed, its meaning is the negation of the consideration being a source for the description, and when it is advanced, its meaning is that the consideration is a source for the negation of description, even though the proposition in both cases is negative. His saying: '(al-mutabadir)' he qualified it with that because it is possible to intend the description with the negation by considering the negation as delayed in meaning, but it is contrary to the evident meaning, and similarly in the case of advancing. In summary: The essence of four, since it is not necessitating of evenness by whatever consideration taken, its not being capable of oddness by that consideration is a fortiori, so reflect. His saying: '(bi-ma'na annaha laysat nafsuha)' – if you say: Why did he not address the soundness of negation in the sense that it is not an accident to it? I say: Because if negation in this sense were correct, it would necessitate the negation of a thing from itself, which no one has said. His saying: '(lakin bi-i'tibar akhar)' is that making the mental part is making the whole, not that the generic nature, for example, as it is part of the specific nature, is identical to it.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage classifies the three types of things that are other than the essence: internal matters (i.e. constituents), accidental matters, and separate matters. For each, the correct negation is explained. The author then discusses the correct answer when asked about the essence and its two opposites, emphasizing that the negation must be of the description, not of the consideration. The placement of the negation particle is crucial: if placed before the consideration, it means the consideration is a source for the negation of the description; if after, it negates the consideration being a source for the description. The commentary then addresses an objection about the need to also say 'not an accident', and explains why it is not said. Finally, a summary about the four and evenness illustrates the principle.

Key Terms
أمور مباينة
أمور عارضة
أمور داخلة
سلب
مرتبة
اتصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that for internal matters, the essence can be said to be 'not an accident' to them, just like separate matters.
Correction: For internal matters, saying 'not an accident' is incorrect because it would negate the essence from itself (since constituents are part of the essence). Only negation of identity is valid.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when comparing the essence to accidental matters, which kind of negation is not sound?
A) Negation of identity.
B) Negation of inherence.
C) Negation of description by one of the two opposites.
D) Negation of being a constituent.

Key Takeaway
Only separate matters allow negation of the essence in both rank and description; internal matters only allow negation of identity.

The Place of the Negation Particle with 'Consideration'
Al-Jurjani explains the correct answer to the contradictory pair and the role of particle placement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the placement of the negation particle relative to the word 'consideration' (haythiyya) changes the logical meaning of the proposition about the essence.

Core Idea
Where you put 'not' determines whether you negate the consideration or the description.

Al-Jurjani now examines a specific logical question: when we ask 'Is humanity, as humanity, F or not F?', the correct answer depends on where we place the negation particle. This lesson unpacks that subtlety and shows how the placement affects the meaning, whether the proposition is understood as negative or affirmative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two extremes
Read the Arabic passage that poses the question about humanity and the two opposites. Focus on the difference between 'laysat min haythu hiya (a)' and 'min haythu hiya laysat (a)'.
The translation distinguishes the two orders.
2
Understand the rule
The author explains that placing the negation before 'consideration' (تقديم السلب على الحيثية) makes the proposition negative, which is correct. Placing it after makes it affirmative, which is false.
This is about the logical impact of word order.
3
Connect to the earlier comparisons
Notice how this rule is applied to the three comparisons from the previous lesson. The correct answer 'laysat min haythu hiya (a)' is used for separate matters.
The rule is consistent across the three cases.
4
Reflect on the commentary
The commentary clarifies that the basis of distinction is advancing or delaying the negation particle relative to the consideration, not the connective.
This reinforces the importance of word order in logic.

According to the passage, what is the correct answer when asked about humanity and the two extremes?
A) 'It is, as it is, not (a).'

D) 'It is both (a) and not (a).'

Key Takeaway
The correct negation of an attribute from an essence under a consideration requires the negation particle to precede the consideration word.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah's attributes, are you careful about the exact phrasing so that you do not inadvertently deny a consideration instead of an attribute? What one change in your speech can you commit to today to avoid such a mistake?
?
Think of a recent argument you had where a small word order changed the entire meaning. How does this logical precision challenge you to examine your own assumptions and words more deeply?
?
If the correct negation requires the particle to precede the consideration, what does that teach you about the importance of sequence in thought and speech? How can you apply this to your daily interactions and worship?

Distinguishing Essence from Others and the Correct Negation Placement
Explanation of the correct answer when asked about essence with contradictory extremes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the placement of the negation particle relative to 'ḥaythiyya' is crucial when determining the relationship between an essence and different types of other matters (separate, accidental, internal).

Core Idea
The negation particle's position relative to 'ḥaythiyya' determines whether the essence is truly negated or not.

In this passage, the author addresses a common logical question: when we ask about an essence like 'humanity' with a contradictory pair (is it or is it not?), how should we answer? The correct answer depends on the placement of the negation word. The author explains this by considering three categories of matters: those separate from the essence, accidental to it, and internal to it. This lesson focuses on the main argument and the three categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'المرصد الثاني' to 'فاندفع ما قيل' without trying to parse every detail. Let the rhythm of the argument sink in.
The passage is dense; focus on the overall flow.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage compares the essence to three types of matters: مبايَنة (separate), عارضة (accidental), and داخلة (internal). Notice how the valid negations differ for each.
For separate matters, both negation of identity and inherence are valid. For accidental, only identity and inherence (but not attribution). For internal, only identity.
3
Understand the core rule
The key is that when the negation precedes the 'ḥaythiyya', the meaning is that the essence, taken with that 'ḥaythiyya', is not the thing. When the negation follows, it would mean the essence is characterized by negation, which is false.
The author explicitly contrasts 'ليست من حيث هي' with 'من حيث هي ليست'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الأول : في تمييز الماهية عما عداها
ليس عينها من حيث هو داخل فيها. وأما الأجزاء المحمولة فهي، وإن كانت بحسب الخارج عين الماهية، لكن باعتبار آخر. فإذا سألنا بطرفي النقيض وقيل: الإنسانية من حيث هي إنسانية، وليست، كان الجواب الصحيح أنها ليست من حيث هي لا أنها من حيث هي ليست، فإن تقديم حرف السلب على الحيثية كما في العبارة الأولى معناه المتبادر أنها إذا أخذت بهذه الحيثية لا هي إلى الأمور المباينة أي المنفكة عنها صح نفيها عنها باعتبار المرتبة والاتصاف معاً، فيقال: إنها ليست نفسها ولا داخلة فيها، لعدم كونها في مرتبتها ولا عارضة لها؛ لعدم اتصافها بها، وإذا قيست إلى الأمور العارضة صح نفيها عنها باعتبار المرتبة بالوجهين فيقال: ليست نفسها ولا داخلة فيها؛ لعدم كونها في مرتبتها، ولا يصح نفيها باعتبار الاتصاف بأحد النقيضين، وإذا قيست إلى الأمور الداخلة صح نفيها عنها باعتبار المرتبة، بمعنى أنها ليست نفسها فقط؛ لأن في مرتبة الماهية شيئان نفسها، ومقوماتها ونفي المقومية ليس بصحيح، فبقي نفي العينية.

فاندفع ما قيل: إنه ينبغي أن يقول: ولا عارضة لها أيضاً، فتدبر فإنه قد زل فيه الأقدام. قوله: (فإذا سئلنا إلخ) تفريع على قوله: فالإنسانية من حيث هي إنسانية ليست إلا الإنسانية قوله: (بطرفي النقيض أي بالمفردين اللذين كل واحد منهما نقيض الآخر، بأن يؤخذ أحدهما سلباً للآخر لا عدولاً ويردد بينهما. قوله: (كان الجواب الصحيح أي الجواب الذي لا شبهة في صحته بناء على المعنى المتبادر. قوله: (فإن تقديم الخ ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره يدل على أن مدار الفرق تقديم السلب على الربط وتأخيره فإنه على الأول تكون القضية سالبة فيفيد نفي الاقتضاء، وهو صحيح. وعلى الثاني موجبة فيفيد اقتضاء الاتصاف بالسلب وهو باطل. وعبارة المتن يدل على أن مدار الفرق تقديم حرف السلب على الحيثية وتأخيره عنها، وهو الظاهر؛ لأنه إذا أخرت كان معناه نفي كون الحيثية منشأ للاتصاف وإذا قدمت كان معناه أن الحيثية منشأ لسلب الاتصاف، وإن كانت القضية في الحالتين سالبة.

قوله: (المتبادر) قيد بذلك؛ لأنه يمكن إرادة الاتصاف بالسلب بأن يعتبر السلب مؤخراً في المعنى، لكنه خلاف المتبادر، وكذا الحال في صورة التقديم.
Second Observatory - First Objective: On Distinguishing the Essence from What is Other Than It. It is not identical to that which is included in it. As for the predicated parts, they, although in external reality they are identical to the essence, yet in another respect [they are not]. So if we ask with the two extremes of contradiction and say: 'Humanity insofar as it is humanity, and it is not,' the correct answer is that it is not insofar as it is, not that it insofar as it is is not. For placing the negation particle before the 'insofar as' (ḥaythiyya) as in the first expression, its apparent meaning is that when [the essence] is taken with this 'insofar as', it is not [the thing] - [when comparing] to matters that are separate, i.e., separable from it, its negation from them is valid with regard to rank and attribution together, so it is said: It is not identical to them nor included in them, because it is not in their rank and not an accident of them; because it is not characterized by them. And when compared to accidental matters, its negation from them is valid with regard to rank in both ways: it is said: It is not identical to them nor included in them, because it is not in their rank; but its negation with regard to attribution by one of the two contradictories is not valid. And when compared to internal matters, its negation from them is valid with regard to rank, meaning that it is not identical only; because in the rank of the essence there are two things: itself and its constituents, and negation of being a constituent is not correct, so only the negation of identity remains. Thus, what was said (that he should have said 'and not an accident of them also') is refuted. So reflect, for feet have slipped here. His statement: (So if we are asked, etc.) is a branch of his statement: 'Humanity insofar as it is humanity is nothing but humanity.' His statement: (by the two extremes of contradiction) means by the two singular terms each of which is the contradictory of the other, i.e., one is taken as the negation of the other, not as an alternative, and the two are alternated. His statement: (the correct answer) means the answer in whose correctness there is no doubt based on the apparent meaning. His statement: (For placing, etc.) indicates that the criterion of the difference is placing the negation before the copula and delaying it; for in the first case the proposition is negative, thus it yields the negation of implication, and that is correct. In the second case it is positive, thus it yields the implication of being characterized by the negation, and that is false. And the text of the Matn indicates that the criterion of the difference is placing the negation particle before the 'insofar as' and delaying it relative to it, and that is apparent; because if it is delayed, its meaning is negating that the 'insofar as' is a source of attribution, and if it is placed before, its meaning is that the 'insofar as' is a source of the negation of attribution, even though the proposition in both cases is negative. His statement: (the apparent meaning) is a qualification to that; because one could intend being characterized by the negation by considering the negation as delayed in meaning, but that is contrary to the apparent meaning. And similarly in the case of placement before.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a proof, explaining how to correctly answer when asked about an essence (māhiyya) with respect to its relation to other things. The author first distinguishes between the essence itself and its parts. Then he introduces the correct answer when asked with the two extremes of contradiction (e.g., 'Is it or is it not?'). The key is the placement of the negation particle relative to the 'ḥaythiyya' (insofar as). The author divides the things to which the essence can be compared into three categories: separate matters, accidental matters, and internal matters. For each, he explains which negations are valid: for separate matters, both negation of identity and inherence are valid; for accidental matters, only negation of identity and inherence with respect to rank, but not negation of attribution; for internal matters, only negation of identity is valid. He then refutes an objection that he should have also mentioned negation as an accident. The rest of the passage is a gloss on specific phrases, explaining the meaning of 'two extremes of contradiction', 'correct answer', and the difference between placing the negation before the copula vs. before the ḥaythiyya. Finally, he clarifies that 'apparent meaning' is used because one could theoretically intend a different reading, but the apparent meaning is what matters.

Key Terms
الماهية
الحيثية
النقيض
السلب
الربط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the correct answer when asked 'is it or is it not?' should always be 'it is not' without considering the placement of 'insofar as'.
Correction: The passage shows that the placement of the negation particle relative to 'ḥaythiyya' is crucial; otherwise, the answer would imply a false characterization.

Checkpoint
When comparing the essence to accidental matters, which negation is NOT valid?
A) Negation of identity
B) Negation of inherence
C) Negation of attribution by one of the two contradictories
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The correct negation of an essence requires placing the negation particle before 'ḥaythiyya' to avoid implying that the essence itself is characterized by negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allāh is not like anything,' have you ensured that your negation is placed correctly — that you are negating similarity from His essence in the sense of 'Allāh, insofar as He is Allāh, is not like anything'? Or do you inadvertently imply that 'Allāh is such that He is not like anything,' which could be interpreted as similarity being a characteristic of His essence? How will you adjust your own speech about Allāh's attributes to reflect the precise logic of this lesson?
?
The author warns that many have slipped in this subtle distinction. Are you confident that you have not inadvertently slipped in your own understanding of negation when it comes to matters of faith? What specific step will you take today to ensure your statements about Allāh's essence and attributes are logically precise and free from ambiguity?
?
Consider a time when you defended a theological point by saying 'Islam does not teach X.' Did you examine whether your negation was properly placed — whether X is truly separable from the essence of Islam or whether you were negating something that is actually part of its definition? How can you apply the threefold classification from this lesson to avoid similar errors in your own reasoning?

Glosses on the Negation Formulation and the Criterion of Placement
Commentary glosses on key phrases regarding negation placement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed glosses on the phrases in the original text, including the meaning of 'two extremes of contradiction' and the criterion for placing the negation particle before or after the copula and the 'ḥaythiyya'.

Core Idea
The difference between placing negation before the copula vs. before 'ḥaythiyya' determines whether the proposition is negative or affirmative in meaning.

This passage provides explanatory glosses on key phrases from the main argument. The commentator clarifies what 'two extremes of contradiction' means, what constitutes a 'correct answer', and most importantly, the exact criterion for the placement of the negation particle: whether it precedes the copula (rabṭ) or the 'ḥaythiyya'. The commentator also notes that the 'apparent meaning' (mutabādir) is the standard, though other interpretations are possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic glosses
Read the Arabic text starting from 'قوله: (فإذا سئلنا إلخ)' to the end. Notice how each phrase is explained in turn.
The glosses are structured around tag phrases from the original.
2
Understand the criterion of placement
The commentator says that some authorities hold the criterion to be placing negation before or after the copula, while the text of the Matn indicates it is before or after the 'ḥaythiyya'. Understand both views.
The copula view yields negative vs. positive propositions; the ḥaythiyya view affects the source of attribution.
3
Grasp the role of 'apparent meaning'
The commentator qualifies that the discussion is based on the apparent meaning (al-mutabādir), because one could theoretically interpret the negation as delayed in meaning, but that is contrary to the apparent.
This shows a respect for linguistic convention.
