The Rule of Negation: Example of Four and Accident Issue
Al-Sanusi summarizes the rule with the example of four and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi uses the example of 'four' to illustrate the rule of negation, why negation as accident is not considered, and the nuance of mental parts.

Core Idea
The quiddity of four shows that negation follows a fortiori from the lack of implication.

This passage from al-Sanusi's commentary brings together a key example and resolves a potential objection. It demonstrates how logical principles are applied to concrete cases, clarifying that certain forms of negation would lead to absurdity. The lesson will guide you through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the three distinct parts: the summary with the example of four, the objection about negation as accident, and the nuance about mental parts.
The passage is compact; read slowly to catch the logical flow.
2
Understand the summary with the example of four
Focus on the first sentence: the quiddity of four does not imply evenness in any respect, so it a fortiori does not imply oddness. This illustrates the rule that negation follows from the absence of implication.
The example is meant to show the logical priority: if A does not imply B, then A does not imply not-B even more so.
3
Grasp the objection and the nuance about mental parts
The objection asks why the author did not address negation in the sense of 'not an accident'. The answer: that would imply self-negation, which is absurd. Then, a nuance is added about mental parts: making a part the whole is not the same as saying the part is identical to the whole.
The nuance prevents a misunderstanding about the relationship between generic and specific natures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة: ماهية الأربعة إذ لم تكن مقتضية للزوجية بأي اعتبار أخذ كان عدم كونها قابلة للفردية بذلك الاعتبار بطريق الأولى فتأمل.
قوله: (بمعنى أنها ليست نفسها إن قلت: لم لم يتعرض لصحة السلب بمعنى أنها ليست عارضة لها؟ قلت لأن السلب بهذا المعنى لو صح لصح سلب الشيء عن نفسه، ولم يقل به أحد.
قوله: (لكن باعتبار آخر هو أن جعل الجزء الذهني جعل الكل، لا أن الطبيعة الجنسية مثلاً من حيث إنها جزء الطبيعة النوعية عينها.
In summary: The quiddity of four, since it does not imply evenness in any respect considered, its not being susceptible to oddness in that respect is a fortiori. So reflect. His statement: (Meaning that it is not identical to itself. If you say: Why did he not address the validity of negation in the sense that it is not an accident of it? I say: Because negation in this sense, if valid, would necessitate negation of a thing of itself, and no one has said that.) His statement: (But in another respect, it is that making the mental part is making the whole, not that the generic nature, for example, insofar as it is part of the specific nature, is identical to it.)
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage first summarizes the previous rule using the example of the quiddity (māhiyya) of four. Since four does not imply evenness in any respect, it follows a fortiori that it does not imply oddness in that respect. Then it addresses an objection: why did the author not mention the validity of negation meaning 'not an accident'? The answer is that such a negation would imply negating a thing of itself, which is absurd. Finally, it clarifies a nuance regarding internal parts: considering a part mentally as the whole is different from saying the generic nature is identical to the specific nature.

Key Terms
الماهية (al-māhiyya)
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍā')
الجزء الذهني (al-juz' al-dhihnī)
الجنس (al-jins)
النوع (al-naw')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that negation of essence from itself as an accident is a valid possibility.
Correction: The passage states that if such negation were valid, it would imply negating a thing of itself, which is logically absurd.

Checkpoint
Why did the author not include the negation of the essence from itself in the sense of being an accident?
A) Because it is impossible
B) Because it would be self-negation
C) Because it is not relevant
D) Because it is not a valid logical operation

Key Takeaway
The example of four illustrates that negation follows from the absence of implication, and negation as accident is invalid because it would imply self-negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When in your own reasoning about your faith have you mistakenly assumed that because something is not part of a thing's essence, it is merely accidental—leading you to negate a reality that God has affirmed? Will you repent from such arrogant logical leaps?
?
The passage warns against self-negation. Have you ever, in an attempt to be 'objective' about your own faults, negated yourself—declaring that you are not truly the person who committed that sin? How does this lesson call you to honest self-accounting?
?
How often do you treat a part of a person (including yourself) as if it were the whole, reducing a complex soul to a single attribute? In light of the distinction between mental part and whole, how will you restore the proper order of seeing others as whole beings created by Allah?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Essence as Such: Neither Existent nor Non-Existent
Al-Jurjani explains the objection about the two modified propositions and the answer 'neither this nor that'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essence in itself cannot be described as existent or non-existent, and why the question is not exhaustive.

Core Idea
The essence in itself does not necessitate any attribute—not even existence or non-existence.

Al-Jurjani addresses an objection: if someone asks whether the essence is existent or non-existent, can we answer? He explains that the question is not exhaustive, so we are not obliged to answer. However, if we answer gratuitously, we say 'neither this nor that,' meaning the essence in itself is free from both. This lesson establishes the principle that the essence in its bare reality is prior to any predicates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: objection, then answer, then justification.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The objection asks: 'Is it (existent) or not (non-existent)?' The answer is 'Neither this nor that.' Understand that this is a gratuitous answer, not required.
The answer is not a direct response to a forced choice.
3
Understand why the question is not exhaustive
The passage says the question is 'not exhaustive' (ghayr hasir) because the essence in itself may not necessitate either alternative. Contrast with the two contradictories (naqidayn) which are exhaustive.
Existence and non-existence are opposites but not exhaustive for the bare essence.
4
Reflect on the meaning of 'necessitate'
The passage says the essence is 'not necessitative of any of the opposites' (laysat muqtadiyatan li shay' min al-mutaqabilat). Consider what it means for an essence to necessitate something—here it means the essence as such does not imply any attribute.
This is the core doctrinal point.
5
Address the mistaken view of the author of al-Maqasid
The passage rejects the claim that the essence necessitates its concomitants. This mistake is refuted because the essence in itself is prior to all attributes.
This distinction guards against attributing properties to the essence in itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سألنا عن المعدولتين) أراد الموجبتين المعدولة والمحصلة على سبيل التغليب ( فقيل : أهي (۱) ، أولا (ا) لم يلزمنا الجواب ) عن هذا السؤال؛ لأنه غير ،حاصر، بخلاف طرفي النقيض؛ إذ لا مخرج عنهما . ( وإن قلنا ) : أي وإن أجبنا عن هذا السؤال تبرعاً قلنا : لا هذا ولا ذاك) بالمعنى الذي عرفته؛ قوله : (وهو حق لما عرفت من أنها ليست مقتضية لشيء من المتقابلات، وما ذكره صاحب المقاصد من أن الماهية من حيث هي مقتضية للوازمها، فقد عرفت فساده . قوله : ( فالمتبادر منها الإيجاب (العدولي) أراد بالإيجاب العدولي : الإيجاب الذي يكون السلب جزءاً من المحمول، وتعبير المصنف بلا لإظهار الجزئية، وذلك لأن الجواب قضية سالبة المحمول ; لما عرفت أن السؤال بطرفي النقيض، فلا يرد أن ليس موضوعة لسلب النسبة، فكيف يكون الإيجاب عدولياً ؟ وما قيل من أن الجواب على تقدير التقديم إذا كانت موجبة سالبة المحمول يكون معناه بعينه معنى السالبة البسيطة لما تقرر من أنهما متلازمان، فيكون كلا الجوابين صحيحاً بلا فرق فليس بشيء; لأن تلازمهما باعتبار عدم اقتضاء وجود الموضوع لا يقتضي أن لا يكون بينهما فرق بأن يكون معنى إحداهما الاتصاف بالسلب، ومعنى الأخرى سلب الاتصاف . قوله : (لم يلزمنا الجواب عن هذا السؤال لأن جوابه بالتعيين والتعيين إنما يلزم إذا كان الترديد حاصراً ، ولا حصر لجواز أن لا يقتضي شيئاً منهما . قوله : ( لا يقتضي إلخ) ظاهر تفريع قوله : فإذا سئلنا إلخ على ما سبق يقتضي أن يقال: هاهنا معناه أن ليس نفسها ولا داخلا فيها. ويمكن أن يقال مراد المصنف بالاقتضاء الاقتضاء بالعينية أو الجزئية لا مطلقله بقرينة قوله سابقاً لازماً لها ومفارقاً إذ لا يصح نفي مطلق اقتضاء اللواحق اللازمة للماهية ضرورة تحقق اقتضاء الفردية للثلاثة مثلا فحينئذ يتلاءم سابق الكلام ولاحقه، ويندفع ما ذكره في شرح المقاصد من أنه إذا أريد بتقديم الحيثية أن ذلك العارض من مقتضيات الماهية صح في مثل قولنا الأربعة من حيث هي زوج إذ ليست بفرد دون قولنا : الإنسان من حيث هو ضاحك؛ إذ ليس بضاحك، فما ذكر في المواقف من أن تقديم الحيثية على السلب معناه اقتضاء السلب وهو باطل ليس على إطلاقه لا يقال : الاقتضاء بالعينية لا له ; لأن الاقتضاء نسبة تقتضى المغايرة; لأنا نقول : المغايرة الاعتبارية كافية فهي متحققة . قوله : (قلنا لا هذا ولا (ذاك فإن قلت : إذا كان معنى هذا الجواب أن الماهية من حيث هي لا هذا ولا ذاك، كان قولاً بأنها تقتضي عدمها لتقدم الحيثية، وقد مر أنه باطل، فإن كان معناه أن الماهية ليست من حيث هي هذا ولا ذاك لم يطابق السؤال، لأن السؤال عن المعدول المرتب على الحيثية، فلا يطابقه الجواب بالسلب الداخل على الحيثية قلت : نختار الشيء الثاني، ولا نسلم عدم المطابقة، وإنما لم يطابق لو كان المقصود تعيين أحدهما، أما لو كان في زعمه ثبوت أحدهما فلا فإن السائل إنما رتب المعدول على الحيثية بناء على زعمه ذلك، والمجيب نبه بإدخال حرف السلب على الحيثية على خطأ ذلك الزعم ، فليفهم .
And if we are asked about the two modified [propositions] — meaning the affirmative modified and the affirmative unmodified, by way of predominance — and it is said: 'Is it (1) or not (a)?' It is not necessary for us to answer this question, because it is not exhaustive, unlike the two contradictories, for there is no way out of them. And if we say — i.e., if we answer this question gratuitously — we say: 'Neither this nor that,' according to the meaning you have understood. His statement: 'It is true, because you have known that it is not necessitative of any of the opposites, and what the author of al-Maqasid mentioned that the essence insofar as it is itself is necessitative of its concomitants—you have known its falsity.' His statement: 'So what is understood from it is the modified affirmation' — he means by modified affirmation the affirmation in which negation is part of the predicate, and the author's expression 'not' (la) is to show the partiality, and that is because the answer is a proposition with a negated predicate, because you have known that the question is by the two contradictories, so it is not objected that 'not' is not posited for negating the relation, so how can it be a modified affirmation? And what is said that the answer, on the assumption of priority, if it were an affirmative with a negated predicate, its meaning would be exactly the meaning of the simple negative, since it is established that they are two equivalent, so both answers would be correct with no difference — that is nothing, because their equivalence in terms of the non-necessitation of the existence of the subject does not require that there be no difference between them, in that the meaning of one is 'attribution with negation' and the meaning of the other is 'negation of attribution.' His statement: 'It is not necessary for us to answer this question, because its answer by specification—specification is only necessary when the disjunction is exhaustive, and there is no exhaustiveness because it is possible that it does not necessitate anything of them.' His statement: 'It does not necessitate, etc.' — the apparent meaning of his branching 'So if we are asked, etc.' on what precedes requires that it be said: Here its meaning is that it is neither its identical nor included in it. And it can be said that the author means by 'necessitation' (iqtida') necessitation by identity or partiality, not absolute necessitation, by the context of his previous statement 'a concomitant' and 'separating,' for it is not correct to deny absolute necessitation of necessary accidents of the essence, given the necessity of the necessitation of individuality for three, for example. Then the earlier and later speech harmonize, and what was mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid is repelled: that if it is intended by the priority of the 'insofar as' (haythiyya) that that accident is among the necessitations of the essence, it would be correct in such as our saying 'four, insofar as it is even, is not odd,' but not our saying 'man, insofar as he is laughing, is not laughing.' So what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif, that the priority of the 'insofar as' over negation means the necessitation of negation, and it is false, is not absolute. It is not said: 'Necessitation by identity is not for it (i.e., the negation), because necessitation is a relation that requires otherness'; for we say: 'Considerative otherness is sufficient, so it is realized.' His statement: 'We say: Neither this nor that.' For if you say: If the meaning of this answer is that the essence insofar as it is itself is neither this nor that, it would be a statement that it necessitates its own non-existence because of the priority of the 'insofar as,' and it has passed that this is false. If its meaning is that the essence is not, insofar as it is itself, this nor that, it would not match the question, because the question is about the modified [predicate] built on the 'insofar as,' so the answer with negation entering on the 'insofar as' does not match it. I say: We choose the second thing, and we do not concede the lack of matching; it would only lack matching if the purpose were to specify one of them, but if it is due to his assumption that one of them is established, then no. For the questioner only built the modified on the 'insofar as' based on his assumption of that, and the answerer, by inserting the particle of negation on the 'insofar as,' alerted to the error of that assumption. So understand.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by extensive commentary. The objection: if someone asks whether the essence (al-mahiyya) is existent or non-existent (the two 'modified' propositions), the answer is that it is not necessary to reply because the disjunction is not exhaustive—the essence in itself (min haythu hiya) does not necessitate either. However, if we answer gratuitously, we say 'neither this nor that', meaning the essence as such is not existent and not non-existent. The commentary then clarifies: this is true because the essence does not necessitate any of the opposites, contrary to the claim that it necessitates its concomitants. The commentator distinguishes between a modified affirmation (al-ijab al-'aduli, where negation is part of the predicate) and a simple negation, and defends the answer against the objection that it is equivalent to a simple negation. He explains that the two are distinct: one signifies 'attribution with negation' and the other 'negation of attribution.' The answer is not to be taken as a specification, but as a rejection of the question's assumption that the essence must be either one of the two. The commentary further discusses the meaning of 'necessitation' (iqtida') as identity or partiality, not absolute, and addresses the charge of circularity or mismatch between the answer and the question. Ultimately, the questioner's error is assuming that the essence in itself requires an attribute; the answerer inserts negation on the 'insofar as' to point out this error.

Key Terms
المعدولتين
الاقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the essence in itself must be either existent or non-existent, as these are contradictories.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the essence in itself (min haythu hiya) does not necessitate either attribute. The dichotomy is not exhaustive because the bare essence is prior to both existence and non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why is it not necessary to answer the question 'Is the essence existent or non-existent?' according to the passage?
A) Because the essence is always existent.
B) Because the question is not exhaustive; the essence in itself may not necessitate either.
C) Because the essence is non-existent in itself.
D) Because the question is about modified propositions which are invalid.

Key Takeaway
The essence in itself (min haythu hiya) does not necessitate any of the opposites, so it cannot be forced into the dichotomy of existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you strip away all your roles, achievements, and even your physical existence, what is left of 'you'? Is that core self truly neutral, or do you feel a pull to define it by something? How does this lesson’s teaching that the essence itself necessitates neither existence nor non-existence challenge your everyday sense of self-worth?
?
Have you ever felt pressured to answer a binary question about your identity (e.g., 'Are you a success or failure?') when in reality you are neither? How might the answer 'neither this nor that' free you from that false dichotomy, and what would it mean for you to live without requiring such labels?
?
The passage refutes the idea that the essence necessitates its concomitants. Are there things you assume are necessarily true about yourself (e.g., 'I am anxious by nature') that might actually be accidental rather than essential? What would change if you saw them as not required by your essence?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
7 min
Modified Affirmation vs. Simple Negation
Al-Jurjani explains the distinction between modified affirmation and simple negation, and clarifies the answer's logical form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a modified affirmation (where negation is part of the predicate) and a simple negation, and why the answer 'neither this nor that' is not equivalent to a simple negation.

Core Idea
A modified affirmation (e.g., 'it is not existent') differs from a simple negation ('it is not the case that it is existent').

In this lesson, Al-Jurjani clarifies the logical form of the answer 'neither this nor that.' He explains that the answer is a modified affirmation (al-ijab al-aduli), where negation is part of the predicate, and distinguishes it from a simple negation. He also addresses the issue of the priority of 'insofar as' (haythiyya) and the meaning of necessitation (iqtida'). This technical discussion guards against misunderstandings about the nature of the essence and its attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note the discussion of al-ijab al-aduli (modified affirmation) and al-salib al-basit (simple negation).
2
Understand the definition of modified affirmation
Al-Jurjani defines modified affirmation as 'affirmation in which negation is part of the predicate.' The particle 'la' in the answer shows this. Contrast with simple negation, which negates the entire relation.
Example: 'The essence is not existent' vs. 'It is not the case that the essence is existent.'
3
Analyze the objection about equivalence
Someone might argue that the answer 'neither this nor that' is equivalent to a simple negation (salib basit), so both answers would be correct. Al-Jurjani rejects this because the two are semantically different: one is 'attribution with negation' (ittisaf bi al-salib) and the other is 'negation of attribution' (salib al-ittisaf).
This distinction matters for logical analysis.
4
Examine the discussion of 'necessitation' and 'insofar as'
The passage explains that if the answer meant 'the essence insofar as it is itself necessitates neither this nor that,' it would imply that the essence necessitates its own non-existence, which is false. The correct interpretation is that 'the essence is not, insofar as it is itself, this nor that'—i.e., the negation applies to the whole 'insofar as' clause.
This subtle point preserves the principle that the essence does not necessitate anything.
5
Reflect on the response to the charge of mismatch
Al-Jurjani responds that the answer does match the question because the questioner assumed the essence must be one of the two, and the answerer inserts negation over the 'insofar as' to correct that false assumption.
This shows the pedagogical purpose of the answer.

( وإن قلنا ) : أي وإن أجبنا عن هذا السؤال تبرعاً قلنا : لا هذا ولا ذاك) بالمعنى الذي عرفته؛ قوله : (وهو حق لما عرفت من أنها ليست مقتضية لشيء من المتقابلات، وما ذكره صاحب المقاصد من أن الماهية من حيث هي مقتضية للوازمها، فقد عرفت فساده . قوله : ( فالمتبادر منها الإيجاب (العدولي) أراد بالإيجاب العدولي : الإيجاب الذي يكون السلب جزءاً من المحمول، وتعبير المصنف بلا لإظهار الجزئية، وذلك لأن الجواب قضية سالبة المحمول ; And if we are asked about the two modified [propositions] — meaning the affirmative modified and the affirmative unmodified, by way of predominance — and it is said: 'Is it (1) or not (a)?' It is not necessary for us to answer this question, because it is not exhaustive, unlike the two contradictories, for there is no way out of them. And if we say — i.e., if we answer this question gratuitously — we say: 'Neither this nor that,' according to the meaning you have understood. His statement: 'It is true, because you have known that it is not necessitative of any of the opposites, and what the author of al-Maqasid mentioned that the essence insofar as it is itself is necessitative of its concomitants—you have known its falsity.' His statement: 'So what is understood from it is the modified affirmation' — he means by modified affirmation the affirmation in which negation is part of the predicate, and the author's expression 'not' (la) is to show the partiality, and that is because the answer is a proposition with a negated predicate, because you have known that the question is by the two contradictories, so it is not objected that 'not' is not posited for negating the relation, so how can it be a modified affirmation? And what is said that the answer, on the assumption of priority, if it were an affirmative with a negated predicate, its meaning would be exactly the meaning of the simple negative, since it is established that they are two equivalent, so both answers would be correct with no difference — that is nothing, because their equivalence in terms of the non-necessitation of the existence of the subject does not require that there be no difference between them, in that the meaning of one is 'attribution with negation' and the meaning of the other is 'negation of attribution.' His statement: 'It does not necessitate, etc.' — the apparent meaning of his branching 'So if we are asked, etc.' on what precedes requires that it be said: Here its meaning is that it is neither its identical nor included in it. And it can be said that the author means by 'necessitation' (iqtida') necessitation by identity or partiality, not absolute necessitation, by the context of his previous statement 'a concomitant' and 'separating,' for it is not correct to deny absolute necessitation of necessary accidents of the essence, given the necessity of the necessitation of individuality for three, for example. Then the earlier and later speech harmonize, and what was mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid is repelled: that if it is intended by the priority of the 'insofar as' (haythiyya) that that accident is among the necessitations of the essence, it would be correct in such as our saying 'four, insofar as it is even, is not odd,' but not our saying 'man, insofar as he is laughing, is not laughing.' So what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif, that the priority of the 'insofar as' over negation means the necessitation of negation, and it is false, is not absolute. It is not said: 'Necessitation by identity is not for it (i.e., the negation), because necessitation is a relation that requires otherness'; for we say: 'Considerative otherness is sufficient, so it is realized.' His statement: 'We say: Neither this nor that.' For if you say: If the meaning of this answer is that the essence insofar as it is itself is neither this nor that, it would be a statement that it necessitates its own non-existence because of the priority of the 'insofar as,' and it has passed that this is false. If its meaning is that the essence is not, insofar as it is itself, this nor that, it would not match the question, because the question is about the modified [predicate] built on the 'insofar as,' so the answer with negation entering on the 'insofar as' does not match it. I say: We choose the second thing, and we do not concede the lack of matching; it would only lack matching if the purpose were to specify one of them, but if it is due to his assumption that one of them is established, then no. For the questioner only built the modified on the 'insofar as' based on his assumption of that, and the answerer, by inserting the particle of negation on the 'insofar as,' alerted to the error of that assumption. So understand.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by extensive commentary. The objection: if someone asks whether the essence (al-mahiyya) is existent or non-existent (the two 'modified' propositions), the answer is that it is not necessary to reply because the disjunction is not exhaustive—the essence in itself (min haythu hiya) does not necessitate either. However, if we answer gratuitously, we say 'neither this nor that', meaning the essence as such is not existent and not non-existent. The commentary then clarifies: this is true because the essence does not necessitate any of the opposites, contrary to the claim that it necessitates its concomitants. The commentator distinguishes between a modified affirmation (al-ijab al-'aduli, where negation is part of the predicate) and a simple negation, and defends the answer against the objection that it is equivalent to a simple negation. He explains that the two are distinct: one signifies 'attribution with negation' and the other 'negation of attribution.' The answer is not to be taken as a specification, but as a rejection of the question's assumption that the essence must be either one of the two. The commentary further discusses the meaning of 'necessitation' (iqtida') as identity or partiality, not absolute, and addresses the charge of circularity or mismatch between the answer and the question. Ultimately, the questioner's error is assuming that the essence in itself requires an attribute; the answerer inserts negation on the 'insofar as' to point out this error.

Key Terms
الإيجاب العدولي
السالب البسيط
الاقتضاء
الحيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the answer 'neither this nor that' is a simple negation of both attributes, equivalent to saying 'the essence is not existent and not non-existent' in a straightforward logical sense.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is a modified affirmation, where negation is part of the predicate. Moreover, the negation applies to the 'insofar as' clause, not simply to the attributes. This nuance prevents misunderstanding the nature of the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between a modified affirmation and a simple negation?
A) One uses the particle 'la' while the other uses 'laysa'.
B) One attributes negation to the subject; the other negates the attribution.
C) One is true and the other is false.
D) There is no difference; they are equivalent.

Key Takeaway
The answer 'neither this nor that' is a modified affirmation where negation is part of the predicate, and it is not equivalent to a simple negation; it corrects the false assumption that the essence must be either existent or non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you, like the questioner in this passage, assumed that a complex issue must fall into one of two simple categories (e.g., existent or non-existent), only to have that assumption corrected? What judgment were you forced to reconsider, and how did you respond?
?
The answer 'neither this nor that' is not a dodge but a precise logical correction. In your own spiritual life, where do you habitually reduce questions about Allah's attributes or theological mysteries into false binaries? Commit to one such area and resolve to accept a 'third option' even if it feels uncertain.
?
Consider a belief you hold about yourself or the world that you treat as self-evident—perhaps something you think you must be or do. The passage shows that even logical equivalence does not mean identity. What would it mean for you to apply the discipline of 'saying neither this nor that' to a deeply held assumption, and what would that cost you?

Humanity in Itself is Neither One Nor Many
Al-Jurjani responds to the objection that humanity must be either one or many across individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani argues that the essence humanity in itself is not subject to the categories of unity or multiplicity, and how the concept of 'insofar as' (al-haythiyya) is used to avoid a dialectical trap.

Core Idea
The essence itself is bare; unity and multiplicity are accidental.

This passage presents a classic objection against the universality of human nature. The objector forces a dilemma: either humanity is the same in all individuals, leading to absurdity, or it differs, destroying universality. Al-Jurjani's answer is subtle: he refuses to accept either horn, because the essence in itself, stripped of all accidents, is neither one nor many. This lesson unpacks that response and the key concept of 'insofar as' (al-haythiyya).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the two horns of the dilemma: (1) If humanity is the same in Zayd and 'Amr, then one person would be in two places and have opposite attributes. (2) If it is different, then humanity is not a universal shared by individuals.
Notice the objector assumes that humanity must be either identical or different across individuals.
2
Understand the answer's strategy
Al-Jurjani's answer does not choose either horn. Instead, he says humanity in itself (min haythu hiya) is neither this nor that. The key move is to invoke the 'insofar as' qualification, which strips away all accidental attributes like unity, multiplicity, location.
The answer is a refusal to predicate any such attribute to the essence in itself.
3
Analyze the concept of 'accidents' (awarid)
Unity, difference, being-in-Zayd, being-in-'Amr are all accidents that attach to humanity when it is realized in particular existents. In itself, these are disregarded.
This aligns with the standard Ash'ari doctrine that the quiddity (mahiyya) is distinct from its existence and accidents.
4
Connect to broader themes
This answer parallels earlier discussions where the essence in itself is neither existent nor non-existent. It shows a pattern of negating attributes from the essence in the 'insofar as' perspective.
The commentary notes that this is not a third position; it is a refusal to apply the dichotomy in the first place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الإنسانية التي لزيد ( من حيث إنها إنسانية ( إن كانت هي التي لعمرو كان شخص واحد في آن واحد في (مكانين ومتصفاً بالأوصاف المتقابلة معاً ، ( وإن كانت غيرها، لم تكن الإنسانية أمراً واحداً مشتركاً بين أفراده ( قلنا : ( معنى هذا الكلام أن الإنسانية من حيث هي، إما واحدة مشتركة بين أفراده، وإما متعددة متغايرة فيها، وعلى كل تقدير يلزم محذور، فلا يلزمنا الجواب ; لأنها من حيث هي ليست شيئاً مما ذكر، فإن الحيثية المذكورة تقتضي قطع النظر عن جميع العوارض وإن أجبنا قلنا: (هي من حيث هي ليست التي في زيد ولا غيرها وليست التي في عمرو، ولا غيرها; لأن وحدتها وتغايرها وكونها في زيد أو عمرو كلها عوارض قطع النظر عنها في هذه الحيثية، ولو وقع بدل قوله : في زيد قولنا في عمرو لكان أظهر. (بل هما ) أي كون قوله : ( بالمعنى الذي عرفته أي الإنسانية من حيث هي لا تقتضي هذا ولا ذاك، وإنما ذلك بعد الاتصاف بالوجود. قوله : (فإن قيل إلخ) عطف على قوله : فإذا سئلنا، أورد الفاء; لأن التفريع الأول متعلق بقوله : فليست موجودة ولا معدومة، وهذا متعلق بقوله : ولا واحدة ولا كثيرة; لأن ماله كما ذكره الشارح قدس سره إلى قولنا الإنسانية من حيث هي إما واحدة أو كثيرة، وبين متعلقيهما ترتب في الذكر، فأورد التفريعين كذلك وليس هذا اعتراضاً على ما وهم; إذ لم يدع فيما سبق أن الإنسانية أمر واحد مشترك بين أفراده قوله : ( من حيث إنها إنسانية ) زاد الحيثية بقرينة الجواب . قوله : ( ولو وقع بدل قوله إلخ لأنه أوفق للسؤال المذكور، حيث ردد الإنسانية التي لزيا بين كونها هي الإنسانية التي لعمرو وبين كونها غيرها
For if it is said: The humanity that belongs to Zayd, insofar as it is humanity, if it is the same as that which belongs to 'Amr, then one person would be in two places at one time and qualified with opposite attributes together; and if it is other than it, then humanity would not be a single thing shared among its individuals. We say: The meaning of this statement is that humanity, insofar as it is itself, is either one shared among its individuals or multiple differing in them, and on either assumption a contradiction follows, so it is not necessary for us to answer, because it (humanity), insofar as it is itself, is nothing of what was mentioned, for the mentioned 'insofar as' requires disregarding all accidents. And if we answer, we say: It, insofar as it is itself, is not that which is in Zayd nor other than it, and it is not that which is in 'Amr nor other than it, because its unity, its difference, and its being in Zayd or 'Amr are all accidents that are disregarded in this 'insofar as.' And if instead of his saying 'in Zayd' we said 'in 'Amr,' it would be clearer. (Rather, both) meaning that... His statement: 'According to the meaning you have known' i.e., humanity, insofar as it is itself, does not necessitate this nor that; that only occurs after being qualified by existence. His statement: 'For if it is said, etc.' is coordinated with his statement 'So if we are asked,' he brought the 'fa' because the first branching is related to his statement 'So it is not existent nor non-existent,' and this is related to his statement 'Nor one nor many'; because its property, as the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned, up to our saying 'humanity, insofar as it is itself, is either one or many,' and between the two related matters there is order in mention, so he brought the two branchings accordingly, and this is not an objection to what is imagined; for he did not claim previously that humanity is a single thing shared among its individuals. His statement: 'Insofar as it is humanity' — he added the 'insofar as' (haythiyya) by the context of the answer. His statement: 'And if instead of his saying, etc.' because it is more fitting to the mentioned question, where he debated the humanity of Zayd between being the humanity of 'Amr and being other than it.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the universality of human nature. The objection argues that humanity either is the same in Zayd and 'Amr, which would lead to one person being in two places, or it is different, which would destroy its universality. The answer denies that we must accept either horn; humanity in itself (min haythu hiya) is neither one nor many, neither identical nor different across individuals. These are accidental attributes (awarid) that attach only when humanity is realized in particular existents. The commentary explains that the question is ill-posed because it assumes that humanity must be either one or many, but the essence as such is considered without any accidents. The commentator also clarifies that the answer 'neither this nor that' does not commit to a third position; rather, it is a refusal to predicate any such attribute to the essence in itself. The commentator elaborates on the logical structure, noting that this branching (tafri') corresponds to earlier points about existence and non-existence, and that the 'insofar as' is added to emphasize that we are considering the essence stripped of all concomitants.

Key Terms
الإنسانية
الحيثية
العوارض
الوحدة
التغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer 'neither one nor many' is a third position or a contradiction.
Correction: It is not a third metaphysical state; it is a refusal to predicate any such attribute to the essence in itself, because these attributes are accidental.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why is humanity in itself neither the same nor different between Zayd and 'Amr?
A) Because humanity is a universal that is instantiated in both.
B) Because unity and difference are accidental attributes that are disregarded when considering humanity in itself.
C) Because humanity in Zayd is identical to humanity in 'Amr by definition.
D) Because the question is invalid as it assumes humanity exists.

Key Takeaway
Humanity in itself, stripped of accidental attributes, is neither one nor many; it is the bare essence considered independently of all attachments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one attribute you deeply identify with—your nationality, career, or a personality trait. If that attribute were stripped away, what would remain? Would you still see yourself as valuable? How does this lesson challenge your attachment to that identity? Commit this week to seeing yourself and one other person purely as human, without labels.
?
When have you judged someone based on an accidental difference—like their beliefs or background—rather than seeing their bare humanity? What would it cost you to look past those differences today? Write down one action you will take to treat someone solely as a fellow human being.
?
This lesson says that even 'being in Zayd' is an accident to humanity in itself. How does that affect your understanding of your own individual identity? Do you see your 'self' as something fixed or as a bundle of accidents? Pause right now and ask: Who am I without my relationships, my body, my history? Let that question unsettle you until you seek an answer beyond attributes.

The Three Considerations of the Quiddity and the Mixed Quiddity's Existence
Defines three considerations of the quiddity and affirms mixed quiddity's external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways a quiddity can be considered relative to its accidents and why the mixed quiddity exists externally beyond doubt.

Core Idea
The quiddity is not divided; it is considered from three perspectives.

Sanusi now turns to a conceptual clarification that is essential for later arguments about existence, universals, and particulars. Before we can discuss whether quiddities are real or mental, we need a precise vocabulary for how a quiddity can be considered. This passage presents the three considerations and establishes that the mixed quiddity—the quiddity taken with the condition of existence—is unmistakably real in the external world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the threefold classification and the emphasis on the mixed quiddity's existence.
The passage begins with 'المقصد الثاني' marking a new topic.
2
Identify the three considerations
Find the three ways the quiddity is considered: restricted by existence (mixed), restricted by non-existence (abstracted), and unrestricted (absolute). Note the Arabic terms for each.
The mixed quiddity is also called 'بشرط شيء'.
3
Understand why the mixed quiddity exists
The passage asserts that the existence of individuals (the mixed quiddity) in the external world is 'بين لا سترة به'. Connect this to the idea that the mixed quiddity is the quiddity plus individuation.
4
Clarify the classification is not a real division
Read the commentary's explanation that the three are 'اعتبارات' (considerations), not a division of the quiddity into parts. This prevents a logical error.
The absolute quiddity is the 'مقسم' (thing divided), not a division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : الماهية إذا أخذت مع قيد زائد ] اعتبارات الماهية بالقياس إلى عوارضها التي ذكر حالها في المقصد الأول، وهي ثلاثة : تقييد الماهية بوجودها، وتقييدها بعدمها وإطلاقها بلا تقييد . فنقول : ( الماهية إذا أخذت مع قيد زائد ) عليها (تسمى مخلوطة، وبشرط شيء ووجودها ) في الخارج ) مما لا مرية (فيه فإن وجود الأشخاص في الخارج بين لا سترة به، وهي عبارة عن الماهية الكلية والتشخص، فالماهية المخلوطة موجودة قطعاً. قوله : ( في اعتبارات الماهية) يعني أنه ليس تقسيماً للماهية إلى الاقسام الثلاثة حتى يلزم تقسيم الشيء إلى نفسه وإلى غيره ، لأن الماهية المطلقة عين المقسم بل بيان اعتبارات الماهية بالقياس إلى العوارض وهو الظاهر من عبارة القوم. قوله : (تقييد الماهية فيه إشارة إلى أن المخلوطة والمجردة عبارتان عن الماهية المقيدة بوجود العوارض وبعدمها كما يدل عليه تسميتها بشرط شيء، وبشرط لا، لا عن الماهية مع العوارض ومع عدمها حتى يلزم بطلان الحصر بالماهية المقيدة بها وامتناع وجود المخلوط لأن اعتبارية، ولا عن الماهية المقارنة بها أو بعدمها حتى يلزم صدق المطلقة على المخلوطة
The second aim: The quiddity when taken with an additional condition. The considerations of the quiddity in relation to its accidents, whose state was mentioned in the first aim, are three: restricting the quiddity by its existence, restricting it by its non-existence, and leaving it unrestricted without restriction. So we say: The quiddity if taken with an additional condition upon it is called mixed, and with the condition of something, and its existence in the external world is beyond doubt, for the existence of individuals in the external world is manifest without concealment, and it is an expression of the universal quiddity and individuation, so the mixed quiddity exists definitely. His statement: 'Concerning the considerations of the quiddity' means that this is not a division of the quiddity into three sections such that it would entail dividing a thing into itself and other than itself, because the absolute quiddity is identical to the thing divided, but rather it is an explanation of the considerations of the quiddity in relation to the accidents, and that is apparent from the expression of the authorities. His statement: 'Restricting the quiddity' contains an indication that the mixed and the abstracted are expressions of the quiddity restricted by the existence of the accidents and by their non-existence, as indicated by their being called 'with the condition of something' and 'with the condition of nothing', not of the quiddity with the accidents and with their non-existence, which would necessitate the invalidity of the enumeration by the quiddity restricted by them and the impossibility of the existence of the mixed because it is conditional, nor of the quiddity concomitant with them or with their non-existence, which would necessitate the truth of the absolute upon the mixed.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of the three considerations of the quiddity and clarifies the nature of the mixed quiddity. It begins by stating that the quiddity can be considered in relation to its accidents in three ways: restricted by existence (i.e., the quiddity taken with the condition of being actualized in the external world), restricted by non-existence (taken with the condition of being devoid of existence), and unrestricted (taken without any condition). The passage then focuses on the first type: the mixed quiddity (مخلوطة), which is the quiddity taken with the additional condition of existence. It asserts that the existence of such a quiddity in the external world is beyond doubt, because the existence of individuals is manifest. The quiddity of an individual is a combination of the universal quiddity (e.g., humanity) and individuation (التشخص), so the mixed quiddity definitely exists externally. The commentary then addresses a potential misunderstanding: this threefold classification is not a division of the quiddity into different kinds, because that would require dividing a thing into itself and something else (since the absolute quiddity is the same as the thing divided). Rather, it is a consideration of the quiddity from different perspectives relative to its accidents. Furthermore, the commentator explains that the terms 'mixed' and 'abstracted' refer to the quiddity as restricted by the presence or absence of accidents, not to the quiddity accompanied by them or their absence. This distinction preserves the logical consistency of the classification.

Key Terms
الماهية
المخلوطة
المجردة
المطلقة
التشخص
العوارض
بشرط شيء
بشرط لا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three considerations are a real division of the quiddity into three distinct kinds or parts.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that this is not a division (تقسيم) of the quiddity, but a consideration (اعتبار) of it from different perspectives relative to accidents. The absolute quiddity is the thing divided (المقسم), not a division.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the mixed quiddity (المخلوطة) said to exist in the external world beyond doubt?
A) Because it is a universal concept
B) Because it is the quiddity taken with the condition of non-existence
C) Because the existence of individuals in the external world is manifest
D) Because it is the absolute quiddity

Key Takeaway
The quiddity can be considered in three ways—restricted by existence, restricted by non-existence, or unrestricted—and the mixed quiddity (restricted by existence) is evidently real in the external world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is not self-sufficient; it depends entirely on the One who gave it to you. This lesson says the mixed quiddity (your essence plus existence) exists externally without doubt. But do you live each day as if your existence is a gift held in His hands at every moment? What one thing would change today if you truly felt that your 'being here' is not automatic but sustained by Allah's will?
?
The quiddity can be considered without existence—an abstract possibility. But you are actually here. How often do you pause to thank the One who brought you from non-existence to existence? Write down one specific blessing of existence you have taken for granted, and commit to turning it into a praise (hamd) today.
?
The passage clarifies that the mixed quiddity is not the quiddity plus accidents as separate parts, but the quiddity restricted by existence. Think about your own life: Do you sometimes see your worldly attributes (wealth, health, status) as 'add-ons' to who you are, rather than as conditions in which your essence expresses itself? How would your gratitude change if you saw everything you have as part of the very condition of your existence from Allah?

The Abstracted Quiddity and External Non-Existence
Defines the abstracted quiddity and argues its non-existence externally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the abstracted quiddity and why it cannot exist in the external world.

Core Idea
The abstracted quiddity is defined by the absence of all accidents, so external existence would violate its definition.

This lesson begins by defining the abstracted quiddity (المجردة) as the quiddity taken with the condition of being free from all attachments. It then argues that such a quiddity cannot exist externally because external existence itself is an attachment that would contradict its abstraction.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage defining the abstracted quiddity and the phrase 'with the condition of nothing' (بشرط لا).
Notice the technical term 'بشرط لا' which is key to understanding the abstraction.
2
Understand the argument for non-existence
Follow the reductio ad absurdum: if it existed externally, external existence and determination would attach, making it not abstracted.
The contradiction (خلف) is the proof.
3
Reflect on the condition
Consider why the condition of being free from all attachments precludes any kind of existence in the external world.
This connects to the broader doctrine of mental existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وإذا أخذت ) الماهية (بشرط الخلو عن اللواحق سميت مجردة وبشرط لا شيء، وأنها لا توجد في الخارج، وإلا لحقها الوجود الخارجي (والتعين، فلم تكن مجردة عن جميع اللواحق كما فرضناه، هذا خلف (وهل توجد) المجردة ( في الذهن) عند القائل بالوجود الذهني؟ قيل: (لا توجد ؛ لأن وجودها في الذهن من العوارض) واللواحق، فلا تكون مجردة عن جميعها كالموجود الخارجي . وقيل : توجد لأن الذهن يمكنه تصور كل شيء حتى عدم نفسه، ولا حجر في التصورات ) أصلاً، ( فلا يمتنع أن
قوله : ( وفيه بحث إلخ ) يعني أن ما ذكر إنما يتم إذا كان التركيب منهما في الخارج، أما إذا كان في الذهن فلا .
قوله : ( وإنها لا توجد في الخارج وما قيل إنها لا تكون معدومة أيضاً، وإلا لحقها العدم فلا تكون موجودة ولا معدومة، فيلزم ارتفاع النقيضين واجتماعهما في الماهية المجردة، فليس بشيء، لأن المعتبر في المجردة الخلو بمعنى التقييد بعدم اللواحق كما مر فلا يمكن أن يعتبر فيه الخلو عن العدم؛ لأن التقييد بعدم العدم تقييد بوجود العوارض فتكون مخلوطة لا مجردة على أن ما ذكره يستلزم أن تكون ممتنعة الوجود لاستلزامها المحال وهو المطلوب. قوله : ( ولا) حجر في التصورات) أي لا نمانع في أنفسها، إنما التمانع فيها بعد اعتبار الحكم معها فكلها ثابتة في نفس الأمر كما مر تحقيقه في تعريف العلم .
And if the quiddity is taken with the condition of being free from accidents, it is called abstracted and with the condition of nothing, and that it does not exist in the external world, otherwise external existence and determination would attach to it, so it would not be abstracted from all accidents as we have assumed; this is a contradiction. And does the abstracted quiddity exist in the mind, according to the proponent of mental existence? It is said: It does not exist, because its existence in the mind is among the accidents and attachments, so it would not be abstracted from all of them like the externally existing thing. And it is said: It exists because the mind can conceive everything, even its own non-existence, and there is no restriction on conceptions at all, so it is not impossible that... His statement: 'And in it is a research, etc.' means that what was mentioned is only valid if the composition (of quiddity and individuation) is in the external world; if it is in the mind, then not. His statement: 'And that it does not exist in the external world, and what is said that it is also not non-existent, otherwise non-existence would attach to it, so it would be neither existent nor non-existent, entailing the elevation and conjunction of the two contradictories in the abstracted quiddity' is nothing, because what is considered in the abstracted is the freedom in the sense of restriction by the non-existence of attachments, as previously mentioned, so freedom from non-existence cannot be considered, because restriction by the non-existence of non-existence is a restriction by the existence of attachments, so it would be mixed, not abstracted. Moreover, what he mentioned necessitates that it be impossible in existence because it entails an absurdity, which is the desired conclusion. His statement: 'And there is no restriction on conceptions' means that there is no obstruction in themselves; the obstruction is only after considering judgment with them, so all of them are established in themselves, as has been verified in the definition of knowledge.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines the abstracted quiddity (المجردة) as the quiddity taken with the condition of being free from all accidental attachments. It is called 'with the condition of nothing' (بشرط لا). The passage then makes a key point: such a quiddity cannot exist in the external world, because if it did, external existence and individuation would attach to it, contradicting its abstraction. This would be a reductio ad absurdum. The passage then raises the question of whether the abstracted quiddity can exist in the mind, a debate among proponents of mental existence (الوجود الذهني). One view holds that it does not exist in the mind because mental existence is itself an accident, so it would not be abstracted from all accidents. The opposing view holds that it does exist in the mind because the mind can conceive anything, including its own non-existence, and there is no inherent obstacle to conceiving an abstracted quiddity. The commentary then addresses additional objections. First, it clarifies that the earlier point about composition (of quiddity and individuation) only holds if the composition is in the external world; if it is mental, the argument changes. Second, it refutes the claim that the abstracted quiddity is neither existent nor non-existent externally, which would entail a contradiction; it explains that the abstracted quiddity is defined as restricted by the non-existence of attachments, so it cannot be free from non-existence itself. Trying to make it free from non-existence would actually make it mixed. Finally, the commentator explains that the phrase 'no restriction on conceptions' means that the mind can conceive anything without contradiction, but the contradiction arises only when judgments are applied; the quiddity as conceived is stable in itself.

Key Terms
المجردة
بشرط لا
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the abstracted quiddity can exist externally as a pure form.
Correction: External existence itself is an accident that would attach to the quiddity, so it would no longer be abstracted.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that the abstracted quiddity cannot exist externally?
A) Because it would become non-existent
B) Because external existence is an accident that would attach to it
C) Because the mind cannot conceive it
D) Because it would be identical to its accidents

Key Takeaway
The abstracted quiddity is defined by being free from all accidents, and therefore cannot exist externally without contradicting its own definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs about yourself that you hold as 'pure' and untainted, yet when you examine your actions, you find contradictions? What would it take to honestly face that contradiction, as the passage does with the abstracted quiddity?
?
The abstracted quiddity cannot exist externally because existence adds something. What in your life have you tried to keep free from additions, but reality forced you to add? Will you accept the imperfection, or will you abandon the ideal?
?
This lesson forces a choice: either the abstracted quiddity is a mere mental abstraction, or it is impossible. Likewise, do you live in abstractions about your faith, or do you accept that real existence comes with conditions? Which do you choose today?

The Debate on Mental Existence of the Abstracted Quiddity
Presents the debate on the mental existence of the abstracted quiddity and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views on whether the abstracted quiddity can exist in the mind and the objections raised.

Core Idea
The mental existence of the abstracted quiddity is debated: some say it adds an accident, others argue the mind can conceive anything without restriction.

This lesson presents a key debate in Islamic philosophy: can the abstracted quiddity, defined as free from all accidents, exist in the mind? The passage presents two positions and then addresses objections, including the claim that the abstracted quiddity would be neither existent nor non-existent, and clarifies that mental conceptions have no inherent restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the two opinions
Read the passage starting from 'وهل توجد المجردة في الذهن' to see the two views on mental existence.
One view denies it because mental existence is an accident; the other affirms it.
2
Understand the objection about non-existence
The objection claims the abstracted quiddity would be neither existent nor non-existent externally. Follow the refutation that its definition restricts it to non-existence of attachments.
The refutation shows that trying to make it free from non-existence would mix it.
3
Clarify the restriction on conceptions
The commentary explains that 'no restriction on conceptions' means concepts themselves are unrestricted; restrictions come from judgments.
This aligns with the definition of knowledge mentioned earlier.

The Abstracted Essence and the Non-Existent: How the Mind Conceives
Defines the mind's conception of the abstracted essence and compares it to the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mind can conceive an essence stripped of all accidentals and how this relates to the concept of the absolutely non-existent.

Core Idea
The mind conceives abstract essences by stripping away accidentals, just as it conceives the non-existent.

This lesson delves into a key topic in Islamic philosophy and kalam: how the mind forms concepts of essences that are not instantiated in the external world. The passage explains that the mind can consider an essence bare of all attributes, even if in reality those attributes exist. It then draws an analogy with the absolutely non-existent, which is also conceived mentally. This discussion is foundational for understanding mental existence and the nature of concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style and parenthetical comments.
2
Identify the two main subjects
The passage discusses two things: the abstracted essence and the absolutely non-existent. Find how each is described and how they are compared.
3
Trace the argument for mental existence
Follow the reasoning that because we can make true judgments about the abstracted essence, it must have some form of mental existence. Note the refutation of the opposing view.
The commentator responds to al-Maqasid's objection.
4
Understand the analogy
The analogy is that just as the non-existent can be conceived and thus becomes a division of the existent, so too the abstracted essence becomes a fixed concept. Compare the two cases.
The difference lies in the impediment: absolute non-existence vs. abstraction.
5
Reflect on the final discussion
Consider the subtle point about whether conception for judgment requires the object to exist in the mind. The commentator suggests it is sufficient to have a mental image.
This touches on the theory of mental existence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعقل الذهن الماهية المجردة عن جميع اللواحق الخارجية والذهنية بأن يعتبر معراة عنها ويلاحظها كذلك، وإن كانت بحسب نفس الأمر متصفة ببعضها . ألا ترى أنه يمكنه الحكم على المجردة مطلقاً باستحالة الوجود في الخارج، ولا حكم على شيء إلا بعد تصوره ويقرب من هذا ما قيل من أن المعدوم مطلقاً أي خارجاً وذهناً قد يتصور، فيعرض له الوجود الذهني، فيكون قسماً من الموجود المطلق باعتبار وجوده في الذهن، وقسيماً له باعتبار ذاته ،ومفهومه فكذلك إذا تصورت المجردة مطلقاً كانت من حيث ذاتها ومفهومها مجردة ومقابلة للمخلوطة ومن حيث وجودها قوله : ( بأن يعتبرها معراة إلخ) ثم بعد اعتبارها كذلك تكون مفهوماً من المفهومات الثابتة نفس الأمر، فتكون الماهية المجردة بعد اعتبارها مفهوماً ثابتاً في نفس الأمر، كسائر الأمور الفرضية بعد اعتبارها ولذا تجري عليها الأحكام الصادقة، ولا أقل من كونها مفهومات اعتبارية، إنما الفرق بينها وبين سائر المفهومات الثابتة في نفس الأمر أنها ثابتة من قطع النظر عن الاعتبار، والفرضيات ثابتة بتوسط الاعتبار، فاندفع ما قاله صاحب المقاصد من أن اللازم مما ذكره هذا القائل وجود المجردة في الذهن وجوداً فرضياً غير مطابق لنفس الأمر، والكلام في وجودها في الذهن بحسب نفس الأمر، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن الكلام في وجودها في الذهن مع قطع النظر عن الاعتبار، لأن التقييد بعدم العوارض لا يكون إلا باعتبار الذهن قوله : ( ولا حكم على شيء إلخ ) وهذا الحكم صادق، فلا بد من وجود المجردة في الذهن بحسب نفس الأمر. قوله : ( ويقرب من (هذا لاشتراكهما في أن المقابلة والقسمية باعتبار الجهتين، وافتراقهما بأن المانع في المعدوم المطلق من الوجود في نفس الأمر العدم المطلق وها هنا التجرد . قوله : ( أن المعدوم مطلقاً) أي مفهومه وذاته المتصف بمفهومه فرضاً بقرينة قوله : باعتبار ذاته ومفهومه . قوله : (قد يتصور إلخ) أما مفهومه فينفسه، وأما ذاته فباعتبار هذا المفهوم. قوله : ( وقسيماً له إلخ) أما ذاته فباعتبار صدق مفهومه، وأما مفهومه فبنفسه. قوله : (كانت من حيث ذاتها ومفهومها (مجردة أما من حيث ذاتها فظاهر، وأما من حيث مفهومها، فلان مفهومها من حيث هو مقابل لمفهوم المخلوط، وإن كان من حيث إنه مفهوم لم يعتبر فيه التقييد بالعوارض ولا بعدمها فرداً من المطلقة. قوله : ( ولا حكم على شيء إلا بعد تصوره فيه بحث أشرنا إليه في بحث الوجود الذهني، أنه يكفي في التصور للحكم حصول المحكوم عليه إجمالاً بواسطة أمر عارض له، وهو المرتسم والموجود في الذهن حقيقة، فلا يلزم من الحكم على الماهية المجردة وتصورها لأجل ذلك الحكم وجودها في الذهن، كما يدل عليه سياق كلامه، فليتأمل. الذهن تكون قسماً من المخلوطة ومحكوماً عليها، وكذا الكلام في المجهول مطلقاً فإنه باعتبار حصوله في الذهن بحسب هذا الوصف العارض له قسم من المعلوم بوجه ما، ومن حيث اتصافه بهذا الوصف فرضاً قسيم له
The mind conceives the essence stripped of all external and mental accidentals by considering it bare and observing it thus, even if in reality it is qualified by some. Do you not see that it can judge on the abstracted essence absolutely the impossibility of existence in the external world, and there is no judgment on anything except after its conception. And close to this is what was said: the absolutely non-existent, i.e., externally and mentally, may be conceived, so mental existence occurs to it, so it becomes a division of absolute existent in respect of its existence in the mind, and its opposite in respect of its essence and concept. Similarly, when you conceive the abstracted essence absolutely, it is, in respect of its essence and concept, abstracted and opposite to the mixed, and in respect of its existence... His statement: (by considering it bare etc.) Then after considering it thus, it becomes a concept among the concepts fixed in reality, so the abstracted essence after considering it becomes a fixed concept in reality, like other hypothetical things after considering them, and therefore true judgments apply to it, and it is at least a conceptual concept. The difference between it and other fixed concepts in reality is that it is fixed without regard to consideration, while hypothetical things are fixed through consideration. So what the author of al-Maqasid said is refuted: that what follows from what this speaker mentioned is the existence of the abstracted in the mind as a hypothetical existence not corresponding to reality, while the discussion is about its existence in the mind according to reality, and it cannot be said that the discussion is about its existence in the mind without regard to consideration, because restriction to the absence of accidentals is only by mental consideration. His statement: (And there is no judgment on anything etc.) This judgment is true, so the existence of the abstracted in the mind according to reality is necessary. His statement: (And is close to this) due to their sharing that opposition and division are with respect to two aspects, and their difference is that the impediment in the absolutely non-existent from existence in reality is absolute non-existence, and here it is abstraction. His statement: (that the absolutely non-existent) i.e., its concept and essence qualified by its concept hypothetically, as indicated by his statement: with respect to its essence and concept. His statement: (may be conceived etc.) As for its concept, it is by itself; as for its essence, it is by virtue of this concept. His statement: (and its opposite etc.) As for its essence, by virtue of the truth of its concept; as for its concept, by itself. His statement: (it is, in respect of its essence and concept, abstracted) As for its essence, it is apparent; as for its concept, because its concept as such is opposite to the concept of the mixed, even if insofar as it is a concept, restriction to accidentals or their absence is not considered, it is an individual of the absolute. His statement: (And there is no judgment on anything except after its conception) contains a discussion we referred to in the discussion of mental existence: that for conception for judgment, it suffices that the judged item is obtained in summary through an accidental thing, which is the mental image and the existent in the mind in reality, so it does not follow from the judgment on the abstracted essence and its conception for that judgment that it exists in the mind, as indicated by the context of his speech, so reflect. The mind becomes a division of the mixed and judged upon, and similarly the absolutely unknown: for by virtue of its occurrence in the mind according to this accidental description, it is a division of the known in some respect, and by virtue of its being qualified by this description hypothetically, it is its opposite.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines how the mind conceives the abstracted essence and draws an analogy with the absolutely non-existent. The main text states that the mind can consider the essence stripped of all external and mental accidentals, even if in reality it has attributes. The commentator explains that after this consideration, the abstracted essence becomes a fixed concept in the mind, like other hypothetical concepts, and thus true judgments apply to it. This refutes the objection that such a concept is merely hypothetical and not real. The passage then draws a parallel with the absolutely non-existent: it can be conceived, acquiring mental existence, and thus can be considered a division of absolute existent in one respect and its opposite in another. The commentator clarifies that the opposition and division are by aspect. Finally, a discussion is added about the necessity of conception for judgment: it is argued that mere conception is sufficient, and the existence of the abstracted in the mind is not necessarily implied by the judgment upon it.

Key Terms
الماهية المجردة (al-mahiyyah al-mujarradah)
اللواحق الخارجية والذهنية (al-lawāḥiq al-khārijiyyah wa al-dhihniyyah)
المعدوم المطلق (al-ma'dūm al-muṭlaq)
الوجود الذهني (al-wujūd al-dhihnī)
المخلوطة (al-makhlūṭah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The abstracted essence cannot be conceived because it has no external existence.
Correction: The passage shows that the mind can conceive it by mental consideration, and it becomes a fixed concept in the mind, allowing true judgments.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the mind to conceive the abstracted essence?
A) It must exist externally.
B) It must be stripped of all accidentals by mental consideration.
C) It must be identical to the absolutely non-existent.
D) It must retain mental accidentals.

Key Takeaway
The mind can conceive abstract essences by stripping accidentals, and such concepts are real mental entities that allow judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the concept of 'justice' or 'mercy' in your mind, do you ever consider how you arrived at that abstract idea? Have you stripped away specific incidents or examples to grasp its pure meaning? How does this process of abstraction influence your understanding of Allah's attributes, which are absolutely real yet beyond your full comprehension?
?
The passage argues that the mind can conceive the non-existent and the abstracted essence, and that these concepts allow true judgments. Think of a belief you hold about the unseen (e.g., the afterlife, angels). Is it possible that you have formed a mental concept of it even though it has no physical existence? How does the reality of that concept affect your actions and choices today?
?
You often make judgments about things based on partial or incomplete conceptions. The lesson states that there is no judgment except after conception. Consider a time you judged someone or something hastily without fully conceiving it in your mind. What would change if you deliberately stripped away accidental impressions and focused on the essence? Will you commit to doing this before your next judgment?

Can Quiddity Be Free from All Accidents in the Mind?
Al-Sharif discusses whether quiddity can be free from all accidents in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why quiddity cannot be free from all accidents in the mind if mental existence is considered an accident, and how the commentator resolves this by distinguishing between accidents that the mind deliberately attaches and those that occur accidentally.

Core Idea
Mental existence is an accident, but not an obstacle to quiddity's freedom.

This passage tackles a subtle problem in metaphysics: can the essence of a thing (quiddity) be conceived in the mind without any accompanying accidents? The commentator engages with a claim that if we require freedom from both external and mental accidents, then quiddity cannot exist in the mind because mental existence itself is an accident. He resolves this by distinguishing between accidents that the mind deliberately attaches as conditions and those that occur accidentally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the different positions: the claim that quiddity cannot be free from all accidents, and the commentator's objection and resolution.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two conditions
Find where the passage mentions two conditions for quiddity's freedom: freedom from external accidents only (possible in the mind) vs freedom from both external and mental accidents (impossible because mental existence is an accident).
3
Understand the objection
The commentator objects that mental existence is not an accident of the concept of quiddity. Reflect on why this matters: it would allow quiddity to be free even with mental existence.
Think about the distinction between 'accident of the concept' and 'accident in reality'.
4
Grasp the resolution
The commentator resolves by distinguishing mental accidents that are deliberately considered (e.g., being a predicate) from accidental mental existence. Only the former prevent quiddity from being free.
This is the key insight.
