المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثاني : الماهية إذا أخذت مع قيد زائد في الذهن تكون قسماً من المخلوطة ومحكوماً عليها، وكذا الكلام في المجهول مطلقاً فإنه باعتبار حصوله في الذهن بحسب هذا الوصف العارض له قسم من المعلوم بوجه ما، ومن حيث اتصافه بهذا الوصف فرضاً قسيم له وقيل : إن شرط تجردها عن الأمور واللواحق الخارجية وجدت في الذهن بلا اشتباه وإن شرط تجردها مطلقاً أي من العوارض الخارجية والذهنية معاً فلا توجد فيه؛ لأن الوجود الذهني من العوارض كما مر. وفيه نظر، فإن كونه أي كون الشيء موجوداً في الذهن ليس من العوارض مفهومها، فلان مفهومها من حيث هو مقابل لمفهوم المخلوط، وإن كان من حيث إنه مفهوم لم يعتبر فيه التقييد بالعوارض ولا بعدمها فرداً من المطلقة. قوله: (وكذا الكلام في المجهول مطلقاً إلخ) أي في قولهم: كل مجهول مطلق يمتنع الحكم عليه بدليل أنه اكتفى في بيان جهتي المغايرة باعتبار ذاته ولم يقل: إنه باعتبار حصوله في الذهن قسم من المعلوم، ومن حيث ذاته ومفهومه قسيم له. ولذا غير الأسلوب ولم يقل: وإن المجهول مطلقاً. قوله: (عن الأمور واللواحق الخارجية أي التي تلحق الشيء في الخارج. قوله: (وجدت في الذهن وامتنع وجوده في الخارج؛ لأنه يستتبع اللواحق الخارجية سواء كان نفسه منها على ما قيل: إنه موجود في الخارج بنفسه، أو من اللواحق الذهنية على ما هو التحقيق من أن زيادته في التعقل. قوله: (من العوارض فلا تكون مجردة عن العوارض مطلقاً. قوله: (كما مر من أن الماهية في نفسها ليست بموجودة. قوله: (ليس من العوارض الذهنية فيه بحث: أما أولاً فلأنه سيصرح في المقصد السادس بأن العوارض الذهنية ما يعرض للشيء باعتبار وجوده في الذهن نحو الذاتية والعرضية والكلية والجزئية، وأما ثانياً فلأن القائل لم يصرح بكونه من العوارض الذهنية، بل بكونه من العوارض مطلقاً، وأما ثالثاً فلأن عدم كونه من العوارض الذهنية بالمعنى المذكور لا يضر في مقصود القائل؛ لأنه حينئذ يكون من العوارض الخارجية إذ لا واسطة فلا يمكن وجود المجردة في الذهن حينئذ أيضاً إن اشترط التجرد عن العوارض مطلقاً. لا يقال: حاصل الاعتراض أنه إذا لم يكن الوجود الذهني من العوارض الذهنية يكون من العوارض الخارجية، فلا يصح قوله: أن شرط التجرد عن اللواحق الخارجية وجدت في الذهن بلا شبهة لأنا نقول: ذلك على تقدير أن يراد من اللواحق الخارجية ما يلحق الشيء في الخارج بمعنى الأعيان لا ما يقابل فرض الفارض أعني نفس الأمر، والوجود الذهني من اللواحق الخارجية بمعنى ما يلحق الشيء في نفس الأمر. ما يقال في توجيهه مراده أن الوجود الذهني ليس من العوارض الذهنية التي تنافي وجود المجردة في الذهن، إذ هي ما يعتبره الذهن عارضاً لها ويلاحظ لها فإنه حينئذ تكون الماهية مخلوطة لا مجردة، والوجود الذهني ليس منها، لأنه لم يعتبر عروضه لها وإن كان عارضاً لها في الذهن. فمعنى قوله: وبعد وضوح الحق أنه بعد وضوح أن العروض المنافي لوجود المجردة ما ذكرنا لا تسمي ما يلحق الشيء في الذهن باللواحق الذهنية كما سيجيء والفاء في قوله: فلا نمنعك إما زائدة تشبيهاً للظرف بالشرط كما في قوله تعالى: {إذا جاء نصر الله} إلى قوله: {فسبح} [النصر: 1-3] الآية، أو جواب أما المقدرة كما في قوله تعالى: {وربك فكبر} [المدثر: 3] الآية. واعلم أن الواجب على الشارح في أمثال هذا المقام أن يبين مراد المصنف ويفصحه كل الإفصاح، فإن مجرد بيان أن العوارض الذهنية عبارة عما يعتبرها الذهن عارضاً له لا ما يعرض له في نفس الأمر، والوجود الذهني من قبيل الثاني دون الأول لا يكفي في توجيه الاعتراض كما لا يخفى بل اكتفاؤه على ذلك يفصح أن الاعتراض هو أن جعل الوجود الذهني من العوارض الذهنية ليس بصحيح، ولا يخفى أنه لا له لأن جعله من العوارض الذهنية بمعنى لا ينافي أن لا يكون من العوارض الذهنية بمعنى آخر. قوله: (إذ هي ما جعله الذهن قيداً فيه على ما ذكره المصنف لا تقابل بالذات بين الخارجية والذهنية من العوارض كما لا يخفى.
The second observation - the second aim: The quiddity when taken with an additional condition in the mind becomes a part of the mixed and is judged upon. Likewise the speech about the absolutely unknown: for it, by virtue of its occurrence in the mind according to this accidental description, is a part of the known in some respect, and by virtue of its being described with this description hypothetically is its counterpart. And it was said: 'If the condition of its being free from external matters and accidents, it is found in the mind without confusion; and if the condition of its being free absolutely, i.e., from external and mental accidents together, then it is not found in it because mental existence is among the accidents as has passed.' And in it there is consideration: for its being, i.e., the thing's being existent in the mind, is not among the accidents of its concept, because its concept as such is opposite to the concept of the mixed, even if from the perspective that it is a concept, the restriction by accidents or their absence has not been considered in it, it is an individual of the absolute. His statement: 'And likewise the speech about the absolutely unknown, etc.' i.e., in their statement: 'Every absolutely unknown is impossible to judge upon' with the proof that he sufficed in explaining the two aspects of difference by considering its essence and did not say: 'It is by virtue of its occurrence in the mind a part of the known, and by virtue of its essence and concept a counterpart to it.' Therefore he changed the style and did not say: 'And that the absolutely unknown...' His statement: 'About external matters and accidents' i.e., those which attach to the thing in the external. His statement: 'It is found in the mind and its existence in the external is impossible because it entails external accidents, whether it is itself from them according to what was said: it is existent in the external by itself, or from mental accidents according to the verification that its increase is in intellection.' His statement: 'From the accidents, so it cannot be free from accidents absolutely.' His statement: 'As has passed that the quiddity in itself is not existent.' His statement: 'It is not from the mental accidents' in it there is discussion: First, because he will explicitly state in the sixth aim that mental accidents are what attach to a thing by virtue of its existence in the mind, such as essentiality, accidentally, universality, and particularity. Second, because the speaker did not explicitly state that it is from the mental accidents, but rather that it is from accidents absolutely. Third, because its not being from the mental accidents in the mentioned sense does not harm the speaker's purpose; because then it would be from the external accidents since there is no intermediary, so the existence of the free [quiddity] in the mind would also be impossible then if freedom from accidents absolutely is stipulated. It cannot be said: 'The summary of the objection is that if mental existence is not from mental accidents, it is from external accidents, so his statement that the condition of freedom from external accidents is found in the mind without doubt is not valid.' Because we say: That is on the assumption that by 'external accidents' is meant what attaches to the thing in the external in the sense of concrete entities, not what opposes the positer's assumption, i.e., the same actual state; and mental existence is from external accidents in the sense of what attaches to the thing in the same actual state. What is said in its interpretation is that his intention is that mental existence is not among the mental accidents that are incompatible with the existence of the free [quiddity] in the mind, for they are what the mind considers as an accident for it and observes for it; then the quiddity would be mixed, not free. And mental existence is not from them because its occurrence has not been considered as an accident for it even though it is an accident for it in the mind. So the meaning of his statement: 'And after the truth is clear' is that after it is clear that the occurrence incompatible with the existence of the free [quiddity] is what we mentioned, do not name what attaches to the thing in the mind as 'mental accidents' as will come. And the 'fa' in his statement 'fa-la naman'uka' (we do not forbid you) is either extra, likening the circumstance to a condition as in His statement: {When the help of Allah comes} up to His statement: {then glorify} [al-Nasr: 1-3], or the answer to the implied 'amma' as in His statement: {And your Lord, so magnify} [al-Muddaththir: 3]. Know that it is incumbent upon the commentator in such places to explain the intention of the author and make it fully clear. For mere explanation that mental accidents are expressions of what the mind considers as an accident for it, not what attaches to it in the same actual state, and mental existence is of the second kind not the first, is not sufficient in directing the objection, as is not hidden. Rather, his sufficing with that makes clear that the objection is that making mental existence from the mental accidents is not correct; and it is not hidden that it is not [correct] because making it from the mental accidents in one sense does not contradict that it is not from the mental accidents in another sense. His statement: 'For they are what the mind makes a condition in it' according to what the author mentioned, there is no essential opposition between the external and the mental from the accidents, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a discussion about the condition under which quiddity (the essence of a thing) can be considered free from accidents, both external and mental. The author begins by restating the second observation and second aim: quiddity, when taken with an additional condition in the mind, becomes part of the 'mixed' (al-makhluta) and is subject to judgment. Similarly, the 'absolutely unknown' is considered known in some respect due to its mental occurrence, yet in itself it is a counterpart. Then a statement is quoted: if the condition for quiddity's freedom from external matters and accidents is stipulated, it is found in the mind without confusion; but if freedom from both external and mental accidents is required, then it cannot be found in the mind because mental existence is itself an accident. The commentator then raises an objection: mental existence is not among the accidents of the concept of quiddity, because the concept of quiddity as such is opposite to the concept of the mixed. Even if considered as a concept, it is not restricted by the presence or absence of accidents, so it is an instance of the absolute quiddity. He then explains each part of the original text in detail. He clarifies that 'absolutely unknown' refers to a thing that cannot be judged because the author sufficed with explaining its essence without considering its mental occurrence. He defines external accidents as those attaching in the external world. He notes that if mental existence were free from external accidents, it would entail a contradiction. He then addresses the objection that mental existence is not a mental accident. He gives three points: first, the author will later define mental accidents as what attach to a thing by virtue of its mental existence, like essentiality; second, the speaker did not explicitly call it a mental accident, but simply an accident; third, even if it is not a mental accident in that sense, it becomes an external accident, so the quiddity cannot be free in the mind either. He then anticipates a counter-objection: if mental existence is not a mental accident, it is an external accident, so the condition of freedom from external accidents fails. The commentator responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'external accidents': those that attach in the external as concrete entities, and those that attach in the same actual state (nafs al-amr). Mental existence is of the second kind. He further explains that the intention of the speaker is that mental existence is not among the mental accidents that prevent freedom, because those mental accidents are what the mind deliberately considers as accidental predicates. Mental existence is an accident in reality but not mentally considered as such, so it does not make the quiddity mixed. He then discusses the syntax of 'fa-la naman'uka' and emphasizes that the commentator must fully clarify the author's intent. The mere distinction between mental accidents as mentally considered and as actual attachments is insufficient; the objection is that calling mental existence a mental accident is incorrect, but that does not preclude it from being a mental accident in another sense. He concludes that mental accidents are what the mind makes a condition, and there is no essential opposition between external and mental accidents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any accident in the mind, including mental existence itself, prevents quiddity from being considered free.
Correction: The correct view is that only those accidents that the mind deliberately considers as conditions (e.g., being a predicate) prevent freedom; mental existence as such does not, because it is not part of the concept of quiddity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the commentator object to the claim that quiddity cannot be free from all accidents in the mind?
A) Because mental existence is not an accident of the concept of quiddity.
B) Because mental existence is an external accident.
C) Because quiddity is always mixed in the mind.
D) Because the condition of freedom is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Quiddity can be free from all accidents in the mind if we do not consider mental existence as a condition; only accidents that the mind deliberately attaches as predicates prevent freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes, do you ever confuse your mental image of them with the reality? This lesson shows that the mind's existence of a concept doesn't make it 'mixed'—but do you treat your understanding as if it were absolute? In what specific area of your faith do you need to purify your mental conception from accidental additions? Will you commit to seeking knowledge that strips away your own mental conditions?
?
Consider a theological concept you thought you understood completely. Could it be that your understanding is 'mixed' with accidental mental conditions—like cultural assumptions or personal preferences? How can you distinguish between the absolute reality and the 'mixed' version in your mind? What will you do today to identify one such accidental addition?
?
The passage discusses quiddity free from accidents—but have you ever pondered how your own soul's essence might be veiled by accidental attachments? What accidents (like pride, fear, or worldly desires) are you allowing to condition your understanding of your own purpose? Are you willing to seek freedom from them, even if it means discomfort?

Plato's Abstract Eternal Individual
Plato states his doctrine of abstract eternal individual, followed by commentary explaining key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Plato's doctrine of the abstract eternal individual and how the commentary clarifies the key terms to avoid misinterpretation.

Core Idea
Plato's abstract individual is not the species itself but a timeless essence free from accidents.

This passage presents Plato's view that from every species there exists an abstract, eternal individual free from all accidents. The commentary then carefully defines each term, distinguishing the 'individual' from the absolute quiddity and explaining why it is not subject to corruption. Pay close attention to the commentary's refinements; they prevent common misunderstandings of Plato's doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the structure: Plato's statement followed by term-by-term commentary.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key claims
What does Plato assert? What is an abstract individual? Why is it eternal and uncorrupted?
The commentary expands on each phrase.
3
Examine the commentary's method
The commentator explains each phrase (قوله) and clarifies meaning. Focus on distinctions: individual vs. absolute quiddity, abstraction from accidents, etc.
The commentator also discusses the phrasing 'for it exists'.
4
Reflect on the misconception
The commentary warns against taking 'individual' as the absolute quiddity. Why would that be wrong?
The absolute quiddity is the species itself, not an individual.
5
Summarize the lesson
Articulate Plato's doctrine in your own words, incorporating the commentary's refinements.
Ensure you distinguish between species and individual.

قال أفلاطون : الماهية المجردة موجودة فإنه يوجد من كل نوع فرد مجرد عن جميع العوارض أزلي أبدي لا يتطرق إليه فساد أصلاً، قابل للمتقابلات. قوله : ( الماهية المجردة موجودة ) زاد الشارح قدس سره هذه العبارة ليظهر مناسبة ما هذا المقصد لما قبله وجعل ما هو المذكور في المتن دليلا على أنه قال به فقوله : فإنه يوجد بتقدير القول، أي فإنه قال يوجد أو تعليلاً للحكم بأنها موجودة، فمقول القول مجموع المعلل والتعليل، والاحتجاج المذكور على التعليل، لكن الوجه هو الأول؛ لأن التنصيص على وجود المجردة لم ينقل منه لا. قوله : (فرد) بهذا يعلم أنه لم يرد الماهية المطلقة؛ لأنها نفس النوع لا فرد منه. قوله : (مجرد) عن جميع العوارض سوى الوجود بقرينة قوله : قابل المتقابلات. قوله : ( لا يتطرق إليه فساد ) لأن الفساد من لواحق المادة، وقد فرض تجرده عن جميع العوارض.
Plato said: The abstract quiddity exists, for there exists from every species an individual abstracted from all accidents, eternal, perpetual, not subject to corruption at all, receptive to opposites. His statement: 'The abstract quiddity exists' – the commentator, may his secret be sanctified, added this phrase to show the suitability of this aim to what preceded it, and he made what is mentioned in the text a proof that he said it. So his statement 'for it exists' is with the assumption of saying, i.e., 'for he said it exists' or as a cause for the judgment that it exists, so the statement consists of the reason and the explanation, and the proof mentioned is for the explanation, but the first is correct because the explicit mention of the existence of the abstract was not transmitted from him. His statement: 'An individual' – by this it is known that he did not mean the absolute quiddity, because it is the species itself, not an individual of it. His statement: 'Abstracted' from all accidents except existence, by evidence of his statement 'receptive to opposites'. His statement: 'Not subject to corruption' because corruption is among the accidents of matter, and he has assumed it abstracted from all accidents.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and expository passage. The author first states Plato's doctrine: from every species there exists an abstract, eternal, perpetual individual receptive to opposites, free from all accidents and corruption. The commentary then explains each key term. The commentator notes that the phrase 'the abstract quiddity exists' was added by the commentator himself to connect this aim to the previous one, and he clarifies that Plato's statement 'for it exists' can be interpreted either as a quotation of Plato's saying or as a causal explanation; the preferred interpretation is the first, since Plato did not explicitly assert the existence of the abstract. The term 'individual' here does not refer to the absolute quiddity (which is the species itself) but to an individual abstracted from all accidents except existence, as indicated by Plato's own proof that man is receptive to opposites. The term 'abstracted' means free from all accidents except existence, and 'not subject to corruption' because corruption is proper to matter, and this individual is assumed free from all accidents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Plato's abstract quiddity is the absolute species essence itself.
Correction: Plato meant an individual of the species, not the species itself; the individual is abstracted from all accidents except existence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does 'individual' (فرد) in Plato's statement exclude?
A) The absolute quiddity (الماهية المطلقة)
B) The abstract quiddity (الماهية المجردة)
C) The material individual
D) The eternal

Key Takeaway
Plato posits an abstract, eternal individual from each species, free from accidents and corruption; the commentary clarifies that this individual is not the species itself and is abstracted from all accidents except existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Plato says there is an abstract individual from your species that is eternal and free from corruption. If that is true, then the 'you' that you know—your body, thoughts, and experiences—is not the whole story. Are you living as if you are merely a collection of changing accidents, or are you acknowledging that part of you which is timeless? What one attachment or habit, if you released it, would help you connect more with that eternal aspect?
?
The abstract individual is 'receptive to opposites'—it can receive both good and evil. Your soul has that capacity. Where is your soul leaning today? Toward what opposites (e.g., patience vs. anger, gratitude vs. complaint) are you currently open? What specific choice can you make in the next hour to cultivate the better opposite?
?
If the abstract individual is free from corruption, then the real you cannot be destroyed by death or loss. What fear in your life—of failure, rejection, or death—is rooted in identifying too strongly with the accidents of this world? What would you dare to do if you believed that your core self is eternal and untouchable?

Classification of Parts: Existential, Relational, and Mixed
Al-Jurjani classifies parts of quiddities into two divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main classifications of parts in quiddities: the first division into existential, relational, and mixed parts, and the second division into existential and privative parts, along with the distinction between real and mental quiddities.

Core Idea
Parts of quiddities are divided by existentiality, relationality, and privativity.

Al-Jurjani opens the discussion of parts of quiddities with a double classification. He first distinguishes between parts that are existential, relational, or a mixture of both, then introduces a second division based on whether parts include privative elements. He also clarifies that these divisions apply to quiddity in general, but if we consider only real quiddities, all parts must be existential.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once to get an overview. Identify the two divisions and the examples given.
Pay attention to the terms إضافي, ممتزج, and وجودي.
2
Understand the first division
The first division includes relational parts (like 'the nearer') and mixed parts (like 'the bed'). Note how the bed combines real wooden pieces with a relational arrangement.
The arrangement is not an independent existent but a relation.
3
Understand the second division
The second division introduces parts that are not entirely existential, like 'the eternal' which includes an existential (existent) and a privative (no beginning).
The author notes that purely privative parts are unintelligible.
4
Note the real vs. mental quiddity
The author states that these divisions apply to quiddity absolutely, but for real quiddities, parts are only existential.
This distinction is important for understanding the scope of the classification.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس : في تقسيم الأجزاء
إضافية نحو الأقرب ) فإن مفهومه مركب من القرب والزيادة فيه، وكلاهما إضافيان ( أو ممتزجة ) من الحقيقية والإضافية (نحو السرير) فإنه مركب من القطع الخشبية وهي موجودات ،حقيقية، ومن ترتيب مخصوص فيما بينها باعتباره يتحصل السرير، وأنه أمر نسبي لا يستقل بالمعقولية . ( والثاني) وهو ما لا تكون بأسرها وجودية (نحو القديم، فإنه موجود لا أول له فقد يتركب مفهومه من وجودي وعدمي، ولم يتعرض لما هو عدمي محض ؛ لأنه غير معقول، فإن العدمات لا تعقل إلا مضافة إلى الوجودات، فيكون المعنى الوجودي ملحوظاً هناك قطعاً . ( واعلم أن هذه الأقسام المذكورة في هذين التقسيمين إنما هي (في الماهية) على الإطلاق ، ( أعم من أن تكون ) ماهية ( حقيقية أو اعتبارية. وأما إذا اعتبرنا الماهية الحقيقية فلا تكون أجزاؤها إلا موجودة فتكون وجودية قطعاً، فلا يتأتى فيها
قوله : ( فإن مفهومه إلخ ) هذا على ما هو التحقيق من أن الذات المبهمة ليست داخلة في
مفهوم المشتق، وإنما يذكر في تفسير معناه لبيان النسبة المعتبرة في مفهومه . قوله : ( ولم يتعرض إلخ) أي لم يورد له مثالاً ، وقد مثل له صاحب المقاصد بسلب الوجود والعدم للإمكان .
قوله : ( فإن العدمات إلخ) أي تعدد العدم ليس بذاته بل بالإضافة إلى الملكات، فالمفهوم الوجودي وهو النسبة إلى الملكة ملحوظ في التركيب من العدمات . قوله حقيقة أو اعتبارية) أي متصفة بالوحدة في الخارج، أو متصفة بها في الاعتبار، كما صرح به الشارح قدس سره فيما بعد
قوله : ( فتكون وجودية (قطعاً لأن ما في مفهومه السلب يمتنع وجوده .
الروح عبارة عن النفس الناطقة المجردة، والبدن مادي فلا يحصل منهما مركب حقيقي، وقد يقال : لا بعد في ذلك كما يؤلف عن المادة الغير المادية والصورة الجسمية ولواحقها المادية جسم موجود مشار إليه والتحقيق أن الموجب لأخذ النفس مع البدن حكم الوحدة، وارتباط أحدهما بالآخر من حيث ينفعل كل منهما عن الآخر، فتأثر النفس عن البدن كالكيفيات النفسانية الحاصلة بسبب القوى الجسمانية غضبية كانت أو شهوانية وتأثر البدن عن النفس مثل أن يقشعر الجلد ويقف الشعر عند استشعار جانب الله تعالى والفكر في جبروته . قوله : ( غير معقول (إلخ فإن قلت : يجوز أن يعتبر الماهية من العدمات بأن تكون تلك العدمات أجزاء للماهية، وعدم معقولية تعلقها إلا مضافة إلى الوجودات لا يستلزم كون تلك الوجودات معتبرة في الماهية بالجزئية . قلت : تلك العدمات إما أن تعتبر من حيث إنها مضافة إلى الوجودات أم لا، فإن كان الثاني لم تتعدد، وإن كان الأول تدخل الإضافة قطعاً، كما أشار إليه الشارح في الحاشية الصغرى . وإن كان المضاف إليه خارجاً، وهي المرادة بالمعنى الوجودي لا المضاف إليه .
التقسيم الثاني باعتبار الوجودية ،والعدمية، ولا باعتبار الحقيقية والإضافية إذا لم تجعل الإضافات من الموجودات الخارجية والنسبة بينها أي بين أجزاء الماهية الحقيقية قد تمتنع على بعض الوجوه المذكورة في التقسيم الأول، كالعموم من وجه على المشهور وكالمساواة على ما قيل من امتناع تركب الماهية الحقيقية الواحدة وحدة حقيقية من أمرين متساويين .
The Second Observatory - The Fifth Objective: On the Division of Parts
(First type:) relational, like 'the nearer' (al-aqrab), for its concept is composed of nearness and increase in it, and both are relational. Or mixed from the real and the relational, like 'the bed' (al-sarir), for it is composed of wooden pieces which are real existents, and of a specific arrangement among them by which the bed is obtained, and it is a relative matter not independent in intelligibility. (The second type:) that whose parts are not entirely existential, like 'the eternal' (al-qadim), for it is an existent with no beginning; its concept may be composed of an existential and a non-existential part. And he did not mention what is purely non-existential because it is unintelligible; for non-existences are only conceived as added to existences, so the existential meaning is definitely taken into account there. Know that these divisions mentioned in these two divisions are only concerning the quiddity (al-mahiya) absolutely, whether it is a real or mental quiddity. But if we consider the real quiddity, its parts are only existent, so they are existential definitely, so it is not possible for it...
His saying: 'For its concept, etc.': This is according to the verification that the vague essence is not included in the concept of the derivative (al-ishtiqaq); rather it is mentioned in explaining its meaning to clarify the relation considered in its concept. His saying: 'And he did not mention, etc.': i.e., he did not give an example for it, and the author of al-Maqasid gave the example of the negation of existence and non-existence for possibility. His saying: 'For non-existences, etc.': i.e., the multiplicity of non-existence is not by itself but by addition to dispositions (al-malakat), so the existential meaning, which is the relation to the disposition, is taken into account in the composition from non-existences. His saying: 'Real or mental': i.e., characterized by unity in the external, or characterized by it in mental consideration, as the commentator (sharh) clarified later. His saying: 'So they are existential definitely': because what contains negation in its concept is impossible in existence.
The spirit (al-ruh) is the rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa al-mujarrada), and the body is material, so no real composite is obtained from them. It is said: There is no difficulty in that, just as a composite is composed from immaterial matter and corporeal form and its material accidents to produce an existent body that is pointed to. The verification is that the requirement for taking the soul with the body is the judgment of unity and the connection of one to the other in terms of each being affected by the other. Thus the soul's being affected by the body is like the psychological qualities resulting from bodily powers, whether irascible or appetitive; and the body's being affected by the soul is like the skin creeping and hair standing on end when sensing the majesty of Allah the Exalted and thinking of His omnipotence. His saying: 'Unintelligible, etc.': If you say: It is possible to consider the quiddity from non-existences, such that those non-existences are parts of the quiddity, and the unintelligibility of their attachment except as added to existences does not entail that those existences are considered in the quiddity as parts. I say: Those non-existences are either considered insofar as they are added to existences or not; if the second, they do not become multiple; if the first, the addition is definitely included, as the commentator indicated in the smaller gloss. And if the addendum is external, and it is intended by the existential meaning, not the addendum.
The second division: with respect to existence and non-existence, not with respect to the real and relational, when the relations are not made among external existents. And the relation between the parts of a real quiddity may be impossible in some ways mentioned in the first division, such as generality from one aspect according to the well-known view, and such as equality according to what is said about the impossibility of a single real quiddity's being composed of two coextensive things.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the types of parts (ajza') that constitute a quiddity (mahiya). The author first distinguishes two main divisions: (1) parts that are purely existential, relational, or mixed, and (2) parts that are not entirely existential. For the first division, examples include 'nearer' (relational) and 'bed' (mixed). For the second, 'eternal' includes a non-existential component (absence of beginning). The commentary then clarifies that these divisions apply to quiddity in general, whether real or mental, but if we consider only real quiddities, all parts must be existential. A later section addresses the soul-body composite, arguing that soul and body do not form a real composite because the soul is immaterial and the body material; their unity is in terms of mutual influence. The passage concludes with the second division, which distinguishes parts by existence/non-existence rather than real/relational, and notes that when relations are not external, certain relations between parts (like generality or equality) may be impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all parts of a quiddity must be existential.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that relational and privative parts are also possible in quiddities considered absolutely, but not in real quiddities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what example is given for a mixed part?
A) الأقرب (the nearer)
B) السرير (the bed)
C) القديم (the eternal)
D) الإنسانية (humanity)

Key Takeaway
Quiddities can be composed of existential, relational, and privative parts, but real quiddities have only existential parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's attribute of 'eternity' (qidam), do you consciously include the privative aspect (no beginning) in your understanding? How does this lesson urge you to refine your mental concept of Allah's attributes to avoid falling into sheer negation?
?
This lesson distinguishes between real and mental quiddities. Consider a concept you deeply cherish, like 'justice'. Is your concept of justice purely mental, or do you believe it has a real counterpart in creation? How should this awareness transform your pursuit of justice in your daily life?
?
Al-Jurjani states that purely privative parts are unintelligible. Reflect on a time you tried to conceive of absolute nothingness. Did you find it impossible? How does this limitation point to your dependence on existence and the Existential One (Allah)?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
5 min
The Soul-Body Composite and the Second Division Constraints
Al-Jurjani discusses the soul-body composite and constrains the second division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul and body do not form a real composite and how the second division of parts imposes constraints on possible relations between parts of a real quiddity.

Core Idea
Soul and body are united through mutual influence, not by forming a real composite.

After the classification of parts, Al-Jurjani addresses a potential counterexample: the composite of soul and body. He argues that the soul is immaterial and the body material, so they do not form a real composite. Their unity is based on mutual influence. He then returns to the second division, noting that when relations are not considered external, certain relations between parts become impossible, such as generality and equality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on soul and body
Read the section starting from 'الروح عبارة عن النفس الناطقة المجردة'. Understand the definition of spirit as the rational soul.
The spirit is immaterial, the body is material.
2
Understand the argument against real composite
The author argues that because the soul is immaterial and the body material, no real composite can arise. He contrasts with the possibility of a composite from immaterial matter and corporeal form.
The verification is that unity is through mutual influence.
3
Examine the mutual influence examples
The soul is affected by the body through psychological qualities, and the body is affected by the soul like skin creeping due to awe.
This shows their connection, not a real composite.
4
Read the final paragraph on second division
The last paragraph refines the second division: when relations are not external, certain relations between parts of a real quiddity are impossible, such as generality and equality.
This limits how parts can combine.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
The First Madhhab: Quiddities Are Not Made
al-Ījī states the first madhhab that quiddities are not made at all and offers a reductio proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first position on whether quiddities are made, and the proof that if they were made, they would not be themselves.

Core Idea
Quiddities cannot be made, because a thing's self-affirmation is necessary.

In this passage, al-Ījī presents the first of three views on whether quiddities are made. He argues that quiddities cannot be made at all, because if they were, they would cease to be themselves whenever the maker refrains. This leads to an absurdity, proving the premise false.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the Key Terms
Identify the terms: ماهية (quiddity), مجعولة (made), جعل (making), جاعل (maker). Understand their meanings in context.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the Proof Structure
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum. It assumes the opposite (that quiddities are made) and shows it leads to a contradiction: a thing would be not itself.
The impossibility of self-negation is key.
4
Reflect on Self-Affirmation
The argument concludes that 'the affirmation of a thing of itself is necessary.' This means a thing's being itself is not contingent on any external making.
This is the core insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : الماهيات هل هي مجعولة أم لا؟ (الماهيات) الممكنة هل هي مجعولة بجعل جاعل أم لا؟ ففيه مذاهب ثلاثة : الأول أنها غير مجعولة مطلقا، سواء كانت بسيطة أو مركبة؛ إذ لو كانت الإنسانية مثلاً بجعل جاعل لم تكن الإنسانية عند عدم جعل الجاعل إنسانية؛ لأن ما يكون أثراً للجعل يرتفع بارتفاعه قطعاً، وسلب الشيء عن نفسه محال بديهة. وحاصله : أنه لو كانت الماهيات في ذواتها مجعولة لارتفعت الماهيات بالمرة على تقدير ارتفاع الجعل، ولو كان كذلك لزم أن لا تكون الماهيات في حد ذواتها ماهيات، لكن التالي باطل؛ لأن ثبوت الشيء لنفسه ضروري.
The sixth topic: Are quiddities made or not? The possible quiddities: Are they made by a maker's making or not? There are three schools. The first: they are not made at all, whether simple or composite; for if, for example, humanity were by a maker's making, then humanity would not be humanity when the maker's making is absent, because whatever is an effect of making is removed definitively when it is removed, and negating a thing of itself is an obvious impossibility. And its summation: if quiddities in themselves were made, they would be entirely removed upon the supposition of removal of making, and if so, it would follow that quiddities in their essence would not be quiddities, but the consequent is false because the affirmation of a thing of itself is necessary.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents the first madhhab regarding whether quiddities are made. The author begins by stating the issue and listing three views. The first view holds that quiddities are not made at all, regardless of whether they are simple or composite. The proof is given: if humanity were made, then in the absence of the maker's making, it would not be humanity. This is because an effect of making is completely removed when the making is removed. But it is self-evidently impossible for a thing to be negated of itself. The commentator summarizes: if quiddities in themselves were made, they would be entirely removed when making is removed, so they would not be themselves. The consequent is false because a thing's self-affirmation is necessary. Thus the premise that quiddities are made is false.

Key Terms
ماهية
مجعولة
جعل
جاعل
موجودة
ممكنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that the maker could refrain from making, which may not be the case.
Correction: The proof does not assume the actual absence of making; it is a hypothetical reductio: if quiddities were made, then on the supposition that making is absent, they would not be themselves. This shows the impossibility of them being made.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise in the proof that quiddities are not made?
A) If quiddities were made, they would be contingent.
B) If quiddities were made, they would not be themselves when the maker refrains.
C) If quiddities were made, they would be eternal.
D) If quiddities were made, they would depend on matter.

Key Takeaway
The first madhhab holds that quiddities are not made because self-affirmation is necessary and cannot be the result of making.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that you cannot negate your own reality as a human being. In what areas of your life have you been trying to deny or escape from your God-given nature—through sin, self-deception, or pretending to be something you are not? What step will you take this week to stop negating your own humanity and accept the limits and responsibilities it entails?
?
If quiddities are not made, then the essence of who you are is not something you can craft or earn. How does this challenge your tendency to base your identity on your achievements, relationships, or failures? What one thing would you do differently today if you truly believed your identity is a gift, not a construction?
?
The proof relies on the impossibility of self-negation. Think of a time when you tried to 'uncreate' a part of your past or willed away a painful truth about yourself. In what way is this denial a form of trying to negate something that is necessarily true? What would it look like to embrace that truth as part of your reality before God, and let Him transform it?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
8 min
The Resolution: Both Denial and Affirmation Can Be Correct
Sanusi analyzes the dispute on maj'uliyya and resolves it by distinguishing two meanings of 'making'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the dispute over whether essences are made can be resolved by distinguishing two senses of 'making': making the essence what it is, and making it existent.

Core Idea
The dispute over essences being made is resolved by clarifying the meaning of 'making'.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a long-standing debate: are essences (mahiyat) created or not? He argues that the dispute arises from ambiguity. By carefully defining what 'making' (jarl) means, we can see that both the absolute denial and absolute affirmation are correct in their proper contexts. He also analyzes the view that composites are made while simples are not, showing when it holds and when it fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Focus on the structure: statement of the dispute, analysis of the two absolute positions, then the third view.
2
Identify the two senses of 'making'
Notice that the author distinguishes between jarl al-mahiyya tilka al-mahiyya (making the essence that essence) and jarl al-mahiyya mawjuda (making the essence existent). Both senses are used.
This distinction is key to resolving the dispute.
3
Understand the third view
The third view says composites are made but simples are not. The author shows that if 'making' means the two senses, the view fails; but if it means the composite's essence itself needs composition, it is correct.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the author reconciles all positions: absolute denial and affirmation are both correct if interpreted correctly, and the third view is also correct in a specific sense.
This shows that the dispute is not intractable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السادس : الماهيات هل هي مجعولة أم لا . ينبغي أن ينازع فيه ولا منافاة بين نفي المجعولية عن الماهيات بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه أولاً، وبين إثباتها لها بما بينا آنفا أنه الحق الذي لا يتوهم بطلانه. فالقول بنفي المجعولية مطلقاً، وبإثباتها مطلقاً كلاهما صحيح ، إذا حملا على ما صورناه . ومن ذهب إلى أن المركبات مجعولة دون البسائط، فإن أرادوا بالمجعولية أحد المعنيين فالفرق باطل، لأن المجعولية بمعنى جعل الماهية تلك الماهية منفية عنهما معاً، وبمعنى جعل الماهية موجودة ثابتة لهما معاً. وإن أرادوا كما هو الظاهر من كلامهم أن ماهية المركب في حد ذاتها مع قطع النظر عن وجودها محتاجة إلى ضم بعض أجزائها إلى بعض وهذا الاحتياج الذاتي لا يتصور في البسيط، فهو والمركب يتشاركان في ثبوت المجعولية بحسب الوجود والحاجة إلى التأثير، وفي نفي المجعولية بحسب الماهية ويتمايزان بأن المركب مجعول في حد ذاته، مع قطع النظر عن وجوده دون البسيط، كان هذا أيضاً صواباً بلا ريبة . قوله : (كلاهما صحيح إذا حملا على ما صورناه) . يعني أن النزاع لفظي، وأنت قد عرفت حال ما صوره والصواب ما صورناه في صدر المبحث من أن النزاع معنوي، والخلاف في أن الماهيات نفسها أثر الفاعل، وكون الماهية موجودة أمر انتزاعي محض أو أن الماهيات أنفسها ماهيات وتأثير الفاعل في اتصاف الماهية بالوجود، فالقائلون بعينية الوجود قائلون بالأول، والقائلون بزيادته يقولون بالثاني. وهذا ما ذكره المحقق الدواني في تصانيفه، وبينه بياناً شافياً، واختاره شارح حكمة العين في منهياته وأشار إليه الشارح قدس سره في حواشيها بقي شيء : أن مرتبة علمه تعالى مقدم على الجعل فالماهيات في مرتبة العلم متميزة متكثرة من غير تعلق الجعل بها، فكيف يقال : إن الماهيات في أنفسها أثر الجعل اللهم إلا أن يقال: إن ذلك التكثر والتعدد بسبب العلم، فتكون أنفسها مجعولة بالجعل العلمي، وإن لم تكون مجعولة بالجعل الخارجي، ونعم ما قاله المصنف إن هذه المسألة من المداحض . قوله (المركب أي الحقيقي وهو ما لا يكون تركيبه بحسب اعتبار المعتبر، وذلك يستلزم كونه موصوفا بالوحدة في الخارج أي مع قطع النظر عن اعتبار المعتبر، سواء كان تركيبه الأجزاء الخارجية أو من الأجزاء المحمولة عند من يرى أنها مغايرة للمركب ماهية . وهو : من ، قوله : ( كان هذا أيضاً صواباً بلا ريبة وأما قولهم : إن الإمكان لا يعرض للبسيط، فلم يريدوا به إمكانه بالقياس إلى وجوده لظهور بطلانه، إذ الكلام في الممكن دون الواجب والممتنع أيضاً، ولو صح نفي هذا الإمكان عن البسيط لانتفى عنه الوجوب والامتناع أيضاً؛ لأنهما نسبة كالإمكان ، بل أرادوا به حاجته في ذاته كما في المركب. وقد يقال : توجيه القول الثالث على ما ذكره فيه البعد الذي كان قد هرب عنه، إذ محصله أن الحاجة إلى الفاعل من لوازم ماهية المركب دون البسيط، فإنها بالنسبة إليه من لوازم الوجود دون الماهية ولك أن تقول البعد المهروب عنه
The second observation - the sixth section: Are essences made or not? It should be contested in it, and there is no contradiction between denying maj'uliyya from essences in the sense we mentioned first, and affirming it for them as we just explained that it is the truth whose falsehood cannot be imagined. So the statement denying maj'uliyya absolutely, and affirming it absolutely, both are correct if interpreted as we depicted. And whoever held that composites are made while simples are not, if they intended by maj'uliyya one of the two meanings, the distinction is invalid, because maj'uliyya in the sense of making the essence that essence is denied for both together, and in the sense of making the essence existent and established is affirmed for both together. And if they intended, as is apparent from their speech, that the essence of the composite in itself, regardless of its existence, is in need of joining some of its parts to others, and this essential need is not conceivable in the simple, then the simple and the composite share in the affirmation of maj'uliyya according to existence and the need for the effect, and in the denial of maj'uliyya according to essence, and they differ in that the composite is made in itself, regardless of its existence, unlike the simple; this is also correct without doubt. His saying: 'Both are correct if interpreted as we depicted,' means that the dispute is verbal, and you have known the state of what he depicted, and the correct is what we depicted at the beginning of the research: that the dispute is real, and the disagreement is whether essences themselves are the effect of the agent, and the existence of the essence is a pure conceptual matter, or that essences themselves are essences and the agent's effect is in the essence's being qualified by existence. So those who say existence is identical say the first, and those who say it is additional say the second. This is what al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani mentioned in his works, explained it sufficiently, and the commentator of Hikmat al-Ayn chose it in his marginalia, and the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) indicated it in his glosses. There remains a thing: the rank of His knowledge, exalted, is prior to making, so essences in the rank of knowledge are distinct and multiple without the attachment of making to them, so how can it be said that essences in themselves are the effect of making? Except if it is said that that multiplicity and diversity are due to knowledge, so they themselves are made by the making of knowledge, even if they are not made by external making. Excellent is what the author said: that this issue is slippery. His saying: (al-murakkab, i.e., the real one: it is that whose composition is not according to the consideration of the considerer, and that necessitates it being described by oneness in the outside, i.e., regardless of the consideration of the considerer, whether its composition is from external parts or from predicative parts according to those who see that they are different in essence from the composite.) And it is: from; his saying: (This is also correct without doubt. As for their saying: 'Imkan does not occur to the simple,' they did not intend by it its possibility in relation to its existence, because its falsehood is apparent, for the discussion is about the possible, not the necessary or the impossible, and if the negation of this imkan from the simple were valid, then necessity and impossibility would also be negated from it, because they are relations like imkan. Rather, they intended by it its need in itself, as in the composite. And it may be said: the direction of the third statement as mentioned has the distance from which he had fled, for its gist is that the need for the agent is among the requisites of the essence of the composite, not the simple, for in relation to the simple it is among the requisites of existence, not essence. And you may say the distance fled from.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a continuous explanatory discussion of the sixth maqsad (section) on whether essences (mahiyat) are made (maj'ula). The author begins by stating that the dispute should be contested, and there is no contradiction between denying and affirming maj'uliyya when properly interpreted. He then explains that both absolute denial and absolute affirmation are correct if understood in the sense he depicted. He then discusses the third view, which claims that composites (murakkabat) are made but simples (basa'it) are not. The author shows that if this view means one of the two senses of maj'uliyya, it fails; but if it means that the composite's essence itself needs composition, which simples don't, then it is correct. He then comments on a statement by the original author (al-Iji?), clarifying that the dispute is verbal in one interpretation but real in another, citing al-Dawani's position that it is real, concerning whether essences are the effect of the agent or whether the agent's effect is only in qualifying the essence with existence. He then raises an objection about divine knowledge being prior to making, and answers that the multiplicity in knowledge is due to a different kind of making (jarl 'ilmi). Finally, he discusses the definition of 'composite' (al-murakkab al-haqiqi) and the question of imkan (possibility) not applying to simples, explaining that the intended meaning is the need in itself, not possibility in relation to existence. The passage concludes with a note about the third statement's gist.

مجعولة
جعل
مركب
بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct understanding of the third view that composites are made while simples are not?
A) It is absolutely false because both are made in the same sense.
B) It is correct if understood as the composite's essence itself needing composition.
C) It is correct only if referring to external making.
D) It is a verbal disagreement with no real content.

Key Takeaway
Essences can be said to be 'made' in the sense of receiving existence, but not in the sense of being made what they are.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you—your own body, your talents, your circumstances—do you primarily see them as 'made' in the sense of their essence (what they are) or in the sense of their existence (that they are)? Which perspective leads you to greater gratitude to the Creator, and why?
?
Sanusi says that denying maj'uliyya 'absolutely' is correct in one sense, and affirming it 'absolutely' is correct in another. In your own life, are there areas where you absolutize a partial truth, failing to see the nuance that reconciles seemingly opposing views? How might this lesson help you approach theological disagreements with more precision?
?
If you truly believed that your very essence (your 'what-ness') is not made by anyone—that it is simply what it is—but your existence is a gift from Allah every moment, how would that change your humility and your reliance on Him? What would you stop taking credit for?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
10 min
The Deeper Real Dispute and the Objection from Divine Knowledge
Sanusi clarifies that the dispute is real and presents the objection from divine knowledge and its resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the real dispute is whether essences themselves are effects of the agent or only their existence, and how the objection from divine knowledge is answered by distinguishing between external making and making in knowledge.

Core Idea
The real dispute is whether essences themselves are effects of the agent or only their existence.

After resolving the verbal aspect, Sanusi clarifies that the dispute is actually real and meaningful. He explains the two positions: one holding that essences are identical to existence (so the agent affects the essence itself), and another holding that existence is additional (so the agent only qualifies the essence with existence). He then raises a powerful objection: if essences are distinct in God's knowledge prior to any making, how can they be said to be made? The answer lies in distinguishing between external making and a 'making in knowledge' (jarl 'ilmi). The passage also touches on the definition of composite and the meaning of imkan (possibility) for simples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that it begins with a comment on the previous author's statement.
This passage continues the discussion from the first lesson.
2
Identify the two real positions
Look for the distinction between those who say existence is identical to the essence and those who say it is additional. This is the core of the real dispute.
The author sides with those who say the dispute is real, not verbal.
3
Understand the objection from divine knowledge
Notice the objection: essences are distinct in God's knowledge before any making, so how can they be the effect of making? The author presents an answer: they are 'made' in knowledge (jarl 'ilmi) even if not externally.
This preserves the idea that essences are effects in a certain sense.
4
Reflect on the imkan discussion
The passage ends with a clarification about imkan (possibility) not applying to simples in the sense of need in existence, but rather in the sense of need in essence for composites.
This ties back to the earlier distinction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute over essences being made is purely verbal and has no real content.
Correction: The author insists that the dispute is real and meaningful, revolving around whether essences themselves are effects of the agent or only their existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the real point of disagreement in the dispute over essences being made?
A) Whether essences are created at all.
B) Whether essences themselves are the effect of the agent or only their existence.
C) Whether composites are made differently from simples.
D) Whether the dispute is verbal or real.

Key Takeaway
The real dispute is whether essences themselves are effects of the agent; the objection from divine knowledge is resolved by distinguishing external making from making in knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very essence was known to Allah before you existed, and even that essence is a product of His knowledge (jarl 'ilmi), what does that say about your independence? Do you ever act or think as if your existence is self-derived, forgetting that even your essence is contingent upon His knowledge and will? How will you remind yourself daily of this complete dependence?
?
The lesson distinguishes between essences being effects in knowledge versus externally. When you reflect on your own shortcomings, do you treat them as merely external failures, or do you recognize that even your inner essence and potential were crafted by Allah? How might this reshape your repentance and reliance on His grace?
?
Consider the idea that for composites like humans, need for the agent is intrinsic to their essence. Do you truly live as someone whose very being is needy at every moment, or do you slip into a sense of self-sufficiency? What practical step can you take today to acknowledge your constant need for your Creator?

Composite as Substance or Attribute
Classifies composite into substance/attribute with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of composite things into self-subsistent substances and attributes, and the conditions necessary for genuine unity.

Core Idea
Composites are either self-subsistent or dependent; their parts must inter-subsist for true unity.

This lesson introduces the seventh section of the second observation, which classifies composite things (murakkabāt) into two fundamental categories: those that are self-subsistent (dhat) and those that subsist through another (sifa). The author explains the necessary conditions for a composite to be genuinely one, gives examples, and briefly compares composites with simples. The passage also includes a pedagogical note about the order of teaching.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification statement
Start by reading the opening sentence where the author classifies all composites as either substance or attribute based on whether they are self-subsistent or subsist through another.
Notice the direct link between the type of subsistence and the category.
2
Understand the condition for self-subsistent composites
The author states that for a composite to be genuinely one, some of its parts must subsist through others. If not, the parts are independent and no true unity results.
This connects to a later section (ninth) about the necessity of need among parts.
3
Examine the definition and examples of self-subsistence
Self-subsistence is defined as not needing a locus (mahall) to support existence. Examples include a body composed of hyle and form, and a bed composed of wood and shape.
Contrast this with composites that subsist through another, which are accidents.
4
Consider the note about teaching order
The author remarks that logically this section should come after the ninth, but teaching convenience justifies its earlier placement. Reflect on why this order is pedagogically sound.
The author emphasizes that need among parts need not be by subsistence; it can be another kind of dependence.
5
Reflect on the comparison with the simple
The passage notes that simples are not restricted to the same categories as composites, because some simples need a locus but are not attributes (e.g., corporeal form). This shows the unique character of composites.
This nuance is important for avoiding confusion between composite and simple entities.

The second observation - the seventh section: The composite is either a substance (dhat) or an attribute (sifa): The composite is either a substance if it is self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsihi) or an attribute if it is subsistent through another (qa'im bi-ghayrihi). The first requires some of its parts to subsist through other parts of it; otherwise, i.e., if some of its parts do not subsist through others, each would be independent of the other, and from them a single essence with true unity would not be obtained, because it will come in the ninth section that there must be a need of some parts for others. On this basis, this section ought to be delayed after the ninth, although the need of some parts for others does not have to be by their subsistence in them, because it is possible that the need is in another way. And in the first (type), it is also necessary that some of its parts be self-subsistent; otherwise the composite would not be self-subsistent, contrary to what is assumed. Regarding his saying: 'if it is self-subsistent,' the meaning of self-subsistence is that it does not need a locus (mahall) that supports it in its existence, like the body composed of hyle and form, and like the bed on the assumption that it is composed of wood and shape. So the meaning of subsisting through another is that it needs that other. Thus the composite subsisting through another is nothing but an accident and attribute, since we have no composite substance (jawhar) that inheres in a locus. Therefore the composite is restricted to substance and attribute. As for the simple, it is not restricted to these two, because among them is what needs a locus to support it but is not an attribute, like the corporeal and specific forms (on the assumption that substance is not a genus). Indeed, the simple is restricted to what is self-subsistent and what subsists through another, as occurred in al-Tajrid. So reflect, for the investigators have been perplexed in this position. His saying: 'some of its parts subsist through others' means by 'other' what is other than the subsisting part, whether it is one or multiple, and that multiple may also need another or not, like the specific forms of the composite of elements, so it includes the composite of two or more parts. His saying: 'i.e., if some of its parts do not subsist through others' but rather each part exists independently, not inhering in the other, then each would be independent of the other in its existence, so the essence considered to be composed of them would not be described with true unity, i.e., fixed independently of the consideration of the considerer. Regarding his saying: 'so this section ought...' he only said 'ought' because it is permissible to build the issue on accepted principles explained elsewhere, but the right way of teaching requires advancing it if possible, as here, so that the student does not wait. His saying: 'although' is a summary of blocking the implication indicated by his saying 'otherwise each would be independent of the other', based on the fact that the negation of subsistence (which is more specific) does not entail the negation of need (which is more general). It is the statement that the dispute of the three schools is about maj'uliyya being among the requisites of the essence or one of the two existents, i.e., that the observed in the title of the research is this meaning, so its being among the requisites of the essence of the composite, not the simple, according to the third school, is not the distance fled from. So reflect. His saying: 'the composite is either a substance etc.' he specified the composite because of the frequency of research into it.

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanatory discussion of the seventh maqsad (section) on composite (murakkab) things. The author begins by stating that a composite is either a self-subsistent thing (dhat) if it is qa'im bi-nafsihi (subsists by itself), or an attribute (sifa) if it is qa'im bi-ghayrihi (subsists through another). He then explains the first type: some of its parts must subsist through others; otherwise each part would be independent and no true unity would result, referring to a point from the ninth section about the necessity of need among parts. He notes that logically this section should be placed after the ninth, but pedagogical reasons justify its earlier placement. He clarifies that the need among parts does not have to be by subsistence, but could be another kind of need. Also, in the first type, some parts must be self-subsistent to ensure the composite is self-subsistent. He then defines 'self-subsistent' as not needing a locus (mahall), giving examples like body from hyle and form, or a bed from wood and shape. Conversely, 'subsisting through another' implies needing that other. Therefore, a composite that subsists through another is nothing but an accident ('arad) or attribute, as there is no composite substance (jawhar) that inheres in a locus. Thus composites are restricted to substance and attribute, but simples are not so restricted because some simples need a locus but are not attributes, e.g., corporeal form. The passage then comments on specific phrases, explaining that 'some parts subsist through others' includes all other parts, regardless of number, and that if parts do not subsist through each other, no true unity exists. The author also discusses the implication that the section could be taught earlier despite logical order, and he clarifies a point about the dispute of the three schools (from the previous section) to show that the third school's view is not problematic. Finally, he notes that the author specifically mentions 'composite' due to the frequency of research into it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all composites must be self-subsistent and that simples are limited to the same categories as composites.
Correction: The passage clarifies that some composites subsist through another (accidents), and simples have a broader range, including entities that need a locus but are not attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'self-subsistent' (qa'im bi-nafsihi) for a composite?
A) It does not need any parts.
B) It does not need a locus (mahall) to support it in existence.
C) It is not composed of any parts.
D) It subsists through another composite.

Key Takeaway
Composites are either self-subsistent substances or dependent attributes; for genuine unity, their parts must be inter-dependent, unlike simples which have broader categorization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that for a composite to have true unity, its parts must inter-subsist—each part needs the other. When you examine your own faith and actions, are there parts that stand independent, not supporting others, causing disunity in your character? Which relationship between belief and practice in your life currently lacks that inter-dependence, and what will you do this week to reattach them?
?
The author discusses the proper order of teaching: some sections should logically follow others, but teaching convenience can advance them. In your own pursuit of knowledge, do you impatiently jump ahead without mastering the foundations? What fundamental principle of aqeedah are you neglecting, and how will you return to it to build a stronger frame?

Are you trying to be self-subsistent in areas where you should depend wholly on Him? Criterion for Composition of a Quiddity
The eighth objective defines the criterion for judging a quiddity to be composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the exact logical criterion for when a quiddity (essence) is considered composite, and why other ways of sharing and differing do not imply composition.

Core Idea
A quiddity is composite only when it shares an essential with another and differs in another essential.

This passage from the eighth objective presents a key principle in logic and metaphysics: how to determine whether a quiddity (essence) is simple or composite. The author sets a precise criterion and systematically eliminates other possibilities. Understanding this criterion is essential for later arguments about the nature of God and His attributes, especially in discussions about composition and simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
First, read the Arabic text and the English translation to get an overview of the argument. Notice the structure: the author states his criterion, then gives an exclusion, and then provides examples and an exception.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main criterion
The key claim is that a quiddity is judged composite only when it shares an essential (dhātī) with another quiddity and differs in another essential. Understand why this is necessary: what is common and what is distinct must both be part of the quiddity.
3
Understand the excluded cases
The author explicitly excludes cases where sharing is in an essential but differing is by an accident (positive or privative). He lists four possibilities and explains that only the first (differing in essential) indicates composition. The others do not.
The fourth possibility (sharing in accident and differing in accident) is omitted as obvious.
4
Examine the exception
Note the special case: if two things sharing an essential differ in the necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the quiddity, that does indicate composition. This is because the necessary concomitant depends on something other than the shared essential.
5
Reflect on the examples
The text gives examples: individuals of a simple species differ by individuations, yet the quiddity is simple; existence shares with existent quiddities in thubūt but differs by a privation, yet existence is not composite. These illustrate the criterion.

 يعم لكن التركيب ظاهراً من أول الأمر بلا احتياج إلى ملاحظة المخالفة في ذاتي ،آخر وأيضاً لـ يستقم حينئذ قوله : لا بأن يشتركا في ذاتي إلى آخره . قوله: (لجواز كونه تمام ماهيتهما الكلام في مشاركة الماهية للغير، فالغيران إما الماهيتان، فلا يتصور كون الذاتي تمام ماهيتهما ؛ إذ لا تتصور الغيرية حينئذ. اللهم إلا أن يراد ما الغير بحسب الاعتبار، وإما الفردان والفرد فمركب لا محالة ولك أن تمنع لزوم تركب الفرد عند المتكلمين، فإنهم قائلون بأن للواجب تعالى تشخصاً مغايراً لماهيته، وأن ذلك التشخص ليس بداخل في هويته تعالى، وإن سلم اللزوم قلنا : إنا نختار الثاني ونقول : المراد كون الماهية مركبة في ذاتها وحقيقتها فذات الأفراد وحقيقتها لا يدخل فيها التعينات. بقي أن الفرد ليس بماهية والكلام في الماهية وجوابه إن الضمير في قوله : إنها مشاركة لغيرها ونظائرها للماهية بمعنى ما به الشيء هو هي وهي أعم من الكلي والجزئي وإن كان المراد بالذاتي والعرضي ما هو كذلك بالنسبة إلى الماهية الكلية .

The second observation - the eighth objective: A quiddity is judged to be composed of parts only for the sake of another (in the essential) in the mentioned sense, because it is known necessarily that that by which there is sharing is other than that by which there is distinction, and since neither of them is external to it, it is composed of them. (Not that they share) i.e., the quiddity is judged to be composed by sharing with another in an essential and differing from it in another essential, not that they share in an essential and differ by a positive accident or a privative accident; because it is possible (that that essential, i.e., that which is not accidental, is the entire quiddity of both. Then it depends on knowledge of sharing with another in it and differing in another. And also his statement 'not that they share in an essential etc.' is not valid. His statement 'not that they share etc.' is an explanation of the negative part of the restriction indicated by 'only', and its conclusion is that sharing in an essential in the mentioned sense only, or differing in it, or sharing in the accident only, or differing in it, does not indicate composition or simplicity at all, and it is obvious. So possibilities remain: one: sharing in an essential and differing in another - this indicates composition; second: sharing in an essential and differing in an accident; third: sharing in an accident and differing in an essential; fourth: sharing in an accident and differing in another accident - none of them indicates composition. The author omitted the fourth due to its obviousness, so his statement 'not that they share' means that their sharing is known. His statement '(i.e., judges etc.)' indicates that his statement 'not that they share' is coordinate with what precedes according to meaning. His statement '(the entire quiddity of both)' - the pronoun refers back to the pronoun of 'they share', namely the quiddity and the other, so the meaning becomes 'the entire quiddity of the quiddity and the other'. So by the added quiddity is meant the logical meaning particular to the universal, by the context of the word 'entire', and by what it is added to is meant that by which a thing is what it is, including the individualizing aspect. Thus the meaning reduces to the possibility of it being a specific nature for the two individuals, so his saying 'like individuals of the simple' is an example of two things sharing in the entire quiddity and differing by accident. This is on the assumption that individuation is external to the person. However, composition would be apparent from the beginning without need to observe difference in another essential, and also his statement 'not that they share in an essential etc.' would not be correct then. His statement '(because it is possible that it is the entire quiddity of both)' - the discussion is about the participation of the quiddity with another. So the two others are either the two quiddities, in which case the essential cannot be the entire quiddity of both, because otherness is not conceivable then, unless what is other is intended according to consideration; or the two individuals, and the individual is necessarily composite. But you may deny the necessity of the individual being composite according to the theologians, for they say that the Necessary Exalted has an individuation different from His quiddity, and that individuation is not included in His identity. And if the necessity is granted, we say: we choose the second and say: what is meant is that the quiddity is composite in its essence and reality, so the essence and reality of individuals do not include individuaions. It remains that the individual is not a quiddity, and the discussion is about the quiddity. Its answer is that the pronoun in His statement 'it participates with another' and similar expressions refers to the quiddity in the sense of 'that by which a thing is what it is', which is more general than the universal and particular, even if by essential and accident is meant what is so relative to the universal quiddity. ... (continues with examples) ... And know that when two things sharing an essential differ in the necessary concomitants of the quiddity, that indicates composition, because the mentioned necessary concomitant dependent on the quiddity does not depend on that by which there is sharing, otherwise it would be shared like it, but must depend on something else considered in the quiddity not shared, so composition follows. So this section is excepted from his statement: 'not that they share in an essential and differ by an accident or a privation'. As for sharing in a positive or privative accident and differing in another positive or privative accident, it is obvious that it does not necessitate composition at all.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the criterion for judging a quiddity (essence) to be composite. The author states that a quiddity is judged composite only when it shares in an essential (dhātī) attribute with another quiddity and differs in another essential. This is known necessarily: the basis of commonality is other than the basis of distinction, and since neither is external, the quiddity is composed of them. He then excludes the case where they share in an essential but differ by an accident (positive or privative), because it is possible that the shared essential constitutes the entire quiddity of both. The commentary elaborates on the different possibilities: sharing in an essential and differing in another essential (composition), sharing in essential and differing in accident (no composition), sharing in accident and differing in essential (no composition), sharing in accident and differing in another accident (no composition). The author omits the fourth as obvious. Then the commentary discusses examples: individuals of a simple specific nature differ by individuations, yet the quiddity is simple; existence shares with existent quiddities in thubūt (affirmation) but differs by a privative restriction, yet existence is not composite. Finally, a special case is noted: if two things sharing an essential differ in necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the quiddity, that does indicate composition, because the necessary concomitant cannot depend solely on the shared essential but must depend on another unshared element. This is an exception to the general rule that differing by an accident does not indicate composition.

Key Terms
ماهية
ذاتي
عرضي
تركيب
بساطة
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing an essential and differing by an accident indicates that the quiddity is composite.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that this does not indicate composition, because the shared essential may be the entire quiddity, and the difference is merely accidental.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth objective, what indicates that a quiddity is composite?
A) Sharing in an essential and differing in an accident.
B) Sharing in an essential and differing in another essential.
C) Sharing in an accident and differing in an accident.
D) Sharing in an accident and differing in an essential.

Key Takeaway
A quiddity is composite only when it shares an essential with another and differs in another essential; all other combinations (including differing by accident) do not necessitate composition, except when the difference is in necessary concomitants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own identity, what do you consider essential to who you are? How might you confuse accidental traits (like status or wealth) with your true essence (your soul and faith)? How does this lesson push you to reevaluate what really defines you?
?
The author argues that accidental differences do not make a quiddity composite. In the Muslim community, how often do we let accidental differences (race, culture) cause division, forgetting that we share the essential of faith? How can you actively work to see beyond the superficial?
?
Consider the exception: differing in necessary concomitants indicates composition. Your actions are necessary concomitants of your faith. If your actions differ from those of other believers, does that indicate a difference in your essential faith? How will you examine your own actions to ensure they truly reflect the essence of Islam?
