Sharing in Essence and Differing in Accidents Does Not Require Composition
Defining when sharing in quiddity with differences does not imply composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when sharing in an essential attribute with differences does or does not imply composition, including the exception for necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
Sharing in essence with accidental differences does not require composition; only differing in necessary concomitants does.

This passage from al-Iji discusses a subtle point in metaphysics: when two things share a core essence but differ in other respects, does that force them to be composite? Al-Iji clarifies that accidental differences do not imply composition, but differences in necessary consequences (lawazim) do. This distinction is crucial for understanding the simplicity of quiddities and existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic rule
Read the first part where al-Iji states that sharing in quiddity with accidental differences does not require composition. Note the examples of individuals of a simple nature and existence versus quiddities.
The example of existence shows that a negative condition (being only thubut) does not make it composite.
2
Identify the exception
Al-Iji then introduces an exception: if two things share an essential attribute but differ in necessary concomitants, that does indicate composition. Understand the reasoning: the necessary concomitant cannot stem from the shared attribute alone, so it must come from another part.
This exception is crucial because it shows that only certain kinds of differences force composition.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider how this applies to real cases. Accidental attributes (positive or negative) do not break simplicity, but differences in necessary properties do. Recognize that the distinction hinges on whether the difference stems from the essence itself or from an accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : لا بد من حاجة الأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض هي كأفراد البسيط الذي هو طبيعة نوعية فإن أفراده تختلف بالتعينات التي أمور عارضة مع أن الماهية واحدة لا تركيب فيها وكذلك الوجود يشارك الماهيات الموجودة في الثبوت ويمتاز عنها بقيد سلبي هو أنه ليس مفهومه إلا الثبوت فقط، وللماهيات أمر وراءه، وليس يلزم من ذلك تركب الوجود ولا بأن يختلفا في ذاتي مع الاشتراك في عارض ثبوتي أو سلب فإن هذا أيضاً لا يقتضي التركيب إذ البسيطان قد يستلزمان صفة ثبوتية أو سلبية ويتمايزان بتمام الحقيقة ولا تركيب في شيء منهما. واعلم أن المشتركين في ذاتي إذا اختلفا في لوازم الماهية دل ذلك على التركيب؛ لأن اللازم المذكور المستند إلى الماهية لا يستند إلى ما به الاشتراك وإلا كان مشتركاً مثله، بل لا بد أن يستند إلى شيء آخر معتبر في الماهية غير مشترك، فيلزم التركيب فهذا القسم مستثنى عن قوله : لا بأن يشتركا في ذاتي ويختلفا بعارض أو سلب وأما الاشتراك في عارض ثبوتي أو سلبي، والاختلاف في عارض آخر ثبوتي أو سلبي، فظاهر أنه لا يقتضي تركيباً أصلاً.
The second observation - the ninth intent: The parts must need each other. It is like the individuals of the simple [nature] which is a specific nature, for its individuals differ by individuations that are accidental matters, while the quiddity is one with no composition in it. Similarly, existence shares with existing quiddities in being established and is distinguished from them by a negative condition, which is that its concept is nothing but establishment only, while the quiddities have something beyond it. It does not follow from that that existence is composite. Nor [does composition follow] from their differing in an essential attribute while sharing in a positive or negative accident, for this also does not entail composition, since two simple things may necessitate a positive or negative attribute and differ in the entirety of the reality, and there is no composition in either of them. Know that two things that share in an essential attribute, if they differ in the necessary concomitants of the quiddity, that indicates composition; because the mentioned necessary concomitant that is based on the quiddity cannot be based on what they share, otherwise it would be shared like it, but rather it must be based on something else considered in the quiddity that is not shared, so composition follows. So this division is excepted from his saying: not that they share in an essential attribute and differ by an accident or negation. As for sharing in a positive or negative accident and differing in another positive or negative accident, it is obvious that it does not entail composition at all.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of cases where two things share in an essential attribute. It states that sharing in a quiddity while differing by accidental individuations does not require composition, using the example of individuals of a simple nature. It then gives the example of existence and existing quiddities: they share in the attribute of being established (thubut), but existence is distinguished by being only establishment, while quiddities have something else. This difference by a negative condition does not make existence composite. Similarly, if two things share an essential attribute but differ by a positive or negative accident, that does not force composition, since two simple things can have different properties while being simple. However, the passage introduces an exception: if two things share an essential attribute but differ in the necessary concomitants (lawazim) of the quiddity, that does indicate composition. The reasoning is that a necessary concomitant that stems from the quiddity cannot stem from the shared essential attribute (otherwise it would be shared), so it must stem from something else in the quiddity, implying composition. This exception is set apart from the previous rule. The passage then reiterates that sharing in an accidental attribute and differing in another accidental attribute clearly does not imply any composition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any difference between two things that share an essential attribute implies composition.
Correction: Only differences in necessary concomitants (lawazim) imply composition; differences in accidental attributes do not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does sharing in an essential attribute with a difference indicate composition?
A) When they differ by an accidental attribute
B) When they differ by a negative condition
C) When they differ in the necessary concomitants of the quiddity
D) When they differ by any attribute whatsoever

Key Takeaway
Sharing in essential attribute with accidental differences does not require composition; only differences in necessary concomitants do.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that accidental differences do not affect the quiddity. How can you use this truth to stop defining yourself by your failures and instead anchor your identity in your shared human nature and your faith?
?
This passage distinguishes between accidental differences and differences in necessary concomitants. In your understanding of Allah's oneness, have you ever unconsciously thought that His attributes (like power and mercy) are accidental additions that would make Him composite? How does the logic of this lesson—that only differences in necessary concomitants imply composition—help you affirm that His attributes are neither accidental nor separate additions?
?
The exception in the passage warns that differing in necessary concomitants of the quiddity indicates composition. Examine your own beliefs: Do you hold any necessary concomitant about Allah that might imply composition in your mind? What concrete step can you take today to purify your 'aqidah from such subtle anthropomorphic conceptions?

Part Interdependence for True Composition and Objection from Army and Electuary
States principle of part interdependence, objection from composite things, and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why true composition requires parts to need each other, and how the objection from the army and electuary is answered.

Core Idea
True composition requires parts to depend on each other; formal parts depend on material parts.

This passage from al-Mawaqif establishes a key principle: for something to be truly one composite reality, its parts must need each other. The author presents this as a self-evident judgment, then addresses an important objection from seemingly independent parts in an army or electuary. Understanding how he resolves this reveals a deeper framework of formal and material parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the principle
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Identify the main claim: parts must need each other for true composition.
The author calls this judgment self-evident (badīhī).
2
Understand the reasoning
The reasoning is that if each part were independent, they would not form a true unity; they would be like a stone next to a human—merely juxtaposed.
The example is for clarification, not proof.
3
Examine the objection and the answer
The objection cites the army (individuals independent) and electuary (simple drugs independent). The answer: these have a formal part (social form) that needs the material parts, preserving interdependence.
Think of the 'form' as the arrangement or structure that gives unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد التاسع : لا بد من حاجة الأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض لا بد في تركب الماهية الحقيقية من حاجة الأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض؛ إذ لو استغنى كل من الأجزاء عن الآخر، لم يحصل منهما ماهية واحدة وحدة حقيقية، كالحجر الموضوع بجنب الإنسانية قالوا هذا الحكم الكلي بديهي والتمثيل للتوضيح. وأورد العسكر فإنه مركب من الآحاد، مع استغناء كل منها عن الآخر، والمعجون فإنه مركب من المفردات مع أن كل مفرد منها مستغن عما عداه فانتقض ذلك الحكم الكلي. وأجيب عنه بأن الجزء الصوري فيهما، وهو الهيئة الاجتماعية العارضة للآحاد كلها وللمفردات بأسرها، محتاج إلى الجزء المادي.
The ninth intent: The parts must need each other. It is necessary in the composition of a true quiddity that the parts need each other; because if each of the parts were independent of the other, they would not constitute one quiddity and a true unity, like a stone placed next to humanity. They said this universal judgment is self-evident and the example is for clarification. And there was adduced the objection of the army, for it is composed of individuals, while each is independent of the other, and the electuary, for it is composed of simple drugs, while each simple is independent of what is other than it, so that universal judgment was contradicted. And it was answered about that by [saying] that the formal part in them, which is the social form accidental to all the individuals and to all the simples, needs the material part.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the principle that true composition requires interdependence of parts, followed by an objection and its answer. It begins by stating the principle: for a quiddity to be truly composite, its parts must need each other. The reasoning is that if each part is self-sufficient, they would not form a true unity; they would be like a stone placed next to a human (merely juxtaposed). The author notes that this universal judgment is self-evident and the example is only illustrative. Then an objection is raised: an army is composed of individuals, yet each individual is independent; similarly, an electuary (medicinal paste) is composed of simple drugs, each independent. These seem to be genuine composites that contradict the principle. The answer is that in both cases, there is a formal part—the social form (al-hay'a al-ijtimā'iyya) that inheres in all the individuals or simples—which is dependent on the material part (the individuals or simples themselves). Thus, true composition still involves interdependence between the formal and material parts, preserving the principle.

Key Terms
تركيب
ماهية حقيقية
جزء صوري
هيئة اجتماعية
جزء مادي
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Composites like an army, where parts are independent, disprove the necessity of interdependence.
Correction: They have a formal part (the social form) that depends on the material parts, so interdependence still holds.

Checkpoint

A) He denies that they are truly composite
B) He argues that the formal part (social form) depends on the material parts
C) He says the judgment only applies to natural not artificial composites
D) He says the army and electuary are not true unities

Key Takeaway
True composition requires part interdependence; apparent counterexamples like an army have a formal part that requires the material parts, preserving the principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you try to be self-sufficient when you are actually part of a community that requires interdependence? What would change if you acknowledged your need for others as the lesson describes true composition?
?
The formal part (social form) depends on the material parts. In your family or mosque, are you contributing to the spiritual atmosphere or just present? What specific part are you playing, and is it truly needed?
?
If true unity requires parts to need each other, how does your relationship with Allah reflect this? Do you recognize your complete dependence on Him, or do you act as if you are an independent 'part'?

The Army as a Conceptual Composite and the Need for Social Form
Distinguishes army as conceptual composite; argues all composites need social form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the army serves as an example of a conceptual composite and why every composite requires a social form as a formal part.

Core Idea
An army exists because its soldiers exist, but its unity is conceptual, not real.

This lesson examines a subtle but crucial distinction in kalam: the difference between composites whose unity is real and those whose unity is merely conceptual. Using the example of an army, the author shows that the social form (al-hay'a al-ijtimā'iyya) is a necessary component of any composite, whether real or conceptual. Understanding this distinction helps clarify how unity and existence are predicated in composite beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's own wording guide your initial understanding.
Notice the flow from specific example to general principle.
2
Identify the key claim about the army
Look for the statement that the army exists externally because its individual parts (soldiers) exist. Contrast this with the stone placed next to a human.
The army is a conceptual whole, not a real one.
3
Understand the generalization
The text generalizes that every composite needs a social form as a formal part. This applies to both real and conceptual composites.
The social form is the formal part (al-juz' al-sūrī).
4
Analyze the objection and answer
Focus on the objection: why single out real essences if every essence has some unity? The answer distinguishes the need of the subject of the form from mere conceptual need.
In real essences, the form is real and parts are conditioned by mixture.
5
Reflect on conceptual vs. real unity
Consider the implications: how does this distinction affect our understanding of existence and composition?
Conceptual unity does not imply external existence of the composite as a real entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلا) أن تلك الهيئة إلخ) لا فرق بينهما إلا بأنه في أن آحادهما موجودة، فيكون الكل موجوداً. وبعد اعتبار الهيئة الاجتماعية يكون المركب اعتبارياً موصوفاً بالوحدة الاعتبارية معدوماً في الخارج، إلا أن القول بعدم وجود العسكر في الخارج، مما لا يقول به عاقل، بخلاف الحجر الموضوع بجنب الإنسان. ومن هذا علم أنه على تقدير التركيب لا بد من الهيئة الاجتماعية، سواء كان المركب حقيقياً أو اعتبارياً، فهذا لا ينافي ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره في حواشي المطالع من أن كل مركب لا بد فيه من هيئة اجتماعية وحدانية تكون جزءاً من المركب. والمراد بالهيئة الاجتماعية الجزء الصوري؛ ليطرد في الجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة، على ما فسره في تلك الحواشي في بحث تقسيم العلم وفي مباحث التعريفات، فلا يرد النقض بالجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة وأنه يلزم أن يكون كل مركب جوهري متقوماً بالعرض. قوله : ( والكلام في الماهية الحقيقية (الواحدة فإن قلت : كل ماهية لها وحدة ولو بحسب الهيئة الاعتبارية، يحتاج جزؤه الصوري، أعني تلك الهيئة إلى باقي الأجزاء، فما معنى تخصيص ما له وحدة حقيقية بهذا الحكم؟ قلت مرادهم احتياج معروض الهيئة، فإن الهيئة ثابتة في الحقيقيات، وإن لم تكن جزءاً، وأجزاء المعدن هي العناصر الممتزجة، فمن حيث الامتزاج يشترط كل منها بالآخر، فلا يبعد اعتبار الأجزاء المادية في الحاجة، ولك أن تقول : المراد الحاجة بحسب نفس الأمر، وحاجة الهيئة الاعتبارية محضة.
His statement: (Except) that form etc.) There is no difference between them except that in it (the army) its individuals exist, so the whole exists. And after considering the social form, the composite becomes conceptual, described by conceptual unity, non-existent externally. However, the claim that the army does not exist externally is something no rational person would say, unlike the stone placed next to the human. From this it is known that upon the assumption of composition, the social form is necessary, whether the composite is real or conceptual. This does not contradict what the commentator (al-Sharih) said in his glosses on al-Matali': that every composite must have a unitive social form that is a part of the composite. And what is meant by social form is the formal part; so that it applies uniformly to the body composed of matter and form, as he explained in those glosses in the division of knowledge and in the investigations of definitions. Thus the objection based on the body composed of matter and form does not apply that it would entail that every substantial composite is constituted by an accident. His statement: (And the discussion concerns the real essence (the one) for if you say: Every essence has unity even if only according to conceptual form, its formal part, i.e., that form, needs the other parts, so what is the point of singling out what has real unity for this ruling? I say: Their intended meaning is the need of the subject of the form, for the form is established in real things even if it is not a part, and the parts of a mineral are the mixed elements; with respect to mixture, each of them is conditioned by the other, so it is not unlikely to consider the material parts in need. And you may say: The intended meaning is need in itself, and the need of the conceptual form is pure.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage discusses the army as an example of a conceptual composite (whole). The army is a sum of individuals with a social form that gives it conceptual unity. Unlike the medicine, the army's parts are real and exist, so the army as a whole exists externally (since its parts exist), but its unity is conceptual. The commentary argues that the army does exist externally, unlike the conceptual unity from stone and human. It then links this to the general principle that every composite, whether real or conceptual, needs a social form as a formal part. This is consistent with the commentator's glosses, where the social form is the formal part, and applies to physical bodies composed of matter and form. The passage ends with an objection and answer: If every essence has some unity, why single out real essences? The answer is that the need is for the subject of the form; in real essences, the form is real and the parts are conditioned by mixture, whereas in conceptual unities the need is only pure.

Key Terms
عسكر
هيئة اجتماعية
ماهية اعتبارية
جزء صوري
هيولى وصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the army is a real unity, existing as a single entity in reality.
Correction: The text clarifies that the army's unity is conceptual; its existence is only the sum of its parts, not a real composite entity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the army exist externally?
A) Because its social form is real.
B) Because its individual parts exist.
C) Because it has a real essence.
D) Because the social form is a part.

Key Takeaway
Every composite, whether real or conceptual, requires a social form as a formal part, but only real composites have a need grounded in external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you view your own identity as a real unity or a conceptual composite of your actions, thoughts, and roles? How does this distinction affect your accountability before Allah?
?
If every composite needs a social form, what is the social form that unites your various roles as a Muslim? Are you merely a collection of individual acts, or is there a unifying intention that gives you real unity?
?
The army's unity is only conceptual, yet it is real in effect. In your daily life, which of your unities are merely conceptual, and which are real? How does that matter for your sincerity and the reality of your faith?

The Problem of Quiddity Composition
Al-Jurjani introduces the problem of quiddity composition and lists three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the problem of quiddity composition as illustrated by the example of man, and the three possible solutions to this problem.

Core Idea
Distinct mental concepts of a quiddity imply either simplicity or multiplicity in reality.

Al-Jurjani begins by examining the quiddity of man, which is described by multiple concepts. Some concepts are essential, others accidental. The distinctness of these concepts in the mind raises a fundamental question: do they correspond to a single simple reality or to multiple realities? This passage presents the three logical possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the example
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the example of 'man' and the concepts predicated of it. Then read the translation.
Notice how the author distinguishes between concepts like substance and walker.
2
Distinguish essential vs accidental concepts
The passage states that some concepts are external to the quiddity (accidental) while others are not (essential). Identify which are which.
Essential concepts are those without which the quiddity cannot be conceived.
3
Identify the three possibilities
Based on the distinctness of essential concepts in the mind, three possibilities arise. Find the enumeration in the passage.
The possibilities hinge on whether the images correspond to one simple thing or multiple quiddities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماهية الإنسان مثلا يصدق عليها مفهومات متعددة كالجوهر والجسم والحيوان، وكالماشي والكاتب والضاحك إلى غير ذلك، وليست نسبة هذه المفهومات إلى الماهية الإنسانية على السوية، بل بعضها خارجة عنها عارضة لها كالماشي وأخواته وبعضها ليست كذلك كالجوهر وأخواته . ثم إن هذه المفهومات التي ليست خارجة عنها لا شك أنها متغايرة في الذهن بحسب أنفسها ووجوداتها أيضاً، فهذه الصور المتغايرة في الذهن إما أن تكون صوراً لشيء واحد في حد ذاته بسيط لا تعدد فيه أو تكون صوراً لأشياء متعددة متغايرة الماهية وعلى الثاني إما أن تكون تلك الماهية المتعددة موجودة بوجودات متعددة، أو بوجود واحد، فهذه احتمالات ثلاثة لا مزيد عليها، وقد ذهب إلى كل واحد منها طائفة : الاحتمال الأول : أن تكون تلك الصور لشيء واحد هو بسيط ذاتاً ووجوداً، لكن ينتزع العقل منه باعتبارات شتى هذه الصور المتخالفة كما مر، وهذا هو القول بأن الأجزاء المحمولة عين المركب في الخارج ماهية ووجوداً، وأن جعل الأجزاء في الخارج هو بعينه جعل المركب فيه ولا امتياز بينهما إلا في الذهن، وهو المختار عند المحققين كما بين في الكتاب
For example, the quiddity of man is predicated of multiple concepts like 'substance' (jawhar), 'body' (jism), 'animal' (hayawān), and like 'walker' (māshī), 'writer' (kātib), 'laugher' (ḍāḥik) and so on. And the relation of these concepts to the human quiddity is not equal; rather, some of them are external to it, accidental to it, like 'walker' and its ilk, and some are not like that, like 'substance' and its ilk. Then, these concepts that are not external to it are undoubtedly distinct in the mind according to themselves and their existences also. So these distinct images in the mind are either images of one thing in itself, simple with no multiplicity in it, or images of multiple things with distinct quiddities; and on the second, either those multiple quiddities exist with multiple existences, or with one existence. These are three possibilities with no more. And each possibility has been held by a group. The first possibility: that those images are of one thing that is simple in essence (dhāt) and existence (wujūd), but the intellect extracts from it, according to various considerations, these differing images as mentioned. And this is the statement that the predicated parts (al-ajzāʾ al-maḥmūla) are identical to the composite in the external world in quiddity and existence, and that the making of the parts in the external world is identical to the making of the composite therein, and there is no distinction between them except in the mind. And this is the chosen view among the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn) as explained in the book.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the problem of metaphysical composition and a classification of possible solutions. The author begins with the example of the quiddity of man, which is predicated of multiple concepts such as 'substance', 'body', 'animal' (which are essential) and 'walker', 'writer', 'laugher' (which are accidental). He notes that the relation of these concepts to the quiddity is not uniform: some are external and accidental, while others are intrinsic and essential. The essential concepts are distinct in the mind and in their own existence. This leads to the core question: are these distinct mental images images of a single simple thing, or of multiple distinct quiddities? If the latter, do those multiple quiddities exist with multiple existences or with a single existence? Thus three possibilities arise. The author then states that each possibility has its proponents. He describes the first possibility in detail: the images are of one simple thing in essence and existence, but the intellect extracts differing images from it through various considerations. This view holds that the predicated parts (the essential concepts) are identical to the composite in the external world, both in quiddity and existence; the making of the parts is identical to the making of the composite, with no distinction except in the mind. The author notes that this is the chosen view of the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn), as explained in the book (i.e., al-Mawāqif).

مفهومات
أجزاء محمولة
بسيط
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All concepts predicated of a quiddity are equally related to it.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the relation is not equal: some concepts (like 'walker') are accidental and external, while others (like 'substance') are essential and not external.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many possibilities are enumerated regarding the distinct mental images?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The three possibilities for the relation between distinct mental concepts and reality are: one simple thing, multiple quiddities with multiple existences, or multiple quiddities with one existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own identity, do you experience yourself as a simple unity or as a bundle of distinct aspects like body, soul, and mind? Which of the three possibilities in this passage best aligns with your inner sense of self? What does that imply about your ultimate accountability before the One who created you?
?
If the essential concepts of your quiddity (like being a human) are distinct in mind yet correspond to a single simple reality, then your deeds (accidents) do not define your essence. Does this comfort you or disturb you? How does that affect your urgency to purify your accidental qualities—like your faith and character—since they are not your core but still real?
?
Consider that Allah knows you as a single, simple reality despite your many attributes. How does this lesson challenge your habit of defining yourself by your changing states (e.g., success, failure, sin)? Will you let the truth of your quiddity in the divine knowledge free you from being enslaved to your transient concepts of yourself?

The First Possibility: Identity of Parts and Composite
Al-Jurjani explains the first possibility: identity of parts and composite in the external world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first of the three possibilities for quiddity composition: the predicated parts are identical to the composite in external reality, differing only in mental consideration.

Core Idea
The parts are mentally distinct but externally identical to the composite.

After enumerating three possibilities, al-Jurjani elaborates on the first view, which is the position of the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn). According to this view, the essential concepts (like 'substance' and 'body') are not separate entities in the external world; rather, they are identical to the whole quiddity of man in external existence, distinguished only by the mind's differing perspectives.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the first possibility
Read the Arabic passage describing the first possibility. Note the key phrase that the parts are 'identical to the composite in the external world'.
The passage states: 'عين المركب في الخارج ماهية ووجوداً'.
2
Understand the meaning of 'identity'
This view holds that in external reality, the parts (substance, body, animal) are not separate; they are the same as the composite (man). The mind distinguishes them by different considerations.
This is a sophisticated position reconciling multiplicity of concepts with unity of being.
3
Note the authoritative status
The author mentions that this is the chosen view among the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn), indicating its acceptance in the tradition.
The 'book' referred to is likely al-Mawāqif itself.

The Proximate Differentia: Why It Must Be One
Defines al-fasl al-qarib and argues for its unicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proximate differentia must be single and independent, and how the unity of the effect's essence precludes multiple independent causes.

Core Idea
The proximate differentia must be one because multiple independent causes for one essence are impossible.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif tackles a key logical principle: the proximate differentia that defines a species must be unique. The author argues that if multiple differentiae were possible, each would be an independent cause for the same genus, leading to an unacceptable concurrence of causes. Understanding this argument is essential for grasping how definitions work in Aristotelian logic and Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the overall argument. Don't worry about details yet—just let the author's flow come through.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the main distinction
Notice how the author distinguishes the proximate differentia from remote and absolute differentiae. What is special about the proximate one?
Remote and absolute differentiae can be multiple; proximate cannot.
3
Understand the condition of unity
The author introduces the condition that the effect (the genus) must be one in essence (dhat). Why does this forbid multiple differentiae?
If essence is one, multiple independent causes cannot coexist.
4
Examine the objection and response
An objector says the differentia alone is not a complete cause because there are parts and conditions. How does the author reply?
The differentia plus conditions together make an independent cause.
5
Reflect on the principle of causality
The author concludes that the differentia is an efficient cause (illa fa'iliyya). Why does this matter for the overall argument?
It reinforces that each differentia would be a full cause, leading to impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل بالقريب؛ لأن الفصل البعيد وكذا المطلق يجوز تعدده، ويكون كل من الفصول المتعددة علة للجنس الذي في مرتبته كالناطق للحيوان والحساس للجسم النامي، والنامي للجسم مطلقاً، وقابل الأبعاد للجوهر. واعتبر وحدة المعلول بالذات؛ لأنه إذا تعدد ذاته جاز توارد العلل عليه كما في أفراد نوع واحد يقع بعضها بعلة وبعضها بعلة أخرى، وأما مع وحدة الذات، فلا مساغ لذلك؛ إذ يستغنى بكل عن كل، سواء كان الواحد بالذات شخصاً، وهو ظاهر، أو لا، كما نحن بصدده، فإن طبيعة الجنس في النوع قبل اعتبار تعدد أفراده ذات واحدة لا تعدد فيها، وقيد العلة بالاستقلال؛ لأن تعدد العلل الناقصة جائز، فإن قلت: ليس الفصل وحده علة تامة للجنس؛ لجواز أن يكون للجنس أجزاء، وأن يكون هناك شرائط معتبرة. قلت: كل واحد من الفصلين مع باقي الأمور المعتبرة علة مستقلة فيلزم توارد العلل المستقلة. قوله: ( وأما مع وحدة الذات إلخ ) يعني أن الدليل الذي ذكروه في امتناع توارد العلل، وإن صوره في الواحد الشخصي، لكنه جار في الواحد بالذات، سواء كان شخصاً أو لا. قوله: ( فإن طبيعة الجنس في النوع ) أي الواحد ذات واحدة بخلافها في النوعين، فإنها متحصصة، ففي كل نوع يكون الفصل علة لحصتها، فلا يكون المعلول واحداً بالذات، وتوارد الفصول مع تخصص الجنس ليس أحدها متقدماً على الآخر، فتدبر. قوله: ( كل واحد من الفصلين إلخ) حاصله: أنه كما يمتنع توارد التامتين يمتنع توارد الناقصتين من جنس واحد كالفاعلين والمادتين والصورتين؛ لاستلزامه توارد التامتين، وفيما نحن فيه على قاعدة العلية يكون الفصل علة فاعلية؛ إذ العلة الموجبة إذا كان أمراً واحداً لا يكون إلا فاعلاً.
The differentia (fasl) is qualified by 'proximate' (qarib) because the remote differentia and likewise the absolute may be multiple, and each of the multiple differentiae is a cause (illa) for the genus (jins) at its rank, like 'rational' (natiq) for animal, 'sensitive' (hassas) for the growing body, 'growing' for body absolutely, and 'receptive of dimensions' for substance. Unity of the effect in essence (dhat) was considered because if its essence is multiple, the concurrence of causes upon it is permissible, as in individuals of one species some occur by one cause and some by another. But with unity of essence, there is no place for that, because each is independent of the other, whether the one in essence is a person, which is obvious, or not, as we are dealing with. Indeed, the nature of the genus in the species before considering the multiplicity of its individuals is a single essence with no multiplicity. The cause was restricted to independence because multiplicity of incomplete causes is permissible. If you say: The differentia alone is not a complete cause of the genus, because there may be parts of the genus and certain conditions. I say: Each of the two differentiae together with the remaining considered conditions is an independent cause, so the concurrence of independent causes is entailed. His saying: 'And as for unity of essence, etc.' means that the proof they mentioned for the impossibility of concurrence of causes, though he pictured it in the personal one, is applicable to the one in essence, whether it is a person or not. His saying: 'Indeed, the nature of the genus in the species' i.e., it is a single essence, unlike in two species, for it is specified. So in each species, the differentia is a cause of its portion, so the effect is not one in essence, and the concurrence of differentiae with specification of the genus is not such that one precedes the other, so reflect. His saying: 'Each of the two differentiae, etc.' Summary: Just as the concurrence of two complete causes is impossible, so is the concurrence of two incomplete causes of the same kind, like two agents, two matters, and two forms, because it entails the concurrence of two complete causes. And in our case, according to the principle of causality, the differentia is an efficient cause, for the necessitating cause (illa mujiba), if it is one thing, is only an agent.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the concept of the proximate differentia (al-fasl al-qarib) and argues for its unicity. The author first distinguishes the proximate differentia from the remote and absolute differentiae, noting that only the latter may be multiple. Each multiple differentia acts as a cause for the genus at its rank. The key condition for unicity is the unity of the effect in essence (dhat). The author explains that if the effect's essence is multiple, multiple causes can concur, as seen in individuals of the same species. However, when the essence is one, multiple causes are impossible because each cause would be independent. The cause is further qualified as independent (mustaqill) because incomplete causes may be multiple. The author then addresses an objection: the differentia alone is not the complete cause of the genus, as there are parts and conditions. The response is that each differentia, together with the required conditions, is an independent cause, leading to the forbidden concurrence of independent causes. The commentary by the sharh clarifies that the proof against concurrence applies to the one in essence, whether personal or not, and that the differentia is an efficient cause according to the principle of causality.

Key Terms
الفصل القريب
العلة المستقلة
وحدة الذات
توارد العلل
الجنس
النوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple differentiae can coexist as partial causes for the same genus.
Correction: The author argues that each differentia, with its conditions, would be an independent complete cause, making multiple impossible due to the unity of the effect's essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the proximate differentia cannot be multiple?
A) Because differentiae are always simple and cannot be combined.
B) Because each differentia would be an independent complete cause for the same effect, leading to impossible concurrence of causes.
C) Because the genus is divisible and can only be differentiated by one attribute.
D) Because the remote differentia covers all necessary differentiation.

Key Takeaway
The proximate differentia must be single and independent; multiple independent causes for a single essence are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though Allah alone is the independent cause of every single moment of your life, or do you implicitly rely on other 'causes' like your health, your wealth, or your social status as if they had independent power? This lesson proves that multiple independent causes for one essence are impossible. How will you uproot any hidden shirk in your daily reliance?
?
Your soul is one essence, created by Allah. Yet you often divide its accountability among multiple factors: your upbringing, your environment, your genetics. According to this argument, can there be more than one independent cause for your spiritual state? If not, how should this change the way you take responsibility for your faith?
?
The differentia is an efficient cause. Every good attribute you possess—whether intellect, piety, or skill—has Allah as its ultimate efficient cause. Does your heart acknowledge that, or do you feel a sense of self-sufficiency? What practical step will you take today to attribute every success directly to Allah alone?

Senses and Voluntary Motion as Differentiae for Animal
Explains why sensitive and voluntary motion are not two proximate differentiae for animal but effects of a single one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attributes of sensation and voluntary motion are not two separate differentiae for the genus animal, but rather effects of a single differentia, and why both are used to indicate it.

Core Idea
Multiple observable attributes do not imply multiple differentiae; they are often effects of one.

This lesson addresses a subtle point in definition theory: when we describe an animal as both sensitive and able to move voluntarily, it might seem these are two separate defining features. The author argues they are both consequences of a single differentia, and explains the reasoning behind using both as indicators. Let's explore the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the full argument. Notice the claim refuted and the alternative explanation.
2
Identify the refuted claim
Find the statement that 'sensitive' and 'voluntary mover' are two proximate differentiae for animal. The author rejects this.
3
Understand the 'effect of a differentia' concept
The author says each attribute is an effect of the differentia. Consider the analogy of 'speech' for the differentia of man. When the true differentia is unknown, its nearest effects are used to express it.
4
Grasp why both attributes are used
Because the priority of sensation over motion (or vice versa) is ambiguous, both are used together as a collective expression. The commentary elaborates on the rebuttal of the priority claim and the necessary restriction of voluntary motion to exclude celestial motions.

Source Text

لا يقال : الحساس والمتحرك بالإرادة فصلان قريبان للحيوان؛ لأنا نقول : بل كل منهما أثر لفصله، فإن حقيقة الفصل إذا جهلت عبر عنها بأقرب آثارها كالنطق لفصل الانسان. ولما اشتبه تقدم كل من الحس والحركة الإرادية على الآخر عبر بهما. قوله: ( أثر لفصله فالفصل واحد عبر عنه باللازمين لكونهما في مرتبة واحدة. قوله: ( ولما اشتبه تقدم إلخ ) إذ الاحساس قد يكون مبدأ للحركة، وقد تكون الحركة مبدأ للإحساس. قوله: (ولما اشتبه تقدم كل من الحس والحركة إلخ قيل : يتقدم الإحساس على الحركة الإرادية لأنه إدراك وهي متوقفة عليه، ورد بأن الموقوف عليه هو الإدراك مطلقاً، لا الإحساس. وأيضاً الإنسان ربما يتحرك إلى شيء ليدركه فبعض الحركة متقدم على الإدراك، فلم يظهر تقدم أحدهما على الآخر على الإطلاق، فوضع الكل موضع الفصل. واعلم أنه لا بد من تقييد الحركة الإرادية الحيوانية بكونها لا على نهج واحد، ليتحقق كونها أثراً لفصله القريب، وإلا فمطلق الحركة بالإرادة موجودة في الفلك، لكن حركة كل من الأفلاك على نهج واحد لبساطته عندهم.
It cannot be said: The sensitive and the voluntary mover are two proximate differentiae for animal; for we say: Rather, each of them is an effect of its differentia. For the reality of the differentia, if unknown, is expressed by its nearest effects, like speech (nutq) for the differentia of man. And because the priority of each of sense and voluntary motion over the other was ambiguous, they were both used. His saying: 'An effect of its differentia, so the differentia is one, expressed by the two concomitants because they are at the same rank.' His saying: 'And because the priority of each of sense and motion, etc.' for sensation may be the starting point of motion, and motion may be the starting point of sensation. His saying: 'And because the priority of each of sense and motion etc.' It was said: Sensation precedes voluntary motion because it is perception and the latter depends on it. But it was rebutted: What is depended upon is perception absolutely, not sensation. Also, a person may move towards something to perceive it, so some motion precedes perception; thus the priority of one over the other is not established absolutely, so the whole (i.e., both attributes) was placed in the position of the differentia. And know that it is necessary to restrict voluntary animal motion to that which is not in a single pattern, so that it can be realized as an effect of its proximate differentia; otherwise, absolute voluntary motion exists in the celestial sphere, but each sphere's motion is in a single pattern due to its simplicity according to them.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that 'sensitive' and 'voluntary mover' are two proximate differentiae for the genus 'animal'. The author argues that each of these attributes is merely an effect of a single differentia, not the differentia itself. When the true nature of a differentia is unknown, it is described by its nearest effects, like 'speech' for the differentia of man. The reason both 'sense' and 'voluntary motion' are used is that their mutual priority is ambiguous: sometimes sensation precedes motion, sometimes motion precedes sensation. The commentator explains that if one claimed sensation precedes because it is perception and motion depends on perception, the rebuttal is that dependence is on perception as such, not sensation specifically. Moreover, a person may move towards something to perceive it, so some motion precedes perception. Therefore, no absolute priority is established, so both attributes are taken together as a collective expression of the single differentia. Finally, the commentator notes that voluntary animal motion must be qualified as not being in a single pattern, because celestial spheres also move voluntarily but in a uniform pattern due to their simplicity, which would not be an effect of the animal differentia.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that multiple observable attributes imply multiple differentiae.
Correction: The author clarifies that multiple attributes like sensation and voluntary motion are not themselves differentiae but consequences of a single differentia.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider 'sensitive' and 'voluntary mover' as effects of a single differentia rather than two differentiae?
A) Because each is an independent cause for animal.
B) Because their mutual priority is ambiguous and they are both necessary for the definition.
C) Because they are simply synonymous.
D) Because the differentia must be one, and these are its nearest effects.

Key Takeaway
The attributes of sensation and voluntary motion are not themselves the differentia of animal but its effects; they are used as indicators because the true differentia is unknown and their priority is ambiguous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at yourself, do you define your humanity by outward attributes like speech or reason, without ever asking what the true differentia is that makes you you? How would your life change if you sought that deeper reality instead of settling for effects?
?
You often judge people by their actions—whether they sense or move in certain ways. But this lesson teaches that effects are not the essence. When will you stop reducing others (and yourself) to mere symptoms and courageously seek the hidden differentia that Al-Khaliq alone fully knows?
?
The ambiguity of priority between sensation and motion shows you cannot always pin down which attribute comes first. What if the same ambiguity exists in your spiritual life—do you prioritize outward acts of worship over inner perception, or vice versa? Are you willing to let go of your preferred order and accept a unified differentia that transcends both?

Two Interpretations of Completeness in the Proximate Differentia
Discusses two ways to understand 'tamam' in proximate differentia definition and its consequence for unicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of 'completeness' in the definition of the proximate differentia and their implications for the possibility of multiple differentiae.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'tamam' determines whether multiple proximate differentiae are possible.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif delves into a subtle logical distinction. When defining the proximate differentia as a 'complete distinguishing part,' the word 'complete' can modify either the part itself or the act of distinguishing. This seemingly minor ambiguity has major consequences for whether a single essence can have multiple differentiae. Let's explore the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repetition of 'His saying' which indicates commentary on a base text.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'tamam'
The passage begins by distinguishing two ways to understand 'the complete distinguishing part': either 'complete' modifies 'part' or it modifies 'distinguishing'. Understand each interpretation.
The first interpretation prevents multiplicity; the second may allow it under certain conditions.
3
Understand the objection about composite differentia
An objector suggests that a differentia composed of two co-equal parts could yield multiple differentiae. The answer is that the parts are not at the same rank relative to the genus.
Think about logical hierarchy: the composite differentia is a single unit at one rank.
4
Grasp the causality argument against multiplicity in a genusless quiddity
Even if the second interpretation allows multiplicity in a quiddity without a genus, the commentator argues it is impossible due to causality: a cause cannot exist without its effect.
The argument uses the example of fire and heat to illustrate necessitation.
5
Note the final clarification about simplicity
The passage concludes by clarifying that when the proximate differentia is said to be 'simple', it means truly simple with no multiplicity in essence or aspects.
This reinforces that true simplicity precludes any composition that could allow multiple differentiae.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إذا جعل التمام في قولهم : الفصل القريب هو الجزء المميز التام . قوله : (امتنع تعدده إلخ) قيل : إِذا تركب ماهية من جنس وفصل مركب من أمرين متساويين، كان ذلك الفصل وكل واحد من جزأيه فصلاً قريباً، بمعنى المميز عن جميع ما عداه . ولا يلزم التوارد؛ لعدم كفاية كل واحد منهما في وجود الجنس. والجواب : أن الجزأين ليسا مرتبة الفصل المركب والكلام في تعدد الفصل القريب في مرتبة واحدة بالقياس إلى الجنس. نعم إنهما في مرتبة واحدة بالقياس إلى الفصل لكن لا جنس فيه . قوله : ( لم يمتنع تعدده في ماهية ليس لها جنس فيه بحث : إذ الظاهر امتناع هذا أيضاً، تفريعاً على العلية ضرورة تخلف المعلول عن العلة المستلزمة. وما ذكره في حواشيه على المطالع من أن بطلانه إنما يظهر إذا كان هناك جنس أو حصة منه ولا يكون الفصل له، وفيما نحن بصدده لم يوجد شيء منهما، محل تأمل؛ لأن وجود العلة بلا معلول، لا وجودهما معاً من غير أن تكون العلة علة له ألا يرى أن ليس المفهوم من قولنا : النار علة موجبة للحرارة أنه لو وجد النار والحرارة كانت الأولى علة للثانية، حتى لو وجد النار بلا حرارة يكن لم من التخلف الممتنع في شيء، ولو كان معنى التخلف ما ذكره لم يستقم الفرع الثالث والرابع إلا بتكلف . قوله : (إذا كان الفصل القريب بسيطاً) أي حقيقياً لا كثرة فيه بوجه من الوجوه، لا بحسب ذاته ولا بحسب جهاته واعتباراته .
His saying: 'When completeness (tamam) is made [to qualify] in their statement: The proximate differentia is the complete distinguishing part.' His saying: 'Its multiplicity is prevented, etc.' It was said: If a quiddity (mahiyya) is composed of a genus and a differentia that is compounded of two co-equal things, then that differentia and each of its two parts could be a proximate differentia, meaning what distinguishes from everything else. And the concurrence [of causes] is not entailed because each one alone is not sufficient for the existence of the genus. The answer: The two parts are not at the rank of the composite differentia, and the discussion is about multiplicity of the proximate differentia at the same rank relative to the genus. Yes, they are at the same rank relative to the differentia but there is no genus in it. His saying: 'Its multiplicity is not prevented in a quiddity that has no genus; there is discussion [in this].' For the apparent view is that this is also impossible, as a corollary of causality, due to the necessary failure of the effect to follow from the necessitating cause. And what he mentioned in his marginalia on al-Matali', that its invalidity only appears when there is a genus or a portion of it and the differentia is not for it, and in our case nothing of them exists, is subject to reflection; because the existence of a cause without an effect [is not conceivable], not that they exist together without the cause being a cause for it. Do you not see that the meaning of our statement 'Fire is a necessitating cause of heat' is not that if fire and heat exist then the first is a cause of the second, such that if fire existed without heat there would be no impossibility of failure? And if the meaning of failure were as he mentioned, the third and fourth branches would not be sound except with difficulty. His saying: 'If the proximate differentia is simple' i.e., truly simple with no multiplicity in any respect, neither in its essence nor in its aspects and considerations.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage examines two interpretations of the phrase 'the complete distinguishing part' (al-juz' al-mumayyiz al-tamm) in defining the proximate differentia. The first interpretation attaches 'completeness' to the 'part' (juz') itself, so the differentia must be a complete part. In this case, multiplicity is impossible because each part would be partial, not complete. The second interpretation attaches 'completeness' to the 'distinguishing' (tamyiz), meaning the differentia completely distinguishes the species. Under this interpretation, multiplicity could be possible in a quiddity that has no genus (i.e., a simple essence), but the commentator argues that even then multiplicity is impossible due to causality: a single cause cannot produce multiple distinct effects without a genus. The passage also discusses an objection: if a differentia is composed of two co-equal parts, could each part be a differentia? The answer is that those parts are not at the same rank as the composite differentia relative to the genus. Finally, the commentator clarifies that when it is said 'if the proximate differentia is simple', it means truly simple with no multiplicity in essence or aspects.

Key Terms
التمام
الجزء المميز التام
الماهية التي لا جنس لها
علية
بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A differentia composed of two co-equal parts can be considered as two separate proximate differentiae at the same rank.
Correction: The two parts are not at the same rank as the composite differentia relative to the genus; multiplicity of proximate differentia at the same rank is not allowed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which interpretation is multiplicity of a proximate differentia allegedly possible?
A) When completeness qualifies the part itself and the quiddity has a genus.
B) When completeness qualifies the distinguishing and the quiddity has no genus.
C) When the differentia is composite of two co-equal things.
D) Never; multiplicity is absolutely impossible under any interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'tamam' in the definition of proximate differentia determines whether multiple differentiae can exist for a single quiddity, but causality ultimately precludes multiplicity even in genusless essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that a single cause cannot produce multiple distinct effects without a common genus. In your own understanding of Allah's attributes, do you ever attribute multiple independent causes to His actions? How can this principle help you guard against errors in tawhid?
?
The term 'tamam' (completeness) can be interpreted in different ways, leading to different conclusions. In your spiritual life, where might you be settling for an incomplete definition of a core belief? What would it take for you to seek a 'complete' understanding?

Essence Accepts Commonality but Not Determination
Defines the property of essence accepting commonality but not determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essence accepting commonality (as predication) and individual determination, and why sharing means predication rather than correspondence.

Core Idea
Essence is shared by predication, not by multiple existence.

In this lesson, we explore the eleventh goal of the author: that essence (such as 'human') can be common to many individuals without being determined to any one. The passage clarifies that this 'commonality' refers to the logical property of predication (ḥaml), not to a one-to-one correspondence with external objects. Understanding this distinction is crucial for further discussions on universals and particulars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the key terms: الماهية, الشركة, التعين, الحمل, المطابقة, المعلوم.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the meaning of 'essence accepts commonality'
The text says essence does not prevent us from supposing its sharing and predication of it. This means we can predicate the same essence (e.g., human) of many individuals.
The example 'human' indicates species-essence.
3
Distinguish between 'sharing as predication' and 'sharing as correspondence'
The commentator clarifies that the sharing mentioned is a property of the known concept (al-ma'lum) and means predication (al-ḥaml), not correspondence (al-muṭābaqa) which is a property of the mental form (al-ṣura).
4
Reflect on the importance of this distinction
This distinction prevents the misunderstanding that a single external object can have multiple essences. Instead, a single concept applies to many individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الحادي عشر : الماهية تقبل الشركة دون التعين ] (الماهية ) كالإنسان مثلاً ) تقبل الشركة أي لا تمنع من فرض اشتراكها وحملها عليها . قوله : ( كالإنسان أشار ذلك إلى أن المراد لماهية الماهية النوعية بقرينة ذكر التعين معها . قوله : ( وحملها إلخ) أشار بالعطف إلى أن الأشتراك الذي هو صفة المعلوم معناه الحمل لا المطابقة، فإنها صفة الصورة التي هي العلم .
The Eleventh Goal: Essence accepts commonality (al-shirka) but not determination (al-ta'ayyun). [Essence] — like 'human' for example — accepts commonality, i.e., it does not prevent our supposing its sharing and predication (ḥaml) of it. His words: (like ‘human’) indicate that what is meant by ‘essence’ is the specific essence (al-māhiyya al-nawʿiyya) due to the context of mentioning determination with it. His words: (and its predication, etc.) indicate by the conjunction that the sharing which is a property of the known (al-maʿlūm) means predication (al-ḥaml), not correspondence (al-muṭābaqa), for correspondence is a property of the mental form (al-ṣūra) which is knowledge.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the eleventh goal (maqsad): that essence (al-māhiyya) — such as the quiddity 'human' — accepts being shared among multiple individuals; it does not prevent us from assuming that it is common and predicable of many. The commentator clarifies that the example 'human' shows the author means the specific essence (al-māhiyya al-nawʿiyya), i.e., the species-essence, because it is contrasted with 'determination' (al-taʿayyun) which applies to individuals. He then explains the commentator's own gloss on 'ḥamluhā' (its predication): the conjunction indicates that the 'sharing' (al-ishṭirāk) mentioned is a property of the known concept, and it means predication (al-ḥaml) — i.e., the universal is predicated of many — not correspondence (al-muṭābaqa), which is a property of the mental representation (al-ṣūra) that is knowledge. In other words, when we say a concept is 'shared', we mean it can be predicated of multiple subjects, not that it matches multiple external objects in a one-to-one way.

Key Terms

الشركة
التعين
الحمل
المطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'essence accepts commonality' means the same essence exists in multiple external objects simultaneously.
Correction: The passage clarifies that sharing here means predication (al-ḥaml), not correspondence (al-muṭābaqa). That is, the concept can be applied to many individuals through predication, not that a single external essence is instantiated in multiple locations.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does 'essence accepts commonality' mean?
A) One essence can exist in multiple external objects simultaneously.
B) The concept of an essence can be predicated of many individuals.
C) Essence is divisible into parts.
D) Essence is determined by its differentia.

Key Takeaway
Essence accepts commonality in the sense of being predicable of many individuals, not in the sense of multiple external instantiations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
How does understanding that essence accepts commonality (ishtirak) but not determination affect your view of the unity of the Muslim ummah? Are you willing to see every believer as sharing the same essential humanity, regardless of outward differences?

Ta'ayyun: The Principle of Individuation Beyond the Common Essence
Defines ta'ayyun and proves it is not merely the universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that ta'ayyun (individuation) is an essential component of an individual's essence that goes beyond the universal nature and is not reducible to accidents.

Core Idea
Ta'ayyun is the ontological 'thisness' that makes Zayd Zayd and not 'Amr.

This passage from al-Mawaqif tackles a fundamental problem in metaphysics: what makes an individual distinct from others of the same kind? The author uses the example of two human persons, Zayd and 'Amr, to argue that each individual's essence includes a unique factor—ta'ayyun—that is not merely the universal human nature nor the accidental properties. This lesson will explore the definition and proof of ta'ayyun as an additional component.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let the author's argument unfold.
Notice the use of 'Zayd' and 'Amr' as examples.
2
Identify the core proof
Find the statement that if Zayd's concept were only 'human', then 'Amr would be Zayd. This is the key reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand what ta'ayyun is not
Note that the author explicitly says ta'ayyun is not accidents or being bound by them, because it does not change and is present even disregarding accidents.
4
Analyze the objection and refutation
Follow the objection from Sahib al-Maqasid and see how the author counters each point, concluding that the extra component is not the accidents themselves but the principle of individuation.
The objection has three 'even if conceded' steps.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding individuality and essence in Islamic metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الحادي عشر : الماهية تقبل الشركة دون التعين مثل زيد ولا ريبة) لعاقل ( في وجوده ، وليس مفهومه مفهوم الإنسان ) وحده (قطعاً، وإلا لصدق على عمرو أنه زيد كما يصدق عليه أنه إنسان . ( فإذن هو الإنسان مع ) قوله : ( أن المراد بالمعين هو الشخص إلخ تقريره أنه لا شك في وجود الأشخاص الإنسانية مثلاً في الخارج، وأن لها ماهيات هي بها هي وأنها متشاركة في شيء مع قطع النظر عن العوارض وليست ماهياتها ذلك الأمر المشترك فقط، وإلا لصدق بعضها على بعض، فماهياتها مشتملة على أمر وراء المشترك، وهو غير العوارض والتقييد بها لاشتمال ماهياتها عليه مع قطع النظر عن العوارض ولعدم تبدله بخلاف العوارض والتقييد بها وهو المعني من التعين. وبما حررنا لك ظهر أن المراد من المفهوم في قوله : وليس مفهومه مفهوم الإنسان أن ليس ماهيته التي هو بها هو الأمر المشترك بينه وبين عمرو مثلاً، واندفع ما أورده صاحب المقاصد من أنا سلمنا أن ليس مفهومه مفهوم الإنسان الكلي الصادق على زيد، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون هو الإنسان المقيد بالعوارض المخصوصة الشخصية التي لا تصدق على عمرو دون المجموع، ولو سلم فجزء المفهوم لا يلزم أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج، ولو سلم فذلك الشيء هو ما يخصه من الكم والكيف والأين ونحو ذلك مما يعلم وجوده بالضرورة من غير نزاع، لكون أكثره من المحسوسات، وهم لا يسمونه التعين بل ما به التعين
The Second Observatory - The Eleventh Goal: Essence admits commonality without individuation. Like Zayd, and there is no doubt for a rational person in his existence, and his concept is not the concept of 'human' alone, absolutely, otherwise it would be true of 'Amr that he is Zayd just as it is true of him that he is human. So he is human with... His statement: 'That what is meant by al-mu'ayyan is the person etc.' Its explanation: There is no doubt in the existence of human persons for example in the external world, and that they have essences by which they are what they are, and that they share in something disregarding accidents, and their essences are not that common thing only, otherwise some would be true of others. So their essences include an matter beyond the common, and it is not the accidents or being bound by them, because their essences include it while disregarding accidents, and because it does not change unlike accidents and being bound by them, and that is what is meant by ta'ayyun. And by what we have clarified for you, it appears that what is meant by 'concept' in his statement 'and his concept is not the concept of human' is that his essence by which he is what he is is not the common matter between him and 'Amr for example. And what was objected by Sahib al-Maqasid is repelled: that we concede that his concept is not the concept of the universal human that is true of Zayd, but why may it not be the human restricted by specific personal accidents that are not true of 'Amr, rather than the aggregate? And even if conceded, a part of the concept does not necessarily exist externally. And even if conceded, that thing is what pertains to him of quantity, quality, location, and the like, which are known necessarily without dispute, because most of them are sensibles, and they do not call that ta'ayyun but rather that by which ta'ayyun occurs.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines ta'ayyun (individuation) and proves that an individual's essence includes an additional component beyond the universal common nature. The author begins with the example of Zayd and 'Amr: Zayd's concept is not merely 'human', otherwise 'Amr would be Zayd. He then explains that essences of individuals share a common aspect but also include a unique determining factor (ta'ayyun) that is not an accident because it is inseparable and does not change, unlike accidents. The author then refutes an objection from Sahib al-Maqasid who suggested that the essence could be the universal restricted by accidents. The author rejects this because (1) the essence includes the additional factor even disregarding accidents, (2) the additional factor does not change, and (3) the accidents that differentiate persons (like quantity, quality) are called 'that by which ta'ayyun occurs' not ta'ayyun itself. Thus ta'ayyun is a distinct metaphysical component.

Key Terms
التعين
الماهية
المشترك
العوارض
المفهوم
الشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'ayyun is often confused with the accidental properties that differentiate individuals.
Correction: The passage argues that ta'ayyun is a non-accidental, unchanging component of essence, not the accidents themselves. Accidents are only the means by which ta'ayyun manifests.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that Zayd's essence is not merely the concept 'human'?
A) Because Zayd and 'Amr are both human but different.
B) Because if it were merely 'human', then 'Amr would also be Zayd.
C) Because 'human' is not a universal.
D) Because accidents are part of the essence.

Key Takeaway
Ta'ayyun is the essential, non-accidental component that makes an individual distinct from others of the same kind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You share the universal 'humanity' with every other person, but your ta'ayyun is uniquely yours. In your daily life, do you live as if your identity is merely a collection of accidents (your job, appearance, relationships), or do you recognize a deeper, God-given distinctness that defines you before Him? How would your actions change if you truly believed your essence includes a divinely ordained 'thisness' that no one else can replicate?
?
The passage argues that ta'ayyun is not reducible to accidents—they only point to it. Think of a time you defined yourself by an accident (e.g., a failure, a success, a label). How does this lesson challenge you to separate your true self from those changing attributes? What would it mean to see your existence as anchored in that unchanging, God-given ta'ayyun rather than in fleeting circumstances?

Mental Composition of Essence and Individuation
Explains that essence and individuation are mentally distinct but externally one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that essence and individuation are mentally distinct but externally one, and how this resolves the objection that it would lead to the unity of distinct individuals.

Core Idea
Essence and individuation are one in existence but two in the mind.

This passage contrasts the earlier view that essence and individuation are externally composite with the later Ash'ari position that they are mentally distinct but externally one. The commentator defends this view against an objection by clarifying that unity in existence does not require conceptual identity or equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source and its literal translation once to get the overall flow. Notice how the commentator moves from historical context to an objection and its resolution.
The passage is a series of glosses on a base text; focus on the core claim.
2
Identify the key claim
Pinpoint the statement that essence and individuation are united in external existence but distinct in the mind. This is the central thesis of the later scholars.
Look for the phrase 'وهما متحدان في الخارج ذاتا'.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection is that if individuation is united with essence, then Zayd's and 'Amr's individuations would be the same. The answer is that union in existence does not mean conceptual identity; one individuator can be united with multiple essences.
This preserves the distinctness of individuals while maintaining the unity of the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ثم إنه إلخ) ما مر من تركيب الشخص من الماهية والتعين في الخارج مذهب الأوائل وقد بالغ الشيخ فيه، وشنع على من نفى وجود الطبائع وما بينه المصنف بقوله : واعلم إلخ اختاره المتأخرون . قوله : ( وإلا لم يصح إلخ فيه أنه إنما يلزم ذلك لو لم يكونا موجودين بوجود واحد، وقد عرفت تحقيقه على أن القائلين بتعدد الوجود والموجود يكتفون في صحة الحمل بالاتحاد في الذات كما مر . قوله : ( إلى الفرق أي بين المشخص والفصل بعد اشتراكهما في النسبة المذكورة . قوله : ( لأن المشخصات) أي المشخصات التي مدركها أمور جزئية مادية، فلا يرد النقض بمشخصات المجردات قوله : ( واعلم أن نسبة الماهية إلى المشخصات إلخ) هذا التحقيق يدل على أن التشخص محمول بالمواطأة على الماهية، وقد بينا أن لا فساد فيه وإن توهم نظراً إلى الظاهر. قوله: (وهما متحدان في الخارج ذاتا إلخ) اعترض عليه بأنه إذا كان المشخص متحداً مع الماهية كان تشخص زيد متحداً مع تشخص عمرو ؛ لاتحادهما في الماهية، وأنه باطل قطعاً. وجوابه : ما ذكره الشارح في حواشي المطول حيث قال : الاتحاد في الوجود الخارجي لا يستلزم اتحاد المفهومين، ولا تساويهما، فجاز أن يتحد أحدهما بالآخر وبثالث ورابع، فيكون مع كل
His statement: 'Then he... etc.' What has passed about the composition of the person from essence and ta'ayyun in the external world is the doctrine of the Ancients, and the Shaykh insisted on it, and denounced those who deny the existence of natures. And what the author (al-Mawaqif) explained by his statement 'Know etc.' is chosen by the later scholars. His statement: 'And if not, it would not be correct etc.' There is in it: that would only follow if they did not exist with one existence, and you have known its verification, given that those who affirm multiplicity of existence and existent suffice for correctness of predication by unity in essence as has passed. His statement: 'To the difference' i.e. between the individuator and the differentia after their sharing in the mentioned relation. His statement: 'Because the individuators' i.e. the individuators whose perceiver is particular material things, so the objection regarding individuators of immaterial things is not valid. His statement: 'Know that the relation of essence to individuators etc.' This verification indicates that individuation is predicated univocally of the essence, and we have shown that there is no corruption in it even if it is imagined to be contrary to apparent. His statement: 'And they are united in existence in essence etc.' An objection was raised against it: If the individuator is united with the essence, then the individuation of Zayd would be united with the individuation of 'Amr, because they are united in essence, and that is absolutely false. Its answer is what the commentator mentioned in the margins of al-Mutawwal: Unity in external existence does not necessitate unity of the two concepts, nor their equivalence, so it is possible for one to be united with another and with a third and fourth, so that it is with each...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of composition of essence and individuation, emphasizing that it is mental, not external. The author begins by noting that the earlier doctrine held external composition, but later scholars (including al-Mawaqif) hold that essence and individuation are mentally distinct but externally one, using the analogy of genus and species. The author clarifies that predication of the universal (e.g., human) on individuals is valid because they share one essence, not because of separate existence. He then distinguishes between individuators (mushakhkhasat) of material entities and those of immaterial ones. The verification shows that individuation is predicated univocally on essence. An objection arises: if individuation is united with essence, then Zayd's individuation would be the same as 'Amr's, which is false. The answer is that union in external existence does not require conceptual identity or equivalence; one individuator can be united with many essences in a chain, thus preserving distinct individuals.

Key Terms
التركيب
المشخصات
الفصل
الموطأة
الاتحاد
المفهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Essence and individuation are separate entities externally just as they are mentally distinct.
Correction: They are united in external existence but distinct only in mental analysis; external separation would undermine predication.

Checkpoint
How do the later scholars explain the composition of essence and individuation?
A) They are separate in existence but combined mentally.
B) They are united in existence but mentally distinct.
C) They are both mentally and externally one.
D) They are separate both mentally and externally.

Key Takeaway
Essence and individuation are one in external reality but distinct in mental conception, allowing for valid predication without compromising individuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own identity, do you instinctively separate your 'essence' (what makes you human) from your 'individual characteristics'? How might this mental separation lead you to misunderstand yourself as a composite being, when in reality you are one unified person? Commit to reflecting on this before your next prayer.
?
The objection that unity of essence would collapse all individuals into one feels intuitively right. Have you ever made a similar mistake in judging others—assuming that because two people share a trait, they must be the same in some deeper sense? How can this lesson's answer (unity in existence does not require unity of concepts) change how you see the unique value of every person? Pause and think of someone you tend to stereotype: what would it mean to affirm their individuality while recognizing their shared humanity?
?
In your understanding of Allah's attributes, do you mentally distinguish His mercy from His justice while affirming they are one in His essence? How does this lesson's distinction between external unity and mental multiplicity apply to your theology? If you have ever struggled with the unity of divine attributes, how does the principle here help? Resolve to examine your assumptions about unity and multiplicity in your faith tonight.
