The Dispute Over Quiddity's Existence: Verbal or Substantive?
Al-Ījī states that quiddity exists externally only as individuals, making the dispute verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dispute over the external existence of universal natures is considered verbal but ultimately substantive.

Core Idea
The dispute is verbal because each side means a different kind of existence.

This short but dense passage from al-Ījī's Mawāqif clarifies a long-standing debate in Islamic philosophy: do universal natures exist externally? Al-Ījī states the position clearly: quiddity accepts commonality but not individuation; only individuals exist externally. The dispute, he argues, is merely verbal—but as the commentary shows, there is a real substantive difference underlying the words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Notice that the passage begins with a general statement about quiddity and ends with a discussion of the dispute.
The passage is from the Second Observatory, Eleventh Purpose.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
The passage mentions two groups: those who negate the existence of universal natures and those who affirm it. Identify what each group means by 'existence'. The deniers mean primary existence; the affirmers mean existence following the principle of abstraction.
The commentary emphasizes that both groups are using the term 'existence' differently.
3
Understand why the dispute is verbal but substantive
The passage says the dispute is 'only in expression' but the commentary adds that it becomes substantive (ma'nawī) because the intended meaning of the affirmers is the same as the deniers' meaning (i.e., not primary existence). So the disagreement is about the correct terminology, but there is no real conflict about the actual state of affairs.
This clarifies that the debate is not purely meaningless; it hinges on definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الحادي عشر : الماهية تقبل الشركة دون التعين وجود في الخارج إلا للأشخاص، والطبائع الكلية منتزعة منها، فلا نزاع إلا في العبارة. قوله : فلا) نزاع إلا في (العبارة فإن من نفى وجودها أراد وجودها بالأصالة، ومن أثبت وجودها أراد وجودها يتبع مبدأ انتزاعها هذا، لكن مراد القائلين بوجودها هو المعنى الأول فالنزاع معنوي.
The Second Observatory - The Eleventh Purpose: Quiddity accepts commonality without individuation. Existence in the external is only for individuals, and universal natures are abstracted from them, so there is no dispute except in expression. His saying: 'so there is no dispute except in expression' — for one who negates its existence intends its existence as primary, and one who affirms its existence intends its existence following the principle of its abstraction, but the meaning intended by those who affirm its existence is the first meaning, so the dispute is substantive.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines the position on quiddity (māhiyyah) and its existence. It states that quiddity accepts commonality (shirkah) but not individuation (ta'ayyun). Externally, only individual persons exist; universal natures are derived from them by abstraction. Therefore, the dispute over whether universal natures exist externally is merely verbal: those who deny them mean they do not exist independently, while those who affirm them mean they exist as mental concepts derived from individuals. The commentary clarifies that the latter group actually means the first meaning (i.e., existence as a concept), so the dispute is ultimately substantive (ma'nawī) but the terms are used differently.

Key Terms
ماهية (māhiyyah/quiddity)
شركة (shirkah/commonality)
تعين (ta'ayyun/individuation)
طبائع كلية (universal natures)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dispute is purely verbal without any real difference of meaning.
Correction: The commentary clarifies that the dispute is substantive (maʿnawī) because the two parties mean different things by 'existence'—one means primary existence, the other means existence as an abstraction. However, since the affirmers ultimately intend the deniers' meaning, the disagreement is about terminology rather than reality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the dispute over the existence of universal natures considered verbal?
A) Because both parties agree they exist externally
B) Because one party denies existence and the other affirms it using different meanings of 'existence'
C) Because universal natures do not exist at all
D) Because the disagreement is about words, not meanings

Key Takeaway
The existence of universal natures is not a real disagreement about facts but about the meaning of 'existence'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a previous disagreement with a fellow Muslim, did you assume the argument was merely verbal? How can you now check if both of you actually meant different things by the same key term, as the passage shows?
?
Do you sometimes treat abstract concepts like 'justice' or 'mercy' as if they exist on their own, separate from concrete actions? How does this lesson's point that quiddity exists only in individuals challenge you to ground your faith in specific, tangible deeds?
?
When you encounter a theological dispute, are you quick to label it as pointless? How will you now leverage the insight that clarifying meanings can turn a verbal dispute into a substantive discussion that moves closer to truth?

Refutation of the Objection on Universal Multiplicity
Presents an objection about universal nature being multiplied and gives the answer with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that universal human nature exists multiplied in individuals and why the answer relies on the concept of individuation (tashakhkhuṣ).

Core Idea
Each individual includes an additional individuator, so no individual is identical to the universal nature.

This passage tackles a classic objection in Islamic philosophy: if a universal nature (like humanity) is receptive to multiplicity, then when it is multiplied by an agent, each individual would be identical to that nature, leading to absurdities. The refutation introduces the crucial idea that individuation adds a further determination beyond the universal. Understanding this argument clarifies how universals and particulars relate in existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the objection: human nature is receptive to multiplicity, so when multiplied, each individual is identical to that nature, thus nature exists externally as multiplied.
Notice the conclusion that the problem of unity does not arise.
2
Identify the key move in the answer
The answer says each individual must include an additional matter of individuation (tashakhkhuṣ/ta'ayyun). Find this part in the text and understand why it refutes the objection.
The additional matter is not part of the universal nature.
3
Analyze the prior-posterior distinction
The commentary explains that multiplication of nature is prior to existence, so the problem only arises if existence were prior. Understand this logical ordering.
This addresses the objection about one person being in multiple places.
4
Review the identity clarification
The commentary clarifies that 'identity' means identity in existence, not in concept. This prevents the conclusion that individuals are identical to each other.
Compare with the genus-species analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يقال : من أن الطبيعة الإنسانية مثلاً قابلة في نفسها للتعدد والتكثر، فتحتاج إلى من يكثرها، فإذا تكثرت بتكثير الفاعل ووجدت تلك الكثرة في الخارج، كان كل واحد منها عين تلك الطبيعة، فتكون الطبيعة الإنسانية موجودة في الخارج على أنها متكثرة، لا على أنها متصفة بالوحدة حتى يلزم ذلك المحذور فجوابه : أن كل واحد تلك الكثرة لا بد أن يشتمل على أمر زائد هو تشخصه ،وتعينه، فليس شيء منها تلك الطبيعة . كيف ولو كان كذلك لكان كل واحد من تلك الكثرة عين منها، وهو باطل بديهة. قوله : (وأما ما يقال إلخ) حاصله أن تكثر الطبيعة النوعية مقدم بالذات على وجودها، والحصول في المكان والاتصاف بالأوصاف متأخر عن وجودها فلا يلزم المحذور، أعني كون الواحد بالشخص في أمكنة متعددة متصفاً بصفات متضادة، إنما يلزم ذلك لو كان وجودها مقدماً على تكثرها . قوله : (قابلة في نفسها أي مع قطع النظر عن وجودها وعدمها . (قول: بتكثير الفاعل) بضمه إليها الأمور التي يحصلها ويجعلها شخصاً، فتكون تلك الأمور داخلة فيها من حيث إنها متحصلة لا على أنها محصلة لأمر ثالث كما عرفت في الفصل بالقياس إلى الجنس وهي قوله : ( عين تلك الطبيعة الإنسانية المحصلة في الوجود. قوله : ( على أنها متكثرة ) أي بناء على أنها متكثرة لابناء على أنها واحدة . قوله) فليس شيء منها إلخ) قد عرفت أن القائل أراد بالعينية في الوجود لا في المفهوم، لا تنافي اشتمال الكثرة على أمر زائد ولا يلزم منها كون كل واحد من الكثرة عين الآخر كما أن كون الجنس عين النوع في الوجود لا ينافي اشتماله على الفصل، ولا يلزم كون كل واحد من الأنواع عين الآخر.
And as for what is said: that human nature, for example, is in itself receptive to multiplicity and manyness, so it needs something to multiply it; when it is multiplied by the agent's multiplication and that multiplicity exists externally, each one of them would be identical to that nature, so human nature would exist externally as multiplied, not as qualified by unity, so that problem would not arise. The answer: each one of that multiplicity must include an additional matter which is its individuation and specification, so none of them is that nature. How? If it were so, each one of that multiplicity would be identical to the other, which is obviously false. His saying: 'And as for what is said, etc.': its summary is that the multiplication of the specific nature is prior in essence to its existence, and coming to be in place and being qualified by attributes are posterior to its existence, so the problem—namely, one person being in multiple places qualified by opposite attributes—does not arise; it would only arise if its existence were prior to its multiplication. His saying: 'receptive in itself' means regardless of its existence and non-existence. His saying: 'by the multiplication of the agent': by attaching to it the matters that realize it and make it an individual, so those matters are included in it inasmuch as they are realized, not as being a third matter, as you learned in the chapter regarding the genus. And it is his saying: 'identical to that human nature realized in existence'. His saying: 'as multiplied' means based on its being multiplied, not based on its being one. His saying: 'so none of them is that nature, etc.': you have learned that the speaker meant identity in existence, not in concept; that does not contradict the multiplicity including an additional matter, nor does it entail that each one of the multiplicity is identical to the other, just as the genus being identical to the species in existence does not contradict its inclusion of the differentia, nor does it entail that each one of the species is identical to the other.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection claims that universal human nature is receptive to multiplicity, so when an agent multiplies it, each individual becomes identical to that nature, and thus nature exists externally as multiplied without the problem of one thing being in multiple places. The answer: each individual includes an additional element of individuation (tashakhkhuṣ/ta'ayyun), so no individual is identical to the universal nature. The commentary explains that the multiplication of the specific nature is prior to its existence, so the problem only occurs if existence were prior. It clarifies that 'identity' used here is identity in existence, not in concept. Thus, including an additional element does not make individuals identical to each other, just as genus is identical to species in existence but not in concept.

Key Terms
طبيعة إنسانية (human nature)
تعدد (multiplicity)
تشخص (individuation)
عينية (identity)
فصل (differentia)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if universal nature exists in each individual, then the individuals are identical in every respect.
Correction: The passage clarifies that individuals are identical only in terms of the universal nature (identity in existence), but each has an additional individuator, so they are not identical in all respects.

Checkpoint
What is the key element that prevents each individual from being identical to the universal nature?
A) The nature itself
B) The agent's multiplication
C) An additional matter of individuation
D) The fact that nature is abstract

Key Takeaway
The objection that universal nature is multiplied in individuals is refuted by introducing an individuating element that prevents identity between the universal and the particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your human nature is the same as everyone else's, what is that one thing (your 'individuator') that you will be held accountable for on the Day of Judgment? Have you ever considered that your distinct individuality is not just a decoration but a gift from Allah that carries responsibility?
?
The objection assumes that if the universal nature is multiplied, each individual is identical to that nature—but you are not just your human essence. What is the 'additional matter' in your life that makes you uniquely you, and are you using it to serve Allah or to imitate others?
?
If you truly believed that no two individuals are identical in their essence (because of the individuator), how would that change the way you judge others? Are you guilty of treating people as interchangeable, forgetting that each soul has its own distinct reality before Allah?

Al-Wujūdī and Al-ʿAdamī: Existential vs. Non-Existential
Defines al-wujūdī and al-ʿadamī, distinguishes from existent and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between al-wujūdī (existential) and al-mawjūd (existent), and why al-wujūdī does not entail actual existence.

Core Idea
Existentiality is about possibility, not actuality.

This passage from al-Ījī's Mawāqif introduces two crucial concepts: al-wujūdī (the existential) and al-ʿadamī (the non-existential). These terms are foundational for understanding the ontology of attributes and quiddities. The author distinguishes them from existence and non-existence, showing that a quality can be existential even if it never actually exists. This prepares us for later discussions on determination and mental considerations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the definition of al-wujūdī as a concept that may exist when inhering in an existent.
Notice the example of blackness (sawād) which is existential even if never actualized.
2
Identify the key distinction
Understand that al-wujūdī is more general than al-mawjūd from one aspect: it does not require actual existence. Contrast with mental considerations like genus which are only mental.
3
Grasp the logical argument
The passage argues that if al-ʿadamī were non-existence, then al-wujūdī would be existence, leading to a false exclusive dichotomy. Since there are infinite concepts beyond existence/non-existence, determination is not simply one of the two.
This refutes the idea that everything is either existence or non-existence.
4
Reflect on the relevance
Consider why this matters: it allows attributes to be considered real (existential) even if they never actualize, which is important for divine attributes and mental concepts.
It also shows that mental considerations (iʿtibārāt) are existential because they are not non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الحادي عشر : الماهية تقبل الشركة دون التعين. ما لا يستقل بنفسه، بل يقوم بغيره ويكون قيامه به بوجوده له في الخارج، (نحو السواد) القائم بالجسم فإن ثبوته له إنما هو بوجوده له ( لا أن يكون ذلك ) أي ثبوته لموصوفه ) باعتبار وجودهما في العقل واتصافه به فيه، كالجنسبة القائمة بالجسم؛ إذ ليست الجنسية موجودة في الخارج قائمة به فيه بل ثبوتها له واتصافه بها إنما هو في الذهن (وهو) أي الوجودي بالمعنى المذكور ( أعم من الموجود ) لا مطلقاً بل من وجه؛ لجواز وجودي لا يعرض له الوجود أبداً كالسواد المعدوم دائماً، فإن ملخص معنى الوجودي أنه مفهوم يصح أن يعرض له الوجود عند قيامه بموجود، فالسواد مثلا وجودي سواء وجد أو لم يوجد، وأما صدق الموجود بدون الوجودي ففي الموجودات القائمة بذواتها. وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن الوجودي مستلزماً للوجود، فلا يكون العدمي مستلزماً للعدم . ( ويقرب من هذا) الذي ذكرناه في تفسير الوجودي ( ما قيل : إنه ) أي الوجودي ( عرض من شأنه الوجود ) الخارجي، سواء وجد أو لم يوجد . ( وبالجملة : فلو كان العدمي هو العدم لكان الوجودي هو الوجود، فلا حصر )؛ إذ المفهومات المغايرة لمفهومي الوجود والعدم غير متناهية، فلا يلزم من أن لا يكون التعين وجوداً أن يكون عدماً (أو) كان الوجودي ( ما ليس بعدم، الأمور الاعتبارية وجودية ) ؛ إذ يصدق عليها أنها ليست نفس مفهوم العدم ولا) قائل به
The Second Observatory - The Eleventh Objective: The quiddity accepts sharing without determination. That which does not subsist by itself, but rather inheres in another and its inherence in it is by its existence for it externally, (such as blackness) inhering in the body, for its establishment for it is only by its existence for it, (not that that) i.e., its establishment for its attribute) by consideration of their existence in the mind and its being described by it therein, like the genus inhering in the body; for the genus does not exist externally inhering in it, rather its establishment for it and its being described by it is only in the mind. (And it) i.e., al-wujūdī in the mentioned sense (is more general than al-mawjūd) not absolutely but from one aspect; because an existential that does not have existence ever occur to it is possible, like blackness that is always non-existent. For the summary of the meaning of al-wujūdī is that it is a concept that may have existence occur to it when it inheres in an existent. So blackness, for example, is existential whether it exists or does not exist. As for the truth of al-mawjūd without al-wujūdī, that is in existents that subsist by themselves. And if that is the case, al-wujūdī does not entail existence, so al-ʿadamī does not entail non-existence. (And close to this) which we mentioned in the interpretation of al-wujūdī (is what was said: that it) i.e., al-wujūdī (is an accident that is apt for external existence), whether it exists or does not exist. (In short: if al-ʿadamī were non-existence, then al-wujūdī would be existence, so there would be no exclusivity); for concepts distinct from the two concepts of existence and non-existence are infinite, so it does not follow from determination not being existence that it is non-existence. (Or) al-wujūdī is (what is not non-existence, and the mental considerations are existential); for they are true of not being the very concept of non-existence, and no one holds (the other view).
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of two key terms: al-wujūdī (the existential) and al-ʿadamī (the non-existential). It begins by defining that which does not subsist by itself but inheres in another, such as blackness inhering in a body. The establishment of the attribute for the subject is by its existence for that subject externally. This is contrasted with mental considerations like genus, which exist only in the mind. The author then states that al-wujūdī in this sense is more general than al-mawjūd (the existent) from one aspect, because a concept like blackness is existential even if it never actually exists—what matters is that it is of a nature that can possibly exist when inhering in an existent. This means that al-wujūdī does not entail actual existence, and likewise al-ʿadamī does not entail non-existence. A similar interpretation is given: al-wujūdī is an accident apt for external existence. The passage concludes with a key argument: if al-ʿadamī were simply non-existence, then al-wujūdī would be existence, but that would imply an exclusive dichotomy, which is false because there are infinite concepts distinct from existence and non-existence. Therefore, it does not follow that if determination is not existence, it must be non-existence. Moreover, al-wujūdī can also be understood as 'what is not non-existence', which includes mental considerations as existential because they are not identical to the concept of non-existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'existential' (al-wujūdī) means the same as 'existent' (al-mawjūd).
Correction: The passage clarifies that al-wujūdī is broader: it is a concept that can possibly exist, whether it actually exists or not. Blackness is existential even if it never occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of al-wujūdī (the existential)?
A) That which actually exists in the external world.
B) A concept that may exist when it inheres in an existent, whether or not it actually exists.
C) Any attribute that is mental and not external.
D) The same as al-mawjūd (the existent).

Key Takeaway
Al-wujūdī denotes a concept that can exist, not one that actually exists, and it is broader than al-mawjūd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's attribute of mercy, do you treat it as merely a mental concept (al-ʿadamī) or as a real existential quality that can be actualized? How does this distinction affect your hope in His mercy?
?
Consider a sin you have committed. Do you view it as existent (موجود) or as existential (وجودي) but not necessarily permanent? How does this lesson's categories shape your understanding of repentance and the possibility of change?
?
You often judge things as either existent or non-existent. Where in your life do you mistakenly treat something as non-existent (al-ʿadamī) when it is actually existential—a possibility that could become real? Will you now begin to see those possibilities as real and act upon them?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Clarifying the Terms: Existential vs. Existent
Objection confusing 'existential' with 'existent' and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between existential (wujūdī) and existent (mawjūd), and why determination being non-existent does not imply it is a non-existence for something else.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not the same as being non-existent.

This lesson addresses a common confusion: equating the terms 'existential' (wujūdī) and 'existent' (mawjūd). The objection argues that if determination is not existent, it must be non-existent, but the answer clarifies that being non-existent does not entail being a non-existence for something else. Understanding this nuance is crucial for following the theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Notice how the objection conflates 'existential' with 'existent', assuming they are synonyms in this context.
The objection states: 'al-wujūdī and al-ʿadamī are also used in the sense of al-mawjūd and al-maʿdūm'.
2
Read the answer
The answer draws a distinction: being non-existent (maʿdūm) does not mean it is a non-existence (ʿadam) for something else; it could simply be non-existent in itself.
This is a key logical point: determination may be a non-existent attribute, not a privation.
3
Reflect on the difference
Consider why this distinction matters for the overall argument about determination. The theologians aim to show determination is non-existential, but here they defend against the objection that non-existential means non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : الوجودي والعدمي قد يطلقان بمعنى الموجود والمعدوم أيضاً، المناسب للمقام، وإذا لم يكن التعين موجوداً كان معدوماً قطعاً. وهو قلت : فحينئذ يجاب : بأن التعين إذا كان معدوماً لم يلزم أن يكون عدماً لشيء آخر، بل ربما كان شيئاً معدوماً في نفسه، وهو ظاهر. ( وأما المتكلمون فقالوا : التعين أمر عدمي لوجهين : الأول لو كان التعين وجودياً لتوقف انضمامه إلى الماهية على النقض الإجمالي؛ لما فيه من الإجمال وترك تفصيل معنى الوجودي الذي كان في المناقضة .
If you say: 'al-wujūdī and al-ʿadamī are also used in the sense of al-mawjūd and al-maʿdūm, and that is appropriate to the context, and if determination is not existent, then it is certainly non-existent.' Then I say: 'In that case, it is answered: that if determination is non-existent, it does not necessarily follow that it is a non-existence for something else; rather, it may be something non-existent in itself, and that is apparent.' (As for the theologians, they said: determination is a non-existential thing for two reasons: the first: if determination were existential, its joining to the quiddity would depend on the complete refutation, because of the vagueness in it and the omission of detailing the meaning of al-wujūdī that was in the rebuttal.)
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and an answer, followed by the theologians' position. The objection states that the terms al-wujūdī and al-ʿadamī are sometimes used synonymously with al-mawjūd (the existent) and al-maʿdūm (the non-existent), and that is the appropriate meaning in this context. If so, then if determination is not existent, it must be non-existent. The answer clarifies that even if determination is non-existent (maʿdūm), it does not entail that it is a non-existence (ʿadam) for something else; it could simply be non-existent in itself. This refutes the objection by distinguishing between being non-existent and being a non-existence. The passage then transitions to the theologians' view that determination is a non-existential (ʿadamī) thing, giving the first reason: if determination were existential, then its joining or attaching to the quiddity would depend on a complete refutation (al-naqḍ al-ijmālī) because of the vagueness and lack of detail in the meaning of al-wujūdī that was used in the earlier rebuttal. In other words, the theologians argue that existential determinations would require a detailed analysis that is absent, so it is better to consider determination non-existential.

Key Terms

العدمي
الموجود
المعدوم
التعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that if something is not existent, it must be a non-existence (privation) for something else.
Correction: The passage clarifies that something can be non-existent in itself without being a non-existence for another.

Checkpoint
What does the answer in this passage clarify about determination?
A) Determination must be existential because it is not non-existent.
B) Determination being non-existent means it is a non-existence for the quiddity.
C) Determination being non-existent does not imply it is a non-existence for something else.
D) Determination is both existent and non-existent simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Being non-existent does not entail being a non-existence for something else; determination may be a non-existent attribute in itself.
